Kafalah — Surety
advanced
15 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Liability in Conditional Kafala bi al-Nafs with Money
Intra-Hanafi dispute on surety liability with conditional money payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the surety's liability in conditional kafala bi al-nafs with money and apply the tarjih to determine the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
The surety is liable upon the plaintiff's mere claim, even if unspecified—custom validates later specification.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute about a specific kafala scenario: a person guarantees to bring a debtor to court, and failing that, to pay a specified sum. The core question is whether the surety becomes liable for the money when the debtor fails to appear, especially if the debt was not clearly specified at the time of the guarantee. The dispute involves the three primary Hanafi authorities—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani—and has practical implications for the burden of proof in suretyship cases.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (first view) hold regarding the surety's liability.
They say the surety is liable upon the plaintiff's claim, regardless of specification.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Muhammad al-Shaybani (and Abu Yusuf's second view) opposes. State his position.
He says no liability if the claim was not specified at the time.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What is Muhammad's dalil? He argues that without specification, the claim is invalid, so the surety is not liable.
Also, the surety did not acknowledge the debt, creating gharar.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How do Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rebut? They rely on custom (urf) of vague claims outside court and later specification.
The principle of tasih (validation) of a rational person's speech is used.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
What is the practical difference? The burden of proof: Muhammad requires specification or proof; the majority accepts mere claim.
The mufta bihi is the view that the surety is liable upon the plaintiff's claim.

بالمال لأنها مبنية عليها ولهما أنه يمكن تصحيحها لأن العادة جرت بالإبهام في الدعاوى في غير مجلس القضاء ثم يبينوها عنده دفعاً للحيل فصحت الدعوى والملازمة على احتمال البيان فإذا بين بعده انصرف إلى البيان والكفيل ينكر وجوبه على الأصيل فعلى الكفيل ألف درهم عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف في قوله الأول، وفي قوله الآخر وهو قول محمد لا شيء عليه ا هـ . وهذا يقتضي أن الحاصل أن أبا حنيفة وحده، ويستفاد بها أن الألف تجب على الكفيل بمجرد دعوى المكفول له وإن كان الكفيل ينكر وجوبه ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وقيد بكون المال على المكفول بالنفس لأنه لو كان على غيره ففيه اختلاف كما لو كفل بنفس رجل على أنه إن لم يواف به في يوم كذا فعليه ما للطالب على فلان آخر جاز ذلك استحساناً وهو قول محمد، وفي القياس لا يجوز ولو قول أبي يوسف؛ ذكره قاضيخان. وفي المحيط : جعل الخلاف على العكس وجعل أبا حنيفة مع أبي يوسف. وفي السراج الوهاج وكذا لو كفل بنفس رجل على أنه إن لم يواف به غداً كان كفيلاً بنفس رجل آخر كان على هذا الاختلاف ا هـ. ولا بد في مسألة الكتاب من إقرار المدعى عليه بالمائة ردياً مثلاً، ثم ظاهر كلامه أن مسألة الكتاب وفاقية والثاني خلافية وليس كذلك. قال في فتح القدير : صورتها في الجامع محمد عن يعقوب عن أبي حنيفة في رجل لزم رجلاً وادعى عليه مائة دينار فبينها أو لم يبينها أو لزمه ولم يدع مائة دينار فقال له رجل دعه فأنا كفيل بنفسه إلى غد، فإن لم أوافك به غداً فعلي مائة دينار فرضي بذلك فلم يواف به غداً قال : عليه المائة دينار في الوجهين جميعاً إذا ادعى ذلك صاحب الحق أنه له وهذا قول أبي يوسف. وقال محمد: إن ادعى ولم يبينها حتى كفل له بالمائة دينار أو ادعاها بعد ذلك لم ألتفت إلى دعواه وأراد بالوجهين ما إذا بينها أي ذكر أنها جيدة أو رديئة أو وسط أو نحو ذلك أو لم يذكر ؛ كذا قيل والأفود أن يراد بالوجهين ما إذا ادعى أي ذكر أنها مائة بينها أو لا وما إذا لم يدع شيئاً حتى كفل له ثم ادعى المقدار الذي سماه اهـ. وقال في النهر : وقد جمع في الجامع الصغير بينهما ولو تبعه المصنف لكان أولى. قوله : (ولا بد في الكتاب من إقرار المدعى عليه بالمائة (الخ يخالف هذا ما في شرح الجامع الصغير لقاضيخان حيث مسألة فأنا بالمائة لما في السراج الوهاج : لو ادعى على رجل ألفاً فأنكره فقال له رجل إن لم أوافك به غداً فهو علي فلم يواف به غداً لا يلزمه شيء لأن المكفول عنه لم يعترف بوجود المال ولا اعترف الكفيل بها أيضاً فصار هذا مالاً متعلقاً بخطر فلا يجوز ا هـ . ولم يذكر المصنف حكم ما إذا علق الكفيل بالمال براءته بموافاته غداً بأن قال كفلت لك مما عليه على أني إن وافيتك به غداً بريء من المال فوافاه به للاختلاف فيه فإن فيه روايتين في رواية يبرأ، وفي رواية لا، وهما مبنيان على تعليق براءة الكفيل بالشرط وستأتي في الكتاب والمسألة في السراج الوهاج كما ذكرناه قال : فإذا بين المدعي ذلك عند القاضي ينصرف بيانه إلى ابتداء الدعوى والملازمة فيظهر صحة الكفالة بالنفس والمال جميعاً ويكون القول قوله في هذا البيان لأنه يدعي صحة الكفالة كمن كفل لرجل في غيبته فلما حضر الغائب قال إنك أقررت لي بالكفالة في الحال التي كنت غائباً وقال الكفيل لا بل ذلك ابتداء كفالة في غيبتك ولم تصح فالقول فيه قول الغائب لأنه يدعي صحة الكفالة والكفيل الفساد .اهـ. ومثله في النهاية. وقال في معراج الدراية : ويكون القول له في هذا البيان لأنه يدعي الصحة والكفيل يدعي الفساد. وفي غاية البيان : ويقبل قول المدعي أنه أراد ذلك عند الدعوى الصحة وقد مر عن الفتح قريباً قوله ويستفاد بها أن الألف تجب على الكفيل بمجرد دعوى المكفول له و به صرح في متن التنوير تبعاً للدرر والغرر وهو المفهوم من قولهم لزمته إذا ادعاها المدعي ولم يقولوا وأثبتها بالبرهان وما في النهر من قوله فعليه المائة أي التي بينها المدعي إما بالبينة أو بإقرار المدعي مبني على ما في السراج وزاد البينة إذ لا فرق، وقد علمت مخالفته للشروح ولإطلاق المتون كالهداية والكنز والمجمع وغيرها ورأيت بخط شيخ مشايخنا الشيخ إبراهيم الغزي الذي تحرر لي أن هذا أي ما في السراج على قول محمد وعلى قول الثاني ثانياً يعلم هذا بمراجعة
So upon him is one hundred dinars, and in it there is a difference of opinion. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If he does not bring him with it, it is binding upon him if the plaintiff claims it. Muhammad said: It is not binding upon him, because when he did not specify it at the time of the claim, the claim is not valid, so his presence at the judge's council is not obligatory, so the suretyship of the person did not occur, so the suretyship of property is not valid because it is based upon it. For them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is possible to validate it because custom has proceeded with vagueness in claims outside the judge's council, then they specify them before him to avoid tricks, so the claim and the liability are valid on the possibility of specification. So if he specifies afterwards, it reverts to the first specification, and the suretyship of the person becomes apparent, so the suretyship of property is valid, on the basis that the surety knew the specific property claimed, to validate the speech of a rational person as much as possible. The conclusion is that we do not judge it as invalid at the time of its issuance; rather, the matter is suspended until the claim appears to that extent. So when it appears, it becomes apparent that he only sponsored for the amount claimed. And in al-Khulasa: He sponsored the person of a man on condition that if he does not bring him tomorrow, then upon him is one thousand dirhams, and he did not say 'which is upon him,' so the next day passed and he did not bring him, and the man says 'nothing is upon me,' and the claimant claims one thousand, and the surety denies its obligation upon the principal. So upon the surety is one thousand dirhams according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf in his first statement. In his other statement, which is the statement of Muhammad, nothing is upon him. This implies that the conclusion is that Abu Hanifa is alone [in this view]. And it is understood from it that the thousand is obligatory upon the surety merely by the claim of the guaranteed person, even if the surety denies its obligation. Similarly in Fath al-Qadir. And it is conditioned that the property is upon the guaranteed person, because if it were upon someone else, there is a difference of opinion, as if he sponsored the person of a man on condition that if he does not bring him on a certain day, then upon him is what the claimant has upon another person; that is permissible by istihsan, and it is the statement of Muhammad. By qiyas it is not permissible, and it is the statement of Abu Yusuf. Qadikhan mentioned it. And in al-Muhit, the difference is placed oppositely, making Abu Hanifa with Abu Yusuf. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: Similarly, if he sponsored the person of a man on condition that if he does not bring him tomorrow, he becomes a surety for the person of another man, it is upon this difference. And in the text's issue, the confession of the defendant to the hundred, for example, as bad, is necessary. Then apparent from his statement is that the text's issue is agreed upon, and the second is disputed, but it is not so. He said in Fath al-Qadir: Its form in al-Jami' from Muhammad from Ya'qub from Abu Hanifa concerning a man who owed a man and claimed a hundred dinars upon him, whether he specified them or did not specify them, or he owed him and did not claim a hundred dinars, so a man said to him: 'Leave him, I am a surety for his person until tomorrow, and if I do not bring him to you tomorrow, then upon me is a hundred dinars.' So he agreed to that, and he did not bring him tomorrow. He said: Upon him is the hundred dinars in both aspects, if the claimant claims that it is his due. This is the statement of Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: If he claimed and did not specify them until he sponsored for the hundred dinars, or he claimed them after that, I do not pay attention to his claim. And he meant by 'both aspects' the case when he specified them, i.e., mentioned that they are good, bad, medium, or similar, or did not mention. Similarly it is said, and the better is that by 'both aspects' is meant the case when he claimed, i.e., mentioned that they are a hundred, whether specified or not, and the case when he did not claim anything until he sponsored, then he claimed the amount he named. And in al-Nahr: He combined both in al-Jami' al-Saghir, and if the author had followed him, it would have been better. His statement: 'And in the text's issue, the confession of the defendant to the hundred is necessary' contradicts what is in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir by Qadikhan where the issue: 'I am for the hundred' based on what is in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If he claimed a thousand upon a man and he denied it, and another man said, 'If I do not bring him to you tomorrow, it is upon me,' and he did not bring him tomorrow, nothing is binding upon him, because the guaranteed person did not admit the existence of the property, nor did the surety admit it, so this becomes property attached to risk, so it is not permissible. And the author did not mention the ruling if the surety conditioned his release upon bringing him tomorrow, such as saying: 'I am a surety for what is upon him on condition that if I bring him to you tomorrow, I am free from the property,' and he brought him tomorrow; due to the difference about it, for there are two narrations: in one narration he is free, and in another not, and they are based on the suspension of the surety's release upon the condition, and it will come in the book. And the issue is in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj as we mentioned. He said: So if the claimant specifies that before the judge, his specification reverts to the beginning of the claim and the liability, so the validity of the suretyship of the person and the property both becomes apparent, and his statement is accepted in this specification because he claims validity, like one who sponsored a man in his absence; when the absent one came, he said: 'You acknowledged to me the suretyship at a time when I was absent,' and the surety said: 'No, that was an initial suretyship in your absence and it is not valid,' so the statement of the absent one is accepted because he claims validity and the surety claims invalidity. Similarly in al-Nihaya. And in Mi'raj al-Diraya: His statement is accepted in this specification because he claims validity and the surety claims invalidity. And in Ghayat al-Bayan: The statement of the claimant that he intended that at the time of the claim is accepted for validity. And it has passed from al-Fath recently: 'And it is understood from it that the thousand is obligatory upon the surety merely by the claim of the guaranteed person.' This is explicitly stated in the text of al-Tanwir, following al-Durar wa al-Ghurar, and it is the understanding from their statement: 'It is binding upon him if the plaintiff claims it,' and they did not say 'and proves it by proof.' And what is in al-Nahr from his statement: 'So upon him is the hundred that the claimant specified, either by evidence or by the claimant's confession,' is based on what is in al-Siraj and he added evidence, as there is no difference. And you have learned its contradiction with the commentaries and with the absolute texts like al-Hidayah, al-Kanz, al-Majma', and others. And I saw in the handwriting of our shaykh's shaykh, Shaykh Ibrahim al-Ghazzi, that this, i.e., what is in al-Siraj, is according to Muhammad's statement, and according to the second statement, it is known by reviewing.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about a specific scenario of kafala (suretyship). The scenario: a man guarantees to bring a debtor to court, and if he fails, he will pay a specified amount (e.g., 100 dinars). The dispute centers on whether the surety is liable for the money if the debtor does not appear, given that the debt was not clearly specified at the time of the suretyship. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) initially hold that the surety is liable if the plaintiff claims the debt, but Muhammad later argues that if the debt was not specified at the time of the claim, the claim itself is invalid, so the surety is not liable. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's first view is that the suretyship can be validated because custom allows vague claims initially, and specification later makes it valid. The passage also cites al-Khulasa, which adds that if the surety did not specify 'which is upon him,' the surety is liable according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's first view, but not according to Muhammad. The author then discusses the need for the debtor's acknowledgment (iqrar) for the surety to be liable, and there is further ikhtilaf about whether the plaintiff's mere claim suffices or proof is needed. The commentary concludes that the relied-upon position is that the surety's liability is established merely by the plaintiff's claim, as per the texts of al-Hidayah, al-Kanz, etc., and that requiring proof (baiyina) is contrary to the madhhab's established position.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafala): A contract of suretyship where a person (kafil) guarantees to bring a debtor (kafil bihi) or to pay a debt.
كفالة بالنفس (kafala bi al-nafs): A suretyship whereby the surety guarantees to produce the debtor's person at a specified time and place.
كفالة بالمال (kafala bi al-mal): A suretyship whereby the surety guarantees payment of a debt if the debtor fails to pay.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or confession, particularly of a debt, by the debtor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a surety says, 'If I do not bring him tomorrow, upon me is 1000 dirhams,' and the debtor denies the debt, what is the mufta bihi ruling in the Hanafi school?
A) The surety is not liable because the debtor denied.
B) The surety is liable only if the surety admits the debt.
C) The surety is liable upon the plaintiff's claim.
D) The surety is liable only if the plaintiff provides two witnesses.

Key Takeaway
In conditional kafala bi al-nafs with money, the Hanafi mufta bihi is that the surety is liable upon the plaintiff's claim, and no acknowledgment or proof is required.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Compulsion for Kafala bi al-Nafs in Hadd and Qisas
Intra-Hanafi dispute on compulsion for kafala and testimony for imprisonment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi intra-school dispute on compulsion for kafala bi al-nafs in hadd and qisas, explain the Sahibayn's basis for distinguishing qadhf and qisas from pure hadd, and apply the testimony threshold for imprisonment.

Core Idea
Compulsion for kafala bi al-nafs is prohibited in pure hadd but allowed in qadhf and qisas due to rights of people.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a key intra-Hanafi dispute: can a court compel a person to give surety for his person (kafala bi al-nafs) when facing a hadd or qisas claim? While Abu Hanifa prohibits compulsion in all such cases, the Sahibayn draw a line between pure hadd (rights of Allah) and qadhf/qisas (rights of people). The text also clarifies that compulsion does not mean imprisonment but rather accompaniment, and it sets a minimal standard of testimony for detaining a suspect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Dispute
Note that Abu Hanifa opposes compulsion (jabr) for kafala bi al-nafs in any hadd or qisas case, while the Sahibayn allow it in the hadd of qadhf and in qisas, but not in other hadd penalties. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf based on the nature of the right involved.
The term 'jabr' does not mean imprisonment; it means the plaintiff may accompany the defendant.
2
Understand the Distinction: Rights of Allah vs. Rights of People
Pure hadd penalties (e.g., for theft, adultery) are considered 'pure rights of Allah' (mahd haqq Allah). Qadhf and qisas involve rights of people (huquq al-ibad). The Sahibayn reason that where the claim involves a personal right, compulsion is valid—like in a debt claim—whereas in a pure divine right, it is not.
Al-Tamartashi included the hadd of theft under rights of people because it requires a claim, but the default view excludes it.
3
Examine the Evidence: Hadith and Rationale
Abu Hanifa relies on the Prophet's hadith 'La kafala fi hadd' as a general prohibition. The Sahibayn interpret this as applying only to pure hadd penalties, arguing that qadhf and qisas are not included because they are founded on a personal claim. The text also notes that kafala is for securing delivery, which is already wajib on the principal in personal rights cases.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), making its interpretation open to ijtihad.
4
Apply the Testimony Requirement for Imprisonment
Before a person can be imprisoned on suspicion of a hadd or qisas crime, testimony of two mastur (unexamined) witnesses or one just (adl) witness is required. This is because imprisonment is based on suspicion of corruption (tuhmat al-fasad), and such testimony is sufficient to establish suspicion.
Mastur witnesses are not known to be corrupt but have not been proven righteous; their testimony suffices for suspicion but not for conviction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا يجبر على الكفالة بالنفس في حد وقود وهذا عند الإمام، وقالا بالجبر في حد القذف والقصاص دون غيرهما. قيد بالجبر لأن أخذه برضاه بلا طلب في حد القذف والقصاص جائز اتفاقاً لهما أنهما شرعت لتسليم النفس وهو واجب على الأصيل فصحت به كما في دعوى المال بخلاف الحدود الخالصة لأنها محض حق الله تعالى. وله إطلاق قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لا كفالة في حد ولأنها للاستيثاق ومبناهما على الدرء. والحق التمرتاشي حد السرقة بهما في جواز التكفيل بنفس من عليه إجماعاً، وفي الاجبار عليها عندهما، وجعله من حقوق العباد لكون الدعوى فيه شرطاً بخلاف غيره لعدم اشتراطها. ولا يجب الحضور بسببها فإذا لم يكفل عنده يلازمه إلى قيام القاضي من مجلسه فإن برهن وإلا خلى سبيله، وليس تفسير الجبر عندهما الجبر بالحبس وإنما هو الأمر بالملازمة . قوله : (ولا يحبس فيهما حتى يشهد شاهدان مستوران أو (عدل) أي في الحدود والقود لأن الحبس لتهمة الفساد وشهادة المستورين أو الواحد العدل تكفي لإثباتها لأن خبر الواحد
His statement: 'And he is not compelled to give suretyship of the person in a hadd and qisas, and this is according to the Imam. The two companions said: There is compulsion in the hadd of qadhf and qisas, but not others.' It is qualified by compulsion because taking him with his consent without request in the hadd of qadhf and qisas is permissible by agreement, because they are legislated for the surrender of the person, and it is obligatory upon the principal, so it is valid as in the claim of property, unlike the pure hadd penalties because they are purely the right of Allah. And for him (the Imam) is the absolute saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'No kafala in a hadd.' And because it is for securing, and they are based on repelling. And al-Tamartashi considered the hadd of theft among them in the permissibility of taking suretyship of the person of one upon whom it is due by consensus, and in the compulsion upon it according to them, and he made it from the rights of people because the claim in it is a condition, unlike others because it is not conditioned. And attendance is not obligatory due to it; so if he does not give suretyship before him, he (the plaintiff) may accompany him until the judge rises from his session. If he provides proof, otherwise he lets him go. And the interpretation of compulsion according to them is not compulsion by imprisonment; rather, it is the command to accompany. His statement: 'And he is not imprisoned in them until two trustworthy witnesses or one just witness testify,' meaning in hadd and qisas, because imprisonment is for suspicion of corruption, and the testimony of two trustworthy witnesses or one just witness suffices to establish it, because the report of one...
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage discusses two related issues: (1) whether a person can be compelled to give suretyship of his person (kafala bi al-nafs) in cases of hadd (fixed penalties) and qisas (retaliation). According to Abu Hanifa, there is no compulsion; the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that compulsion is allowed in hadd of qadhf (false accusation of adultery) and qisas, but not other hadd penalties. The reason is that qadhf and qisas involve rights of people (huquq al-ibad), whereas pure hadd penalties are rights of Allah. The Prophet's hadith 'La kafala fi hadd' is cited. The text also clarifies that compulsion does not mean imprisonment but rather the plaintiff may accompany the defendant until the judge decides. (2) The requirement for imprisonment: before a person can be imprisoned on suspicion of a hadd or qisas crime, at least two upright witnesses (shahidan masturan) or one just ('adl) witness must testify. This is because imprisonment is based on suspicion of corruption, and such testimony is sufficient to establish the suspicion. The text ends abruptly; the author likely continues.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): A fixed punishment prescribed by Allah for specific crimes (e.g., theft, adultery, qadhf).
قود (qisas): Retaliation in kind for homicide or bodily harm, a right of the victim or heirs.
جبر (jabr): Compulsion or coercion, here meaning the court can compel a person to provide a surety for his person.
شاهدان مستوران (shahidan masturan): Two witnesses whose uprightness is not openly known but who are not known to be corrupt (mastur = concealed or not investigated).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), in which case is a person compelled to provide kafala bi al-nafs?
A) In all hadd penalties and qisas.
B) Only in pure hadd penalties like theft.
C) In the hadd of qadhf and in qisas.
D) Only in qisas, not in any hadd.

Key Takeaway
Compulsion for kafala bi al-nafs is limited to cases involving personal rights (qadhf and qisas) in the Sahibayn's view; the hadith 'La kafala fi hadd' is interpreted restrictively.

Kafalah — Surety

Suretyship for the Person in Hadd and Qisas: The Jabr Dispute
Expansion on compulsion in suretyship for criminal cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on compulsion (jabr) in suretyship for the person in hadd and qisas, and explain the rationale based on the distinction between divine and private rights.

Core Idea
Compulsion for suretyship depends on whether the penalty involves a private right – a Hanafi nuance.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks a classic intra-Hanafi dispute over whether a surety can be compelled to produce the principal in criminal cases. While Abu Hanifa holds that no compulsion applies in any hadd or qisas, his two disciples Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) distinguish cases that include a private right, such as qadhf (false accusation) and qisas (retaliation). Understanding this difference is crucial for knowing when a judge can enforce a surety contract.

Guided Path
1
State the main ruling and the dispute
Identify the core ruling: 'He is not compelled to give suretyship for the person in hadd and qisas.' Then note that this is according to Imam Abu Hanifa, while the Sahibayn hold compulsion in qadhf and qisas.
The passage specifies 'wa hadha 'inda al-Imam'.
2
Identify the opposing proof of the Sahibayn
The Sahibayn argue that qadhf and qisas involve a private (servant's) right because a claim is required, analogizing them to property claims where compulsion is permitted.
Property claims allow jabr because they are purely private rights.
3
Examine Abu Hanifa's dalil
Abu Hanifa relies on the hadith 'La kafalah fi hadd' and the principle that hadd penalties are pure divine rights, so no compulsion is allowed.
The hadith is general; the Sahibayn restrict it to purely divine hadd.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if a person kills another, according to Abu Hanifa the surety cannot be compelled to produce him; according to the Sahibayn, he can be compelled.
This affects whether the judge can enforce the surety contract.
5
Note the additional condition for imprisonment
The passage also rules that imprisonment in hadd and qisas requires testimony of two upright or one just witness – because imprisonment is ta'zir and can be based on suspicion.
This is separate from the jabr dispute but complements the procedural rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة الكفالة بالنفس في حد وقود ولا يحبس فيهما حتى يشهد شاهدان مستوران أو عدل وبالمال فأنا بالمائة لما في السراج الوهاج : لو ادعى على رجل ألفاً فأنكره فقال له رجل إن لم أوافك به غداً فهو علي فلم يواف به غداً لا يلزمه شيء لأن المكفول عنه لم يعترف بوجود المال ولا اعترف الكفيل بها أيضاً فصار هذا مالاً متعلقاً بخطر فلا يجوز ا هـ . ولم يذكر المصنف حكم ما إذا علق الكفيل بالمال براءته بموافاته غداً بأن قال كفلت لك مما عليه على أني إن وافيتك به غداً بريء من المال فوافاه به للاختلاف فيه فإن فيه روايتين في رواية يبرأ، وفي رواية لا، وهما مبنيان على تعليق براءة الكفيل بالشرط وستأتي في الكتاب والمسألة في السراج الوهاج كما ذكرناه قوله : (ولا يجبر على الكفالة بالنفس في حد وقود وهذا عند الإمام، وقالا بالجبر في حد القذف والقصاص دون غيرهما. قيد بالجبر لأن أخذه برضاه بلا طلب في حد القذف والقصاص جائز اتفاقاً لهما أنهما شرعت لتسليم النفس وهو واجب على الأصيل فصحت به كما في دعوى المال بخلاف الحدود الخالصة لأنها محض حق الله تعالى. وله إطلاق قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لا كفالة في حد ولأنها للاستيثاق ومبناهما على الدرء. والحق التمرتاشي حد السرقة بهما في جواز التكفيل بنفس من عليه إجماعاً، وفي الاجبار عليها عندهما، وجعله من حقوق العباد لكون الدعوى فيه شرطاً بخلاف غيره لعدم اشتراطها. ولا يجب الحضور بسببها فإذا لم يكفل عنده يلازمه إلى قيام القاضي من مجلسه فإن برهن وإلا خلى سبيله، وليس تفسير الجبر عندهما الجبر بالحبس وإنما هو الأمر بالملازمة .
قوله : (ولا يحبس فيهما حتى يشهد شاهدان مستوران أو (عدل) أي في الحدود والقود لأن الحبس لتهمة الفساد وشهادة المستورين أو الواحد العدل تكفي لإثباتها لأن خبر الواحد حجة في الديانات والمعاملات فتثبت شهادة العدل التهمة وإن لم تثبت أصل الحق. وظاهر كلامهم أنها لا تثبت بخبر مستور واحد والحبس بتهمة الفساد مشروع لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام حبس رجلاً بتهمة بخلاف دعوى الأموال حيث لا يحبس فيها قبل الثبوت لأنه نهاية عقوبتها فلا يثبت إلا بحجة كالحد نفسه، وكلامهم هنا يدل ظاهراً على أن القاضي يعزر المتهم وإن لم يثبت عليه وقد كتبت فيها رسالة وحاصلها أن ما كان من التعزير من حقوقه تعالى فإنه لا يتوقف على الدعوى ولا على الثبوت بل إذا أخبر القاضي عدل بما يقتضيه أحضره القاضي وعزره لتصريحهم هنا بحبس المتهم بشهادة مستورين أو واحد عدل والحبس تعزير، وصرحنا بجواز الهجم على بيت المفسد وجواز إخراجه من البيت وجواز نفيه عن البلد وتخليد حبسه إلى أن يتوب وأن من ذلك ما إذا سمع صوت غناء في بيته أو أخبر القاضي باجتماعهم على الشراب أو كان يؤذي الناس بيده ولسانه، وجواز التعزير بالقتل وجوازه بأخذ المال. ومعناه على ما في البزازية إمساكه عنه إلى أن يتوب. وفي السراج الوهاج : وأما التعزير فتجوز الكفالة به يعني أنه يجوز للقاضي الابتداء بطلب ذلك لأنه من حقوق العباد كالديون ا هـ . فظاهره أن ما كان من حقوقه تعالى لا يجوز به کالحدود
Book of Suretyship: The suretyship for the person in hadd and qisas, and he is not imprisoned in them until two upright witnesses or one just witness testify. And the discussion about the property is as in Siraj al-Wahhaj: if a man claimed a thousand [dirhams] and the debtor denied, and another man said, 'If I do not bring him to you tomorrow, it is upon me,' and he did not bring him, nothing becomes due because the principal debtor did not acknowledge the debt nor did the surety acknowledge it, so it becomes a debt attached to a risk, thus not permissible. The author did not mention the ruling if the surety makes his release conditional upon his appearance tomorrow, such as saying, 'I stand surety for you for what is upon him, on condition that if I bring him to you tomorrow I am free from the debt,' and he does bring him; there are two narrations: in one he is released, in the other not, based on the conditionality of the surety's release by the condition, as will come in the book. His statement: (And he is not compelled to give suretyship for the person in hadd and qisas) – this according to Imam [Abu Hanifa], while they [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said compulsion in the hadd of qadhf and qisas but not others. The compulsion is specified because taking him with his consent without demand is permissible by agreement in qadhf and qisas; they were legislated for delivering the person which is obligatory upon the principal, so it is valid as in a property claim, unlike exclusive hadd penalties because they are purely the right of Allah. He has the absolute statement of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'No suretyship in hadd.' And because it is for securing, and they are based on avoidance. Al-Tamirtashi considered the hadd of theft among them regarding the permissibility of surety for the person by consensus, and regarding compulsion according to them, and he made it among the rights of servants due to the condition of a claim, unlike others because they do not require it. Attendance is not obligatory due to it; if he does not give surety, he is made to accompany [the claimant] until the judge rises from his session; if he presents evidence [he is released], otherwise he is let go. Compulsion in their view is not compulsion by imprisonment but rather the order of accompaniment. His statement: (And he is not imprisoned in them until two upright witnesses or one just witness testify) – i.e., in hadd and qisas, because imprisonment is due to suspicion of corruption, and the testimony of upright witnesses or one just witness is sufficient for establishing it, because the report of one witness is a proof in religious matters and transactions, so the testimony of the just witness establishes suspicion even if it does not establish the original right. From the apparent of their statements, it is not established by a single concealed witness. Imprisonment due to suspicion of corruption is legislated because the Prophet (peace be upon him) imprisoned a man on suspicion, unlike property claims where one is not imprisoned before proof because it is the ultimate penalty, so it is not established except by proof like the hadd itself. Their statements here apparently indicate that the judge may punish (ta'zir) the accused even if it is not established against him, and I have written a treatise on it; the gist is that whatever is ta'zir from the rights of Allah does not depend on a claim nor on proof; rather, if a just person informs the judge of something that necessitates it, the judge brings him and punishes him, due to their explicit statements here about imprisoning the accused by testimony of two concealed or one just witness, and imprisonment is ta'zir. We stated explicitly the permissibility of raiding the house of a corrupt person, of removing him from the house, of banishing him from the city, of imprisoning him indefinitely until he repents, and that includes when one hears singing in his house, or the judge is informed of their gathering for drinking, or he harms people by hand and tongue, and the permissibility of ta'zir by killing and by taking property. Its meaning according to al-Bazzaziyya is holding him until he repents. In Siraj al-Wahhaj: 'As for ta'zir, suretyship for it is permissible,' meaning that the judge may initially demand it because it is from the rights of servants like debts. So its apparent is that what is from the rights of Allah is not permissible for it, like hadd.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the rulings of suretyship (kafalah) for the person in cases of hadd (fixed punishments) and qisas (retaliation). The author begins by presenting the main ruling that the surety is not compelled to produce the person in hadd and qisas according to Imam Abu Hanifa, but his two students Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that compulsion (jabr) applies specifically in the hadd of qadhf (false accusation of adultery) and in qisas, but not in other hadd penalties. The reason given is that qadhf and qisas involve a private right (haqq al-''ibad) since a claim from the victim or his heir is required, unlike purely divine rights such as zina, where no claim is needed. The commentary cites the hadith 'La kafalah fi hadd' (no suretyship in hadd) as a proof for Abu Hanifa's position. Then it addresses the condition for imprisonment in hadd and qisas: it is not permitted until two upright witnesses (musturan) or one just witness ('adl) testify to the ground for suspicion. The justification is that imprisonment is a discretionary punishment (ta'zir) based on suspicion of corruption, and the testimony of one just witness is sufficient to establish suspicion in religious and transactional matters. The passage then digresses into the broader rules of ta'zir, noting that the judge may administer ta'zir without formal proof, including actions like searching a house of corruption, banishment, and even killing in extreme cases, as long as it is for a right of Allah. The passage ends by applying this to suretyship: ta'zir that is a right of servants may have suretyship required, while ta'zir that is a pure divine right does not.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; a contract guaranteeing the presence of a person or payment of a debt.
جبر (jabr): Compulsion; the surety is compelled by the judge to produce the principal debtor.
حد (hadd): A fixed punishment prescribed by the Quran or Sunnah, e.g., for zina, theft.
قود (qisas): Retaliation, e.g., life for life in murder.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses not covered by hadd or qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), in which of the following cases is the surety for the person compelled (jabr)?
A) Hadd of zina
B) Hadd of qadhf
C) Hadd of theft
D) Hadd of drinking

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school differs internally on whether a surety can be compelled in criminal matters; the key is whether the punishment involves a private right or is purely divine.

Kafalah bil-Mal: Suretyship for Property Even When Amount Unknown
Ruling on the validity of suretyship for property with unknown amount.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that suretyship for property is valid even if the amount is unknown, and explain the evidence from consensus (ijma) and analogy (qiyas) with the right of pre-emption (darak).

Core Idea
Kafalah bil-mal is valid despite jahalah because it is built on expansion (tawassu'), not strictness like sale.

The Hanafi school is known for its pragmatic approach in contracts of guarantee. In this lesson, we explore a clear example: suretyship for a debt is valid even if the exact amount is unknown. The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states this ruling, and the commentary provides evidence from consensus and analogy with the surety for pre-emption (darak). Let's unpack the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The matn states: 'And (with property, even if unknown, when it is a valid debt).' Identify that the ruling is about the validity of kafalah bil-mal when the amount is unknown.
The underlying debt must be valid; if the debt itself is invalid, the suretyship is also invalid.
2
Trace the evidence: Ijma and Qiyas
The commentary cites consensus (ijma) on the validity of suretyship for darak (right of pre-emption), where the exact amount is unknown. This serves as a precedent via qiyas (analogy).
Darak is a form of suretyship for defects in a sale; its validity despite ignorance of the amount proves the principle.
3
Understand the principle of expansion
The reason (illah) is that kafalah is based on expansion (tawassu'), unlike sales which require certainty. This explains why jahalah is tolerated.
Other leniencies in kafalah include allowing the option (khiyar) for more than three days.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبالمال ولو مجهولاً إذا كان ديناً صحيحاً) أي تصح الكفالة بالمال ولو كان المال مجهولاً، وصحتها بالإجماع وصحت مع جهالة المال لبنائها على التوسع ولذا جاز شرط الخيار فيها أكثر من ثلاثة ويدل عليه إجماعهم على صحتها بالدرك أنه لا يعلم كم يستحق من المبيع كله من الهداية والفتح والخلاصة .اهـ. قوله : (بل إذا أخبر القاضي عدل الخ) قال في النهر : فإن قلت ينبغي أن يكون هذا على رأي المتقدمين من جواز قضاء القاضي بعلمه أما على رأي المتأخرين وهو المفتى به أنه لا يقضي بعلمه في زماننا فينبغي أن يتوقف على الثبوت قلت يجب أن يحمل الخلاف على ما كان من حقوق العباد، أما حقوق الله تعالى فيقضي فيها بعلمه، ويدل على ذلك ما في الخانية والظهيرية والخلاصة والبزازية : الرجل إذا كان يصوم ويصلي ويضر الناس باليد واللسان وذكر بما فيه لا يكون غيبة وإن أخبر السلطان بذلك ليزجره فلا إثم عليه .اهـ. قلت مخالف لما ذكروه. قال في شرح الوهبانية للشرنبلالي بعد كلام ما نصه والمختار الآن عدم حكمه بعلمه مطلقاً لفساد أحوال القضاة كما أنه لا يقضي بعلمه في الحدود الخالصة الله تعالى كحد الزنا والسرقة وشرب الخمر مطلقاً يعني سواء علمه بعد توليته أو قبلها غير أنه يعزر من به أثر السكر للتهمة اهـ. وممن نص على الاتفاق على عدم القضاء بعلمه في الحدود الخالصة ابن الهمام قبيل باب التحكيم، وذكره في شرح أدب القضاء ولم يحك فيه خلافاً، وعلله بأن كل واحد من المسلمين يساوي القاضي فيه وغير القاضي إذا علم لا يمكنه إقامة الحد فكذا هو ثم قال : إلا في السكران أو من به أمارة السكر ينبغي له أن يعزره للتهمة ولا يكون حداً .اهـ. فعلم أن التعزير ليس بقضاء ولذا لا يتوقف على الدعوى ونحوها
His statement: (And with property, even if unknown, when it is a valid debt) i.e., suretyship for property is valid even if the property is unknown. Its validity is by consensus, and it is valid with ignorance of the property because it is based on expansion; for this reason, the condition of option (khiyar) is allowed for more than three days. This is supported by their consensus on the validity of suretyship for the right of pre-emption (darak), even though it is not known how much is entitled from the entire sold item, as in al-Hidayah, al-Fath, and al-Khulasa. His statement: (Rather, if a just person informs the judge...) – al-Nahr says: If you say that this should be on the view of the early scholars regarding the permissibility of a judge adjudicating based on his own knowledge, but according to the later scholars, which is the fatwa, that he does not adjudicate based on his knowledge in our time, so it should depend on proof. I say: the disagreement should be restricted to what concerns the rights of servants; as for the rights of Allah, he adjudicates based on his knowledge. Evidence for this is in al-Khaniyya, al-Zahiriyya, al-Khulasa, and al-Bazzaziyya: A man who fasts and prays but harms people by hand and tongue, and one mentions his faults, it is not backbiting, and if one informs the ruler to restrain him, there is no sin. I say: this contradicts what they mentioned. In Sharh al-Whabaniyya by al-Shurunbulali: 'The chosen view now is the absolute non-adjudication based on his knowledge due to the corruption of judges, just as he does not adjudicate based on his knowledge in the pure rights of Allah such as the hadd of zina, theft, and drinking, whether his knowledge came after his appointment or before, except that he may punish (ta'zir) one with the effect of drunkenness due to suspicion.' Among those who explicitly state the agreement on not adjudicating based on knowledge in pure hadd penalties is Ibn al-Humam before the chapter on tahkim; he mentioned it in Sharh Adab al-Qada and did not report disagreement. He reasoned that every Muslim is equal to the judge in it; if a non-judge knows, he cannot establish the hadd, so the judge is the same. Then he said: 'Except for the drunkard or one with signs of drunkenness, he should punish him for suspicion, and it is not a hadd.' So it is understood that ta'zir is not adjudication, hence it does not depend on a claim and the like.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage covers two sub-topics. First, the ruling on suretyship for property (kafalah bil-mal): the author states that it is valid even if the amount is unknown, as long as the principal debt is valid. This is by consensus, and the justification is that kafalah is built on leniency and expansion, unlike sales which require certainty. The consensus on suretyship for the right of pre-emption (darak) is cited as precedent, where the exact amount is unknown. Second, the text shifts to a discussion about the judge acting on information from a just person. The commentary from al-Nahr explains that the early scholars allowed a judge to adjudicate based on his own knowledge, but the later preferred view (mufta bihi) is that he does not, especially in pure divine rights hadd penalties. However, ta'zir (discretionary punishment) is an exception: it is not considered qada (adjudication) and thus does not require formal proof or a claim. The passage thus connects to the earlier discussion on imprisonment on suspicion.

Key Terms
كفالة بالمال (kafalah bil-mal): Suretyship for a debt or property; the surety guarantees payment of a known or unknown amount.
جهالة (jahalah): Ignorance, uncertainty; here, the amount of the debt is unknown.
الدرك (al-darak): Right of pre-emption or concept of surety for defects in a sale; used as analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because it is based on strictness
B) Because it is based on expansion and leniency
C) Because the amount can be determined later
D) Because it is like a sale

Key Takeaway
Suretyship for property is valid even if the amount is unknown, by consensus and by analogy with the surety for pre-emption.

Kafalah — Surety

Judge's Knowledge in Hadd and Ta'zir: The Later Hanafi Position
Dispute on judge acting on personal knowledge, especially in hadd and ta'zir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the early and later Hanafi views on a judge adjudicating based on his personal knowledge, and identify the exception for ta'zir.

Core Idea
A judge may not use personal knowledge for hadd penalties, but may for ta'zir, which is not considered adjudication.

This passage addresses a nuanced fiqh issue: can a judge use his own knowledge to issue a verdict? The early Hanafi scholars permitted it, but the later preferred view prohibits it, especially for pure divine rights (hadd). However, ta'zir (discretionary punishment) is an exception, as it is not considered formal qada. This reflects the school's evolving methodology in light of practical corruption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The matn says 'if a just person informs the judge'; the commentary discusses whether the judge can act on that information.
The issue is about the judge's use of personal knowledge (knowledge obtained independently of the court process).
2
Contrast early and later views
Early scholars (mutaqaddimin) allowed adjudication based on personal knowledge; later scholars (muta'akhkhirin) forbid it in our time, especially in pure hadd penalties.
The later view is mufta bihi (relied upon for fatwa).
3
Apply the distinction: hadd vs ta'zir
For pure hadd penalties (zina, theft, drinking), the judge cannot use personal knowledge. For ta'zir (e.g., suspicion of drunkenness), he can, because ta'zir is not qada.
Ta'zir does not require formal proof or a claim.
4
Understand the rationale
The reason given is the corruption of judges (fasad al-qudat), and because every Muslim is equal in establishing hadd; a non-judge cannot enforce it, so the judge cannot either.
This is a qiyas: the judge is like any other Muslim regarding hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبالمال ولو مجهولاً إذا كان ديناً صحيحاً) أي تصح الكفالة بالمال ولو كان المال مجهولاً، وصحتها بالإجماع وصحت مع جهالة المال لبنائها على التوسع ولذا جاز شرط الخيار فيها أكثر من ثلاثة ويدل عليه إجماعهم على صحتها بالدرك أنه لا يعلم كم يستحق من المبيع كله من الهداية والفتح والخلاصة .اهـ. قوله : (بل إذا أخبر القاضي عدل الخ) قال في النهر : فإن قلت ينبغي أن يكون هذا على رأي المتقدمين من جواز قضاء القاضي بعلمه أما على رأي المتأخرين وهو المفتى به أنه لا يقضي بعلمه في زماننا فينبغي أن يتوقف على الثبوت قلت يجب أن يحمل الخلاف على ما كان من حقوق العباد، أما حقوق الله تعالى فيقضي فيها بعلمه، ويدل على ذلك ما في الخانية والظهيرية والخلاصة والبزازية : الرجل إذا كان يصوم ويصلي ويضر الناس باليد واللسان وذكر بما فيه لا يكون غيبة وإن أخبر السلطان بذلك ليزجره فلا إثم عليه .اهـ. قلت مخالف لما ذكروه. قال في شرح الوهبانية للشرنبلالي بعد كلام ما نصه والمختار الآن عدم حكمه بعلمه مطلقاً لفساد أحوال القضاة كما أنه لا يقضي بعلمه في الحدود الخالصة الله تعالى كحد الزنا والسرقة وشرب الخمر مطلقاً يعني سواء علمه بعد توليته أو قبلها غير أنه يعزر من به أثر السكر للتهمة اهـ. وممن نص على الاتفاق على عدم القضاء بعلمه في الحدود الخالصة ابن الهمام قبيل باب التحكيم، وذكره في شرح أدب القضاء ولم يحك فيه خلافاً، وعلله بأن كل واحد من المسلمين يساوي القاضي فيه وغير القاضي إذا علم لا يمكنه إقامة الحد فكذا هو ثم قال : إلا في السكران أو من به أمارة السكر ينبغي له أن يعزره للتهمة ولا يكون حداً .اهـ. فعلم أن التعزير ليس بقضاء ولذا لا يتوقف على الدعوى ونحوها
His statement: (And with property, even if unknown, when it is a valid debt) i.e., suretyship for property is valid even if the property is unknown. Its validity is by consensus, and it is valid with ignorance of the property because it is based on expansion; for this reason, the condition of option (khiyar) is allowed for more than three days. This is supported by their consensus on the validity of suretyship for the right of pre-emption (darak), even though it is not known how much is entitled from the entire sold item, as in al-Hidayah, al-Fath, and al-Khulasa. His statement: (Rather, if a just person informs the judge...) – al-Nahr says: If you say that this should be on the view of the early scholars regarding the permissibility of a judge adjudicating based on his own knowledge, but according to the later scholars, which is the fatwa, that he does not adjudicate based on his knowledge in our time, so it should depend on proof. I say: the disagreement should be restricted to what concerns the rights of servants; as for the rights of Allah, he adjudicates based on his knowledge. Evidence for this is in al-Khaniyya, al-Zahiriyya, al-Khulasa, and al-Bazzaziyya: A man who fasts and prays but harms people by hand and tongue, and one mentions his faults, it is not backbiting, and if one informs the ruler to restrain him, there is no sin. I say: this contradicts what they mentioned. In Sharh al-Whabaniyya by al-Shurunbulali: 'The chosen view now is the absolute non-adjudication based on his knowledge due to the corruption of judges, just as he does not adjudicate based on his knowledge in the pure rights of Allah such as the hadd of zina, theft, and drinking, whether his knowledge came after his appointment or before, except that he may punish (ta'zir) one with the effect of drunkenness due to suspicion.' Among those who explicitly state the agreement on not adjudicating based on knowledge in pure hadd penalties is Ibn al-Humam before the chapter on tahkim; he mentioned it in Sharh Adab al-Qada and did not report disagreement. He reasoned that every Muslim is equal to the judge in it; if a non-judge knows, he cannot establish the hadd, so the judge is the same. Then he said: 'Except for the drunkard or one with signs of drunkenness, he should punish him for suspicion, and it is not a hadd.' So it is understood that ta'zir is not adjudication, hence it does not depend on a claim and the like.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage covers two sub-topics. First, the ruling on suretyship for property (kafalah bil-mal): the author states that it is valid even if the amount is unknown, as long as the principal debt is valid. This is by consensus, and the justification is that kafalah is built on leniency and expansion, unlike sales which require certainty. The consensus on suretyship for the right of pre-emption (darak) is cited as precedent, where the exact amount is unknown. Second, the text shifts to a discussion about the judge acting on information from a just person. The commentary from al-Nahr explains that the early scholars allowed a judge to adjudicate based on his own knowledge, but the later preferred view (mufta bihi) is that he does not, especially in pure divine rights hadd penalties. However, ta'zir (discretionary punishment) is an exception: it is not considered qada (adjudication) and thus does not require formal proof or a claim. The passage thus connects to the earlier discussion on imprisonment on suspicion.

Key Terms
حدود خالصة (hudud khalisah): Pure divinely ordained penalties (hadd) such as zina, theft, and drinking.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment, not a fixed hadd; may be based on suspicion.
المتقدمون (al-mutaqaddimun): The early Hanafi scholars (e.g., Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and their immediate successors).
المتأخرون (al-muta'akhkhirun): The later Hanafi scholars from the 6th century onwards, who often revised earlier rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi view, can a judge use his personal knowledge to impose a hadd penalty for theft?
A) Yes, because he knows the truth
B) No, because he must have witnesses
C) Yes, but only if he is sure
D) No, but he can impose ta'zir instead

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi position prohibits a judge from using personal knowledge in pure hadd penalties, but allows ta'zir based on suspicion, as ta'zir is not formal adjudication.

Kafalah for Ta'zir: The Judge's Discretionary Authority
Explains permissibility of kafalah for ta'zir based on judicial discretion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the permissibility of kafalah for ta'zir and explain why the judge can act on testimony without full proof.

Core Idea
Kafalah is valid for ta'zir because it is a right of the servant, not a hadd.

In this passage from Bahr al-Raiq, we examine the intersection of kafalah (suretyship) and ta'zir (discretionary punishment). The author establishes that ta'zir, unlike hadd punishments, does not require strict proof; a judge may act on the report of a just witness. This opens the door for kafalah to secure ta'zir obligations, treating them like debts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Kafalah (suretyship) for ta'zir (discretionary punishment). The ruling is that it is permissible.
The passage explicitly states 'تجوز الكفالة به' (kafalah is permissible with it).
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
What proof is cited? The Prophet (peace be upon him) imprisoned a man on suspicion (لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام حبس رجلاً بتهمة). This shows that ta'zir can be based on suspicion, not full proof.
This is a Sunnah ahad, zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalalah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal link)
How does this prove the ruling? The Prophet's action demonstrates that suspicion suffices for ta'zir. Since kafalah is a guarantee for a ta'zir obligation, and ta'zir can be based on minimal evidence, kafalah is valid.
The Hanafi school uses this to argue that ta'zir is a right of the servant (haqq al-'abd), like debts.
4
Distinguish ta'zir from hadd
Why does this not apply to hadd punishments? Hadd punishments (e.g., for theft, adultery) require strict proof because they are huquq Allah (rights of Allah). Kafalah is not permissible for hadd.
The passage contrasts ta'zir with property claims where imprisonment is not allowed before proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة حجة في الديانات والمعاملات فتثبت شهادة العدل التهمة وإن لم تثبت أصل الحق. وظاهر كلامهم أنها لا تثبت بخبر مستور واحد والحبس بتهمة الفساد مشروع لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام حبس رجلاً بتهمة بخلاف دعوى الأموال حيث لا يحبس فيها قبل الثبوت لأنه نهاية عقوبتها فلا يثبت إلا بحجة كالحد نفسه، وكلامهم هنا يدل ظاهراً على أن القاضي يعزر المتهم وإن لم يثبت عليه وقد كتبت فيها رسالة وحاصلها أن ما كان من التعزير من حقوقه تعالى فإنه لا يتوقف على الدعوى ولا على الثبوت بل إذا أخبر القاضي عدل بما يقتضيه أحضره القاضي وعزره لتصريحهم هنا بحبس المتهم بشهادة مستورين أو واحد عدل والحبس تعزير، وصرحنا بجواز الهجم على بيت المفسد وجواز إخراجه من البيت وجواز نفيه عن البلد وتخليد حبسه إلى أن يتوب وأن من ذلك ما إذا سمع صوت غناء في بيته أو أخبر القاضي باجتماعهم على الشراب أو كان يؤذي الناس بيده ولسانه، وجواز التعزير بالقتل وجوازه بأخذ المال. ومعناه على ما في البزازية إمساكه عنه إلى أن يتوب. وفي السراج الوهاج : وأما التعزير فتجوز الكفالة به يعني أنه يجوز للقاضي الابتداء بطلب ذلك لأنه من حقوق العباد كالديون ا هـ . فظاهره أن ما كان من حقوقه تعالى لا يجوز به كالحدود
The Book of Kafalah: A proof in financial transactions and dealings, so the testimony of a just person is accepted despite potential bias even if it does not establish the original right. Their apparent position is that it is not established by the report of one concealed person. Imprisonment on suspicion of corruption is legitimate because the Prophet (peace be upon him) imprisoned a man on suspicion, unlike claims of property where imprisonment is not done before proof because it is the ultimate punishment, so it is only established by proof like the hadd itself. Their statement here apparently indicates that the judge punishes (yuzarrir) the accused even if it is not proven against him. A treatise was written on this, the gist of which is that what is from the discretionary punishments (ta'zir) from the rights of Allah does not depend on a claim nor on proof; rather if a just person informs the judge of what warrants it, the judge summons him and punishes him, due to their explicit statements here about imprisoning the accused on the testimony of two concealed persons or one just person, and imprisonment is ta'zir. We have explicitly stated the permissibility of raiding the house of a corrupt person, permissibility of removing him from the house, permissibility of exiling him from the town, and prolonging his imprisonment until he repents, and that includes cases where he hears singing in his house, or the judge is informed of their gathering for drinking, or he harms people with his hand and tongue, and permissibility of ta'zir by killing and by taking property. Its meaning according to Al-Bazzaziyyah is to detain him until he repents. In Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: As for ta'zir, kafalah is permissible with it, meaning it is permissible for the judge to initiate requesting it because it is from the rights of the servants like debts. So its apparent meaning is that what is from the rights of Allah is not permissible with it, like the hadd punishments.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the permissibility of kafalah (suretyship) in matters of ta'zir (discretionary punishments). The author begins by establishing the general principle that in financial transactions, the testimony of a just person is accepted even if there is potential bias, and it does not require establishing the original right. He then discusses imprisonment on suspicion, citing the prophetic precedent of detaining a man on suspicion, but distinguishing property claims where imprisonment is not allowed before proof. The key point is that ta'zir, being a right of Allah, does not require a formal lawsuit or complete proof; rather, the judge can act on the report of just witnesses. The passage details various forms of ta'zir including imprisonment, exile, and even taking property, and concludes by quoting Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that kafalah is permissible for ta'zir because it is a right of the servant (like debts), unlike hadd punishments which are rights of Allah. This establishes the Hanafi position that kafalah is valid for ta'zir obligations.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses that are not subject to fixed hadd penalties; it is a right of Allah but can be enforced with less stringent proof.
الكفالة (al-kafalah): A contract of guarantee by which a person (kafil) assumes liability for a debt or presence of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because ta'zir is a right of Allah and thus requires stricter proof
B) Because ta'zir is considered a right of the servant (haqq al-'abd), like debts, so it can be guaranteed
C) Because ta'zir is a form of hadd punishment
D) Because kafalah for ta'zir is not permitted

Key Takeaway
Kafalah is permissible for ta'zir because ta'zir is a right of the servant, allowing the judge to act on testimony without full proof.

Kafalah — Surety

6 min
Kafalah with Unknown Amount is Valid
States the ruling and justifies with consensus and leniency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kafalah bi al-mal when the amount is unknown, identify the proof (ijma' and qiyas based on leniency), and distinguish it from sale contracts.

Core Idea
Kafalah is built on leniency, so unknown amount does not invalidate it.

In Hanafi fiqh, guarantee contracts (kafalah) are treated with flexibility because their purpose is to secure rights, not to exchange property. This lesson examines a key ruling: a guarantee for a property debt is valid even if the exact amount is not specified. The ruling is established by scholarly consensus and the principle that kafalah is 'built on leniency', allowing terms that would be invalid in stricter contracts like sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
From the passage, extract the precise ruling: kafalah bi al-mal is valid (sahih) even if the property amount is unknown, provided the debt is a sound debt (dayn sahih).
The ruling is clearly stated in the commentary on the matn.
2
Examine the proofs
Two proofs are cited: (1) Ijma' (consensus) – 'valid by consensus', and (2) qiyas based on leniency – kafalah is built on tawassu', just like kafalah bi al-darak (guarantee of recourse in sale) is valid despite unknown amount.
The qiyas is implicit: the validity of kafalah bi al-darak by consensus shows that unknown amount does not invalidate.
3
Understand the leniency principle
The principle of leniency (tawassu') allows conditions in kafalah that are not allowed in sale, such as stipulating an option period longer than three days. This contrasts with sale contracts which require exact specification of price and goods.
This illustrates that different contracts have different levels of strictness.
4
Apply to a new scenario
Consider a case where a person guarantees the debt of another without knowing the exact amount. According to this ruling, the guarantee is valid. If the debtor later defaults, the surety must pay whatever the proven debt turns out to be.
The surety assumes the risk of the unknown amount, which is permissible due to leniency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبالمال ولو مجهولاً إذا كان ديناً صحيحاً) أي تصح الكفالة بالمال ولو كان المال مجهولاً، وصحتها بالإجماع وصحت مع جهالة المال لبنائها على التوسع ولذا جاز شرط الخيار فيها أكثر من ثلاثة ويدل عليه إجماعهم على صحتها بالدرك أنه لا يعلم كم يستحق من المبيع كله
His statement: 'And with property, even if unknown, provided it is a sound debt' means: Kafalah with property is valid even if the property is unknown. Its validity is by consensus, and it is valid despite ignorance of the property because it is built on leniency. Therefore, stipulating an option period of more than three days is permissible. This is supported by their consensus on the validity of kafalah by way of recourse (bi al-darak), because one does not know how much is owed from the entire sold item.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary on the matn 'Wa bil-mal wa law majhulan idha kana daynan sahihan'. The author explains that kafalah (guarantee) for a debt is valid even if the exact amount is not specified. The basis is ijma' (consensus) and the principle that kafalah is built on leniency (tawassu'), which allows flexibility. This leniency also permits stipulating a choice period (khiyar) of more than three days, unlike in sales. The proof cited is the consensus on the validity of kafalah bi al-darak (guarantee of recourse in sale), where the amount is unknown because it depends on the portion of the sold item that may be reclaimed. This ruling is specific to Hanafi fiqh and reflects the school's approach of facilitating transactions.

Key Terms
الكفالة بالمال (al-kafalah bi al-mal): A guarantee of a property debt, where the surety (kafil) assumes liability to pay a debt if the debtor fails.
التوسع (al-tawassu'): Leniency or flexibility in contracts, allowing conditions that would otherwise be invalid in stricter contracts like sale.
الكفالة بالدرك (al-kafalah bi al-darak): A guarantee of recourse in a sale, where the seller guarantees the buyer against any defect or claim that may diminish the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school allow kafalah with an unknown amount of property?
A) Because the amount does not matter at all in kafalah
B) Because kafalah is built on leniency (tawassu')
C) Because the surety can always ask the debtor later
D) Because it is the same as a sale contract

Key Takeaway
Kafalah bi al-mal is valid even if the amount is unknown because the contract is founded on leniency, not strict exchange.

Dispute on Judge's Use of Personal Knowledge in Rights of Allah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qada bi ilm in rights of Allah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the earlier and later Hanafi positions on a judge's use of personal knowledge (qada bi ilm) and apply the exception for ta'zir in rights of Allah.

Core Idea
A judge cannot rely on his own knowledge for hadd, but can for ta'zir — because ta'zir is not a judicial ruling.

This passage delves into an internal Hanafi dispute about whether a judge may act on his personal knowledge when the case involves rights of Allah (huquq Allah), specifically hudud and ta'zir. The earlier Hanafi masters allowed it broadly; later scholars, concerned about corruption, restricted it. The key resolution: ta'zir stands apart as a non-judicial measure, permissible on suspicion. Understanding this distinction is critical for applying the rules of kafalah and judicial evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The passage addresses whether a judge can use his personal knowledge to decide cases involving rights of Allah, especially hadd and ta'zir.
2
Map the dispute
The earlier Hanafi view allowed judging by knowledge; the later view (mufta bihi) prohibits it absolutely except for ta'zir.
3
Analyze the distinction between huquq Allah and huquq al-ibad
The author notes that the dispute applies to rights of servants; rights of Allah allow more flexibility according to some, but the later view unifies them.
4
Identify the exception for ta'zir
Ta'zir, particularly for intoxication based on suspicion, is permitted even under the later view because it is not a 'judgment' (qada).
5
Derive the practical consequence
In practice, a judge today cannot enforce hadd based on his own observation, but can impose ta'zir (e.g., for drunkenness) if he has suspicion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بل إذا أخبر القاضي عدل الخ) قال في النهر : فإن قلت ينبغي أن يكون هذا على رأي المتقدمين من جواز قضاء القاضي بعلمه أما على رأي المتأخرين وهو المفتى به أنه لا يقضي بعلمه في زماننا فينبغي أن يتوقف على الثبوت قلت يجب أن يحمل الخلاف على ما كان من حقوق العباد، أما حقوق الله تعالى فيقضي فيها بعلمه، ويدل على ذلك ما في الخانية والظهيرية والخلاصة والبزازية : الرجل إذا كان يصوم ويصلي ويضر الناس باليد واللسان وذكر بما فيه لا يكون غيبة وإن أخبر السلطان بذلك ليزجره فلا إثم عليه .اهـ. قلت مخالف لما ذكروه. قال في شرح الوهبانية للشرنبلالي بعد كلام ما نصه والمختار الآن عدم حكمه بعلمه مطلقاً لفساد أحوال القضاة كما أنه لا يقضي بعلمه في الحدود الخالصة الله تعالى كحد الزنا والسرقة وشرب الخمر مطلقاً يعني سواء علمه بعد توليته أو قبلها غير أنه يعزر من به أثر السكر للتهمة اهـ. وممن نص على الاتفاق على عدم القضاء بعلمه في الحدود الخالصة ابن الهمام قبيل باب التحكيم، وذكره في شرح أدب القضاء ولم يحك فيه خلافاً، وعلله بأن كل واحد من المسلمين يساوي القاضي فيه وغير القاضي إذا علم لا يمكنه إقامة الحد فكذا هو ثم قال : إلا في السكران أو من به أمارة السكر ينبغي له أن يعزره للتهمة ولا يكون حداً .اهـ. فعلم أن التعزير ليس بقضاء ولذا لا يتوقف على الدعوى ونحوها
His statement: 'Rather, if a just person informs the judge, etc.' Al-Nahr said: If you say this should be according to the view of the earlier scholars who permit a judge to judge by his knowledge, but according to the later scholars—which is the view given for fatwa—he does not judge by his knowledge in our time, so it should depend on proof. I say: The difference should be restricted to what is from the rights of servants; as for the rights of Allah, he judges by his knowledge. Evidence for this is in Al-Khaniyyah, Al-Zahiriyyah, Al-Khulasa, and Al-Bazzaziyyah: A man who prays and fasts but harms people with hand and tongue; mentioning him for what he does is not backbiting, and if he informs the authority to restrain him, there is no sin. I say: This contradicts what they mentioned. In Sharh al-Wahbaniyyah by al-Shurunbulali after some words, the text says: The chosen view now is that he does not judge by his knowledge absolutely, due to the corruption of judges, just as he does not judge by his knowledge in the pure hadd punishments of Allah like the hadd for zina, theft, and drinking wine absolutely—meaning whether he learned it after his appointment or before—except that he punishes (yu'azzir) someone with a trace of intoxication due to suspicion. And among those who explicitly stated the consensus on not judging by knowledge in pure hadd punishments is Ibn al-Humam before the chapter on arbitration, and he mentioned it in Sharh Adab al-Qada and did not report a difference, reasoning that every Muslim is equal to the judge in that regard; if a non-judge who knows of a hadd cannot enforce it, so too the judge. Then he said: Except for the intoxicated person or one with signs of drunkenness; he should punish him due to suspicion, and it is not a hadd. So it is understood that ta'zir is not a judgment, so it does not depend on a lawsuit or similar.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether a judge may act on his personal knowledge in matters of rights of Allah, particularly hadd punishments and ta'zir. The discussion starts with a possible objection: the earlier ruling (that a judge can punish based on testimony without proof) seems to contradict the later view that a judge cannot judge by his knowledge. The author (via Al-Nahr) resolves this by distinguishing between rights of servants (where knowledge is not used) and rights of Allah (where it can be used). However, other authorities like al-Shurunbulali and Ibn al-Humam state the chosen view is that the judge does not act on his knowledge absolutely, except for ta'zir of intoxication where there is suspicion. The conclusion is that ta'zir is not truly a judicial ruling (qada'), so it does not require a formal lawsuit. This nuance is important for the application of kafalah in ta'zir: since ta'zir is not a 'judgment' in the strict sense, the rules of proof are more relaxed.

Key Terms
القضاء بعلمه (al-qada bi 'ilmihi): A judge's ruling based on his personal knowledge of a case, without formal evidence presented in court.
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment that is not a hadd; it can be based on suspicion and is not considered a judicial ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi position (mufta bihi), in which case can a judge act on his personal knowledge?
A) In all hadd punishments
B) In rights of servants
C) In ta'zir of intoxication due to suspicion
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi view prohibits a judge from using personal knowledge for hadd but permits ta'zir on suspicion, due to a distinction between judicial rulings and discretionary punishment.

Defining Sound Debt for Kafalah: Dien Sahih
Defines and delimits 'sound debt' for kafalah with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which debts qualify as 'sound' (dien sahih) for valid kafalah and explain why debts extinguishable by ta'jiz are excluded, based on the Hanafi definition.

Core Idea
A sound debt is only extinguished by payment or discharge; only such stable debts are valid for kafalah.

In Hanafi fiqh, not every debt can be guaranteed through kafalah. The contract of suretyship is only valid for a 'sound debt' (dien sahih) – one that is stable and not subject to automatic termination. This lesson unpacks the definition from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, showing which debts qualify and which do not, using examples like salam, arsh, and badal al-kitabah.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the Definition
Understand that a sound debt (dien sahih) is defined as a debt that can only be extinguished by payment (ada') or discharge (ibra'). This is the core criterion.
Any debt that can be extinguished by other means, like ta'jiz (declaring inability), is not a dien sahih.
2
Apply to Examples
Classify each example given: salam debt (included), arsh shijaj and qat' al-taraf (included, as they are stable debts), badal al-kitabah (excluded because it can be extinguished by ta'jiz), and diyah (excluded by Hanafi consensus).
The contrast between arsh and diyah is noted: arsh is stable, diyah is not considered a sound debt.
3
Identify the Rationale
The key distinction is whether the debt is 'sound' (thabit) and not liable to become void due to the debtor's incapacity (ta'jiz). Badal kitabah falls when the mukatib cannot pay, so it lacks stability.
This rationale also explains why kafalah for diyah is invalid – it shares a similar instability.
4
Note Intra-Hanafi Differences
Be aware that within the Hanafi school, there is a difference regarding badal al-sa'ayah (compensation for unlawful intercourse): Abu Hanifa holds it invalid for kafalah, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it valid.
The mufta bihi position on badal al-kitabah and diyah is clear invalidity.
5
Test with New Cases
Apply the definition to other potential debts, such as a debt of qard (loan) or a price deferred in a sale (thaman). These are generally sound debts and valid for kafalah.
Always check if the debt can be extinguished by any means other than payment or forgiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والدين الصحيح ما لا يسقط إلا بالأداء أو الإبراء دخل فيه المسلم فيه ففي فتاوى الحانوتي : الكفالة بالمسلم فيه صحيحة لأنه لا مبيع، وممن نقل صحته الوالد عن شرح التكملة والتصريح داخلاً في قولهم تصح الكفالة بالدين .اهـ. قوله : (مع أنه لا يسقط إذ هو لا دین بالغ عزيز وإن كان هو يقبل التعجيز) قال في النهر وكأنه ألحق ببدل الكتابة قوله : بخلاف) أرش الشجة وقطع الطرف : الخ) قال الرملي والكفالة بالدية لا تصح كما في الخلاصة والبزازية وفي الظهيرية واعلم الكفالة ببدل الكتابة والدية لا تجوز .اهـ.
His statement: 'A sound debt is that which is not extinguished except by payment or discharge. Included in it is the salam debt. In Fatawa al-Hanuti: Kafalah for a salam debt is valid because it is not a sale. Among those who transmitted its validity is al-Walid from Sharh al-Takmila, and it is explicitly included in their statement: 'Kafalah is valid for a debt.' His statement: 'Even though it is not extinguished, since it is not a debt that is waived by inability, although it accepts frustration'—Al-Nahr said: It seems he likened it to badal al-kitabah. His statement: 'Unlike the compensation for a head wound and amputation of a limb'—Al-Ramli said: Kafalah for blood money (diyah) is not valid, as in Al-Khulasa and Al-Bazzaziyya, and in Al-Zahiriyya: Know that kafalah for badal al-kitabah and blood money is not permissible.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling regarding the type of debt (dien) for which kafalah is valid. The matn defines 'dien sahih' as a debt that is only extinguished by payment (ada') or discharge (ibra'). This excludes debts like badal al-kitabah (the redemption price of a mukatib slave) which can be extinguished by ta'jiz (declaring the slave unable to pay) and thus is not a sound debt. The commentary explains that a salam debt (advance payment for future delivery) is included because it is not a sale of a specific item but a debt. The text then contrasts with arsh shijaj (compensation for wounds) and qat' al-taraf (amputation), which are sound debts, so kafalah is valid. However, blood money (diyah) is not, as stated in multiple sources. The passage clarifies the boundaries of what debts can be guaranteed, ensuring that only debts that are stable and not subject to automatic extinction are valid for kafalah.

Key Terms
الدين الصحيح (al-dien al-sahih): A debt that cannot be extinguished except by payment or discharge, not by other means like ta'jiz.
بدل الكتابة (badal al-kitabah): The amount a mukatib slave agrees to pay for his freedom; it can be extinguished by ta'jiz (declaring inability).
التعجيز (al-ta'jiz): A declaration by the mukatib slave that he is unable to pay the kitabah, which extinguishes the debt automatically.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a valid subject for kafalah according to this passage?
A) Salam debt
B) Arsh shijaj (compensation for head wound)
C) Badal al-kitabah (redemption of a mukatib)
D) Qat' al-taraf (compensation for amputation)

Key Takeaway
A sound debt (dien sahih) is one that is only extinguished by payment or discharge; only such debts are valid subjects for kafalah in the Hanafi school.

Kafalah with Option: The Surety's Choice Between Two Persons
Ruling on kafalah with ambiguity as to the principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of kafalah with ambiguity between two principals and identify who holds the right to choose.

Core Idea
In kafalah with two possible principals, the surety chooses, not the creditor.

The following passage clarifies the Hanafi position on a specific form of kafalah where the surety guarantees the debt of one of two persons. The key issue is who has the right to designate the principal. The relied-upon view is that the surety has the option, based on the principle of leniency in suretyship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous kafalah scenario
Recognize that the surety's statement 'I guarantee either Zaid's debt or 'Amr's debt' creates uncertainty about the principal.
This is not a conditional kafalah; it is vague from the start.
2
Recognize the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Note that Al-Bada'i' assigned the option to the creditor, but Al-Fath and Kafi al-Hakim assign it to the surety.
The difference hinges on whether the vagueness is resolved by the surety or the creditor.
3
State the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the surety has the right to choose which principal he will guarantee.
This is the view adopted in Al-Fath and Kafi al-Hakim.
4
Understand the basis
Kafalah is based on leniency, so the surety is given the benefit of the doubt and the right to choose.
If the creditor had the choice, it would impose a burden on the surety.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والتعيين للمكفول (له) مخالف لما قبله عن البدائع حيث جعل الخيار للكفيل في نظير هذه المسألة وبه صرح في الفتح في هذه المسألة ونصه : ولو قال رجل كفلت بمالك على فلان أو مالك على فلان رجل آخر لأنها جهالة المكفول عنه في غير تعليق ويكون الخيار للكفيل اهـ. وفي كافي الحاكم : لو قال أنا كفيل بفلان أو فلان كان حائزاً يدفع أيهما شاء الكفيل اهـ.
His statement: 'And the designation is for the one for whom the guarantee is made (the creditor)' contradicts what was before from Al-Bada'i' where the option is given to the surety in a similar case. This is explicitly stated in Al-Fath in this case, and its text: 'If a man says, I guarantee for you your debt owed by Zaid, or your debt owed by another man, because it is ignorance of the principal in an unconditional form, the option is for the surety (kafil).' In Kafi al-Hakim: 'If he says, I am a surety for Zaid or for Amr, it is valid, and he hands over whichever the surety wishes.'
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on a specific form of kafalah where the surety guarantees the debt or presence of one of two individuals. The mubtadi' might think the creditor has the right to choose, but the correct Hanafi position is that the option belongs to the surety (kafil). Al-Fath and Kafi al-Hakim both affirm that the surety may choose which of the two he will be responsible for. This is because the contract is vague regarding the principal, and since kafalah is based on leniency, the surety is given the choice. The passage highlights a point of intra-Hanafi difference: Al-Bada'i' seems to give the option to the creditor, but the relied-upon position (as per Al-Fath) gives it to the surety. This ruling applies when the kafalah is not conditioned on a specific person.

Key Terms
التعيين (al-ta'yin): The designation of who is being guaranteed; here it refers to the right to choose between two principals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The creditor
B) The surety
C) Both must agree
D) The option is invalid

Key Takeaway
In ambiguous kafalah, the surety has the right to choose which principal he guarantees.
