Kafala of a Muslim for a Dhimmi's Wine: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafala of wine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi opinions on the validity of kafala for wine and apply the qiyas used by the second opinion.

Core Idea
Kafala for wine may be valid by analogy to transporting wine, if the wine is a specific object.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents an internal Hanafi disagreement on whether a Muslim can guarantee wine owed by a dhimmi to another dhimmi. While one view declares it absolutely invalid, another permits it under a specific condition, drawing on Abu Hanifa's reasoning on the permissibility of transporting wine. Understanding this dispute clarifies the scope of kafala and the application of qiyas in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
A Muslim acts as guarantor (kafil) for a dhimmi's debt of wine owed to another dhimmi. Determine the subject matter (khamr) and the parties.
The wine is the object of the guarantee.
2
State the two opinions
First opinion: kafala is absolutely invalid (la yasih mutlaqan). Second opinion: it is valid if the wine is a specific 'ayn (specified property) in the possession of the principal debtor.
The condition 'specific wine' is crucial.
3
Analyze the qiyas
The second opinion uses qiyas jaliy: analogizing kafala of wine to the permissibility of hiring oneself to transport wine, as ruled by Abu Hanifa.
Identify the asl (original case) and far' (new case).
4
Identify the effective cause (illa)
The illa is that in both cases, the Muslim is not consuming or owning the wine but merely facilitating its transfer; there is no direct involvement in prohibited consumption.
This illa justifies the ruling of validity.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under the first opinion, the kafala contract is void and unenforceable; under the second, it is valid and binding, provided the wine is a specific 'ayn.
This affects the rights and obligations of the parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصولين : كفل مسلم عن ذمي بخمر لذمي قيل لا يصح مطلقاً، وقيل لو كنت الخمرة بعينها عند المطلوب يصح على قياس قول أبي حنيفة إذ يجوز عنده للمسلم أن يلزمه نقل الخمر كما لو أجر نفسه لنقلها ا.هـ.
Two chapters: A Muslim guaranteed for a dhimmi wine for a dhimmi. It is said it is absolutely invalid. And it is said if the specific wine is with the required party, it is valid by analogy to the saying of Abu Hanifa, for it is permissible according to him for a Muslim to oblige himself to transport wine, just as if he hired himself to transport it. End.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a Muslim guaranteeing for a dhimmi a debt of wine owed to another dhimmi. Two views are reported: the first states absolute invalidity, the second conditions validity on the wine being a specific 'ayn (not a debt) in the possession of the principal debtor, by qiyas to Abu Hanifa's permissibility of a Muslim obliging himself to transport wine (e.g., as a hired carrier). The qiyas is based on the idea that the object (khamr) is not being consumed or traded by the Muslim, but merely transferred. The text does not specify which view is mufta bihi.

Key Terms
كفل (kafala): Guarantee or suretyship; obligating oneself to fulfill an obligation of another.
خمر (khamr): Wine, an intoxicating beverage, legally impure.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; extending a ruling from an original case to a new case based on common effective cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which condition is necessary for the validity of kafala of wine according to the second opinion?
A) The wine must be a debt (dayn).
B) The wine must be a specific 'ayn (عين) in the possession of the principal debtor.
C) The guarantor must be a dhimmi.
D) The wine must be consumed by the guarantor.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim's kafala for wine is not always invalid; it may be valid by qiyas if the wine is a specific 'ayn.

Kafala for a Ruler's Confiscation Demand
Ruling on a guarantor's liability for a ruler's claim on a person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a person guarantees payment demanded by a ruler through musadara and explain the proof using the principle that sensory claim is like legal claim.

Core Idea
In kafala, a physical taking (musadara) creates a valid obligation, even if the ruler had no legal right.

This passage extends the rules of kafala to an oppressive situation: a ruler confiscates property and demands money. A guarantor pays and then tries to reclaim, arguing the ruler had no claim. But Shams al-Islam and al-Bazzaziyya rule that the judge can claim from the guarantor because the sensory claim (actual taking) is equivalent to a legal claim. This lesson unpacks that rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the ruling
Identify the scenario: a ruler (wali) confiscates (musadara) and demands money; a person becomes guarantor and pays; then the guarantor claims no liability because the ruler had no claim. The ruling from Shams al-Islam: the judge can claim from the guarantor.
2
Locate the proof
The proof is a rational analogy (qiyas): the sensory claim (mutalaba hissiyya) is likened to a legal claim (mutalaba shar'iyya). This is cited from Shams al-Islam and al-Bazzaziyya.
3
Understand the analogy
Just as a legal claim creates an obligation to pay, the actual taking by force creates a de facto obligation. The basis of the analogy is the effect: both result in a financial demand that must be fulfilled.
4
Note the implication
The guarantor cannot recover the paid amount based on the ruler's injustice; the obligation is effective despite the illegitimacy of the original demand.
This ruling is from fatawa mutakhkhirin, not the core zahir al-riwayah texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودخل فيه ما لو صادر الوالي رجلاً وطلب منه مالاً وضمن رجل ذلك وبذل الخط ثم قال الضامن ليس لك علي شيء لأنه ليس للوالي عليه شيء. قال في شمس الإسلام والقاضي يملك المطالبة لأن المطالبة الحسية كالمطالبة الشرعية؛ كذا البزازية .
Included in it is if the governor confiscated from a man and demanded money from him, and a man guaranteed that and paid the sum, then the guarantor said: 'You have no claim on me because the governor has no claim on him.' He said in Shams al-Islam: The judge has the right to claim because the sensory claim is like the legal claim; likewise in al-Bazzaziyya.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage extends the previous ruling to a case where a governor (wali) confiscates property from a person, and another person guarantees the demanded amount and pays it. Later, the guarantor argues that since the governor had no legal right to that money, the recipient (the governor or the claimant) has no claim against the guarantor. The text rebuts this by citing Shams al-Islam: the judge (qadi) can demand payment because the physical act of taking (al-mutalaba al-hissiyya) is equivalent to a legal claim (al-mutalaba al-shar'iyya). This means that once the guarantor has paid, he cannot reclaim it on the basis that the original demand was unjust. The proof is rational: the act of taking creates a de facto obligation.

Key Terms
مصادرة (musadara): Confiscation of property by a ruler or authority.
مطالبة حسية (mutalaba hissiyya): Sensory or physical claim, i.e., actual taking of property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The ruler has a legal right to the money.
B) The guarantor volunteered.
C) The sensory claim is like a legal claim.
D) The principal debtor admitted the debt.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi kafala, a physical claim (musadara) by a ruler creates a valid obligation on the guarantor, equivalent to a legal claim.

Wording of Kafalah: ʿIndī and ʿAlā and the Role of Custom
Explanation of phrases creating kafalah and the effect of conditionality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the word 'ʿindī' creates a binding kafalah when used in the context of a debt, and distinguish this from the unconditional effect of 'ʿalā'.

Core Idea
ʿIndī with debt context creates a guarantee; custom decides, not the word alone.

In Hanafi law, the wording of a kafalah contract determines its validity. While 'ʿalā' unambiguously indicates a debt obligation, 'ʿindī' is ambiguous and its legal effect depends on context and custom. This lesson examines the dispute among Hanafi scholars regarding whether 'ʿindī' creates a binding guarantee unconditionally or only when accompanied by contextual clues, and how the later school settled on the reliance on custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrases
Recognize that the words 'ʿindī' (عندي) and 'ʿalā' (على) are used in kafalah. 'ʿalā' clearly indicates a debt obligation; 'ʿindī' is ambiguous, possibly meaning deposit (wadīʿah) or guarantee depending on context.
The phrase 'ilayya' (إلي) is also mentioned as a word of kafalah.
2
Understand the default meaning
In absolute terms, 'ʿindī' signifies possession or deposit, not a debt. However, when associated with a debt (e.g., 'your debt is with me'), custom can shift its meaning to a guarantee.
The default is important for determining when a guarantee is not created.
3
Analyze the role of custom
Hanafi scholars like Qadikhan and al-Zaylaʿī argue that custom ('urf) overrides the literal meaning. If 'ʿindī' is used in a debt context, it indicates obligation (wujūb), making the speaker a guarantor.
Conditionality does not negate this; the key is the presence of a debt context.
4
Apply the rule to a scenario
If a creditor demands his debt and the debtor says 'Do not demand, your property is with me (ʿindī),' this creates a valid kafalah because the context of debt triggers the guarantee interpretation.
This is the preferred view in the Hanafi school, as supported by the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقدمنا كلامه الرملي: وكذلك لو غصب فرساً... وأنت على علم بأن يصح ضمان العين المغصوبة كما هو صريح في شرائط المكفول به وسيأتي في المتن أيضاً صريحاً والله تعالى أعلم. قوله: (لو أتى بهذه الألفاظ منجزاً) قال الرملي: أي أنا أدفعه أنا أسلمه أنا أقبضه. قوله: (وعلى هذا وقعت حادثة الخ) قال الرملي: كيف هذا أن قاضيخان علل المسألة بقوله لأن «عند» إذا استعمل في الدين يراد به الوجوب وهو يقتضي عدم الفرق بين التعليق وغيره أن النظر إلى القرينة الدالة تأمل. قوله: (وقدمنا عن الخانية في المعلقة الخ قال في النهر وأقول: صرح في الخانية أن «عند تفيد اللزوم إذا أضيفت إلى الدين غير مقيد بالتعليق فإذا طالبه بدينه فقال له لا تطالب مالك عندي كان كفيلاً؛ هذا هو الظاهر فتدبره .اهـ. وكتب عليه الرملي ما نصه أقول قال الغزي وأقول أيضاً: المنقول في التتارخانية في الفصل الثاني من كتاب الكفالة أن من ألفاظ الكفالة إلي وعندي ثم قال: وإن مطلقه لفظ يني عندي للوديعة لكنه بقرينة الدين يكون كفالة .اهـ. ما نقله الغزي. أقول: وهو يقتضي عدم الفرق كتليل قاضيخان وأقول: ذكر الزيلعي في شرح قوله في الإقرار عندي معي الخ أن مطلقه. يعني الكلام يحمل على العرف وفي العرف عندي إذا قرن بالدين يكون ضماناً له تنبه. وأقول: ومقتضى ذلك أن القاضي لو سأل المدعى عليه عن جواب الدعوى فقال عندي كان إقراراً تأمل.
And we have previously presented the words of al-Ramli: And likewise, if he usurped a horse... and you know that guarantee of a usurped object is valid as is clear from the conditions of the guaranteed thing, and it will also come explicitly in the text, and Allah knows best. His saying: (If he uttered these words unconditionally) al-Ramli said: i.e., 'I will pay it, I will hand it over, I will receive it.' His saying: (And on this a case occurred etc.) al-Ramli said: How is this? Qadikhan explained the issue by saying because 'ʿind' when used for a debt indicates obligation, which necessitates no difference between conditional and unconditional; rather consideration is given to the contextual clue: ponder. His saying: (And we have previously presented from al-Khaniyyah regarding the conditional case) He said in al-Nahr: And I say: it is clearly stated in al-Khaniyyah that 'ʿind' indicates liability when attached to a debt not tied to a condition. So if he demands his debt and he says 'Do not demand, your property is with me,' he becomes a guarantor; this is apparent, so reflect. And al-Ramli wrote on it: what he said: I say, al-Ghazzi said, and I also say: the transmitted in al-Tatar Khaniyyah in the second chapter of the Book of Kafalah that among the words of kafalah are 'ilayya' and 'ʿindī'. Then he said: its absolute use indicates wadīʿah, but with the context of debt it becomes kafalah. That is what al-Ghazzi transmitted. I say: it necessitates no difference like the reasoning of Qadikhan. And I say: al-Zaylaʿī mentioned in his commentary on his statement in Iqrār 'ʿindī' and 'maʿī' etc. that its absolute use means the statement is interpreted according to custom, and in custom, 'ʿindī' when coupled with debt is a guarantee for it, so be aware. And I say: what follows from this is that if the judge asks the defendant about the answer to the claim and he says 'ʿindī', it is an admission: ponder.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe discussing the legal effect of the words 'ʿindī' and 'ʿalā' in the context of kafalah. The author, Ibn Nujaym, is examining the opinions of earlier scholars, particularly Qadikhan and al-Ramli, regarding whether these words create a binding guarantee. The core issue is whether 'ʿindī' is like 'ʿalā' in effecting kafalah unconditionally or only with context. Qadikhan argued that 'ʿindī' indicates obligation when used for a debt, so it creates kafalah regardless of conditionality, relying on contextual clues. Al-Ramli questions this view, noting that in al-Khaniyyah it is stated that 'ʿindī' only creates liability if attached to a debt without a condition. The passage also references al-Tatar Khaniyyah which lists 'ʿindī' as a word of kafalah but notes its absolute use indicates wadīʿah unless there is a context of debt. The author sides with Qadikhan and al-Zaylaʿī that the matter is determined by custom, and in custom 'ʿindī' when coupled with a debt implies guarantee. The practical consequence is that if a judge asks a defendant about a debt and he says 'ʿindī', it is considered an admission. This shows the Hanafi reliance on 'urf and qarina in interpreting ambiguous contractual terms.

Key Terms
عندي (ʿindī): Literally 'with me,' but in the context of kafalah it can mean 'I am liable for this debt' depending on context.
على (ʿalā): Literally 'upon me,' which is a standard word for debt obligation; in kafalah it means 'I owe it as guarantee.'
كفالة (kafālah): Suretyship or guarantee, where a person (kafil) undertakes to fulfill a debt or obligation of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does al-Zaylaʿī say about interpreting 'ʿindī'?
A) It is always a deposit.
B) It is interpreted by custom.
C) It is a guarantee only if unconditional.
D) It is meaningless.

Key Takeaway
The word 'ʿindī' in kafalah is interpreted by custom; when used for a debt, it creates a binding guarantee.

Kafalah with Unknown Amount: The Effect of 'Idhā' and 'Kullamā'
Rulings on kafalah with unknown amount and conditional wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi validity of kafalah with an unknown amount and apply the distinction between 'idhā' (single) and 'kullamā' (repetitive) to determine the guarantor's liability.

Core Idea
A guarantee for an unknown future debt is valid, but the particle 'idhā' limits liability to the first sale, unlike 'kullamā' which repeats.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Rā'iq discusses two interconnected issues: the validity of guaranteeing a debt whose amount is not yet known, and the crucial linguistic distinction between conditional particles. Understanding these rules is essential for determining the extent of a guarantor's obligation in deferred or uncertain transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Ruling
Recognize that kafalah with an unknown amount (al-kafālah bi al-majhūl) is valid (sahih) because the debt becomes known later. The example given is guaranteeing 'whatever I sell to so-and-so is on me.'
This is an exception to the general rule that the subject matter of a contract must be known; here the debt is determinable later.
2
Analyze the Linguistic Proof
The author cites from al-Mabsūṭ the difference between 'idhā' (which does not imply repetition) and 'kullamā' and 'mā' (which do imply repetition). Understand that this linguistic distinction is the basis for the ruling.
Other particles like 'matā' and 'in' follow the same rule as 'idhā'.
3
Apply the Distinction to a Concrete Example
If the guarantor uses 'idhā' and makes two sales, only the first sale is covered. If he uses 'kullamā', each sale creates a new obligation.
This shows how a single word changes the scope of liability.
4
Understand the Revocation Rule
The guarantor may revoke before any sale occurs. Since no liability has accrued, the revocation is valid. The rationale is that kafalah is based on removing deception (ghurur); once the creditor is warned, he cannot claim reliance.
Revocation after a sale would be ineffective because liability has already attached.
5
Note the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Abu Yusuf differs, holding that 'idhā' can imply repetition and thus all sales are covered. The muftā bihi is Abu Hanifa's view (only first sale).
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school; the relied-upon position is from zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وبما يدركك في هذا البيع وهذا هو ضمان الدرك... قوله: (وما بايعت فلاناً فعلي من أمثلة الكفالة بالمجهول. وفي المبسوط: ولو قال إذا بقته شيئاً فهو علي فباعه متاعاً بألف درهم ثم باعه متاعاً بعد ذلك بألف درهم لزم الكفيل الأول دون الثاني لأن حرف «إذا» لا يقتضي التكرار بخلاف كلما) و (ما) ومثل إذا متى» و «إن». ولو رجع هذا الضمان قبل أن يبايعه ونهاه عن مبايعته ثم بايعه بعد ذلك لم يلزمه شيء لأن لزوم الكفالة بعد وجود المبايعة وتوجه المطالبة على الكفيل فأما قبل ذلك هو غير مطلوب بشيء ولا ملتزم في ذمته شيئاً فيصح رجوعه، يوضحه أن بعد المبايعة إنما أوجبنا المال على الكفيل دفعاً للغرور عن الطالب لأنه يقول إنما اعتمدت في المبايعة معه كفالة هذا الرجل وقد اندفع هذا الغرور حين نهاه عن المبايعة ا هـ. وفي الولوالجية: لو قال رجعت عن الكفالة قبل الكفيل عن أيضاً: المنقول في التتارخانية في الفصل الثاني من كتاب الكفالة أن من ألفاظ الكفالة إلي وعندي ثم قال: وإن مطلقه لفظ يني عندي للوديعة لكنه بقرينة الدين يكون كفالة .اهـ. ما نقله الغزي.
His saying: (And regarding what will catch up with you in this sale) and this is ḍamān al-darak... His saying: (And whatever I sold to so-and-so is upon me) is an example of kafalah with an unknown amount. In al-Mabsūṭ: 'If he said, "Whenever I sell something to him, it is upon me," then he sold him goods for one thousand dirhams and then sold him goods after that for one thousand dirhams, the first guarantor is liable only for the first sale, not the second, because the particle 'idhā' does not necessitate repetition, unlike 'kullamā' and 'mā'. And similar to 'idhā' are 'matā' and 'in'. And if he revoked this guarantee before he sold to him and forbade him from selling to him, then he sold to him after that, he is not liable for anything, because the binding of the kafalah occurs after the sale and the demand being directed at the guarantor. Before that, he is not demanded for anything and nothing is obligated in his dhimmah, so his revocation is valid. This is clarified by the fact that after the sale we only make the guarantor pay the money to remove deception from the claimant, because he says, 'I only relied on this man's guarantee when selling to him,' and this deception is removed when he forbids him from selling.' In al-Walwalijiyyah: 'If he said, I have revoked the kafalah before the guarantor... also the transmitted in al-Tatar Khaniyyah in the second chapter of the Book of Kafalah that among the words of kafalah are 'ilayya' and 'ʿindī'. Then he said: its absolute use indicates wadīʿah, but with the context of debt it becomes kafalah.'
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage covers two issues: first, kafalah with an unknown amount (al-kafālah bi al-majhūl). The example given is someone guaranteeing 'whatever I sell to so-and-so is upon me.' This is valid as a kafalah because the underlying debt is known later. Second, the effect of conditional wording: if the guarantor says 'idhā' (whenever), it covers only the first sale, not subsequent ones, because 'idhā' is a particle for a single future event, not repetition. In contrast, 'kullamā' and 'mā' imply repetition, so each sale triggers a new obligation. The same rule applies to 'matā' and 'in'. The author also discusses revocation: if the guarantor revokes before any sale, the revocation is effective because no liability has yet been incurred. The reason is that kafalah is based on removing deception (ghurūr); once the guarantor forbids the creditor from selling, the creditor is warned and cannot claim reliance. Finally, the passage reiterates from al-Tatar Khaniyyah the status of the words 'ilayya' and 'ʿindī' as words of kafalah with contextual interpretation.

Key Terms
إذا (idhā): A conditional particle that indicates a single future event, not repetition.
كلما (kullamā): A particle meaning 'every time,' implying repetition of the condition and consequent obligation.
الكفالة بالمجهول (al-kafālah bi al-majhūl): A guarantee for a debt whose amount is unknown at the time of the kafalah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a guarantor says 'إذا بعت فلاناً شيئاً فعلي' (Whenever I sell something to so-and-so, it is on me) and then sells two items, what is the liability according to Abu Hanifa?
A) He is liable for both sales.
B) He is liable for only the first sale.
C) He is liable for neither because the condition is ambiguous.
D) He is liable for the greater of the two prices.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah with an unknown amount is valid, and the particle 'idhā' limits the guarantor's liability to the first occurrence, while 'kullamā' covers each subsequent occurrence.

Stipulation of Discharge Converts Kafalah to Hawala
Explaining contract conversion via meaning over wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a stipulation of discharge transforms kafalah into hawala and apply the principle of i'tibar li al-ma'ani to similar contract conversions.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, the meaning behind a contract overrides its literal wording—a stipulation of discharge turns kafalah into hawala.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a key Hanafi principle: the legal effect of a contract is determined by its underlying meaning, not its literal name. If a surety stipulates that he will not seek reimbursement from the debtor, the contract becomes an assignment of debt (hawala). Conversely, if in hawala the transferor stipulates he remains liable, it becomes a suretyship (kafalah). The commentator supports this with examples from agency, hire, and loan, showing that the law consistently prioritizes substance over form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and stipulation
What is the default form of kafalah? How does a stipulation of discharge change it? Recognize that the stipulation alters the underlying meaning.
2
Understand the principle of i'tibar li al-ma'ani
Why does the law consider meaning over wording? The commentator gives examples from wakalah, ijarah, and i'arah to illustrate that the same principle applies across contract types.
3
Apply the principle to the given case
How does this principle convert kafalah to hawala and vice versa? Follow the logic: if discharge is stipulated, the surety is not seeking repayment, which is the essence of hawala.
4
Recognize the exception
In which contract does the principle not apply? The passage notes that in sharikat al-mufawadah, wording is considered over meaning. Understand why this exception exists.
5
Derive the ruling
What are the consequences for the parties? If the contract is deemed hawala, the original debtor is discharged; if kafalah, the surety retains recourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إلا إذا شرط البرءاة فحينئذ تكون حوالة كما أن الحوالة بشرط أن لا يبرأ بها المحيل كفالة) اعتباراً للمعنى فيهما مجازاً لا للفظ وإذا صارت حوالة جرى فيها أحكامها، وكذا في عكسه تجري أحكام الكفالة. وفي وكالة البزازية : الوصاية حال حياته وكالة والوكالة بعد موته وصاية لأن المنظور المعاني ا هـ . وفي إجارتها وتنعقد بقوله أعرتك هذه الدار شهراً بكذا وكل شهر بكذا ولا تنعقد الإعارة بالإجارة حتى لو قال أجرتك منافعها سنة بلا عوض تكون إجارة فاسدة لا عارية، وكذا لو قال وهبتك منافعها بلا عوض لا تكون عارية ا هـ . لفظ العارية للإجارة دون عكسه وليس خارجاً عن قولهم الاعتبار للمعاني لأن معنى الإجارة وجد في الإعارة لأنها من التعاور وهو التناوب وهو معنى الإجارة حيث كان والإجارة لا تستعار للإعارة لأنها تفيد العوض والإعارة تفيد عدمه، وقدمنا في أول البيوع أن شركة المفاوضة يعتبر فيه لفظها لا المعنى وذكرنا الجواب عنه قوله : ولو طالب أحدهما كان له أن يطالب الآخر ذكرنا - قالوا - بخلاف المغصوب منه إذا اختار أحد الغاصبين لأن اختيار أحدهما يتضمن السيك من عند قضاء القاضي به فلا يمكنه التمليك بعد ذلك، الآخر من وأما المطالبة بالكفالة لا تة نسميه ما لم يوجد منه حقيقة الاستيفاء. وفي غصب البزازية : اختار المالك تضمين الغاصب الأول ورضي به الغاصب أو لم يرض لكن حكم له بالقيمة على الأول فليس له أن يرجع ويضمن الثاني، وإن لم يرض به الأول ولم يحكم به كان له أن ويضمن الثاني، فإن اختاره الأول ولم يعطه شيئاً وهو مفلس فالحاكم يأمر الأول بقبض ماله على الثاني ويعطيه له، فإن أبى المالك يحضرهما ثم يقبل البينة على الغاصب الثاني للغاصب الأول ويأخذ ذلك من الثاني فيقبضه اهـ.
His statement: (Except if he stipulates discharge, then it becomes hawala, just as hawala with the condition that the transferor is not discharged by it is kafalah.) This is by considering the meaning metaphorically, not the wording. If it becomes hawala, the rulings of hawala apply to it, and likewise in the opposite case, the rulings of kafalah apply. And in Wakalah of al-Bazzaziyyah: Guardianship during his lifetime is agency, and agency after his death is guardianship, because the considerations are meanings. And in its Ijarah: A contract is made by saying 'I lend you this house for a month for such and such, and each month for such and such,' and a loan (i'arah) is not contracted by ijarah, even if he says 'I hire out its benefits for a year without compensation,' it is an invalid ijarah, not a loan. And likewise if he says 'I gift you its benefits without compensation,' it is not a loan. The word 'i'arah' is used for ijarah but not vice versa, and this is not outside their statement that consideration is given to meanings, because the meaning of ijarah exists in i'arah, as it is from ta'awur which is alternation, and that is the meaning of ijarah wherever it is, but ijarah is not used for i'arah because it implies compensation and i'arah implies lack of it. And we mentioned earlier in the beginning of sales that the partnership of mufawadah considers its wording not its meaning, and we mentioned the answer to that. His statement: 'And if one of them demands, he may demand the other' – we mentioned – they said – unlike the one whose property has been usurped if he chooses one of the two usurpers, because his choosing one entails [؟] by the judgment of the judge, so he cannot transfer ownership after that. As for demanding in kafalah, it does not become [؟] as long as actual recovery has not taken place. And in Ghasp of al-Bazzaziyyah: If the owner chooses to hold the first usurper liable and the usurper agrees or not, but judgment is given against the first for the value, then he cannot go back and hold the second liable. But if the first does not agree and no judgment is given, he may hold the second liable. If he chooses the first and he does not pay anything and is insolvent, the judge orders the first to seize his property from the second and give it to him; if the owner refuses, the judge summons both, then accepts evidence against the second for the first, and takes it from the second and gives it to him.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling that if the surety stipulates that he is discharged from liability, the contract becomes hawala (assignment of debt), and conversely, if in hawala the transferor stipulates that he is not discharged, it becomes kafalah. The commentator explains that this is because the law considers the underlying meaning and substance, not the literal wording. He supports this with examples from other areas: in wakalah, guardianship during life is considered agency and agency after death is considered guardianship because the meanings are what matter. Similarly, in ijarah and i'arah, the commentator shows that the word 'i'arah' can be used for ijarah (since both involve exchange of benefits), but ijarah cannot be used for i'arah because ijarah requires compensation and i'arah does not. However, he notes an exception: in sharikat al-mufawadah, the wording itself is considered, not the meaning. The passage also addresses the question of demanding payment from the surety or debtor: if one demands from either, he may later demand from the other, unlike in the case of multiple usurpers where choosing one precludes recourse against the other after judgment. The commentator then cites a fatwa from al-Bazzaziyyah detailing the rules for choosing among usurpers.

Key Terms
اعتبار للمعاني (i'tibar li al-ma'ani): Giving weight to the underlying meanings and purposes of contracts rather than literal wording.
حوالة (hawalah): Assignment of debt where the creditor transfers his claim to a third party, and the original debtor is released.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person says 'I am the surety for Zaid's debt, but if I pay, I will not claim from Zaid,' what type of contract is this?
A) Kafalah with an invalid condition
B) Hawala
C) A void contract
D) Kafalah without any effect

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the substance of a contract governs its legal effect; a stipulation of discharge converts kafalah into hawala.

Suspending Kafalah on a Suitable Condition
Explaining the ruling and conditions for suspending kafalah on a suitable condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on suspending kafalah on suitable conditions and distinguish between suitable and unsuitable conditions.

Core Idea
A kafalah suspended on a condition that causes or enables payment is valid, even if the condition involves a third party.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the validity of suspending a guarantee (kafalah) on a condition that is suitable (mulaim) to the contract. The commentator clarifies that a condition is suitable if it is a cause for the obligation of the debt or a means to enable the creditor to recover the debt. We will walk through the ruling, the evidence from the Quran, and an important intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether the condition must be tied to the principal debtor or can involve any person whose presence facilitates payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Recognize that the author states: 'It is valid to suspend kafalah on a suitable condition.' The ruling is that such suspended kafalah is sahih (valid).
Suitable conditions are those that are a cause of the obligation or enable recovery.
2
Categorize Suitable Conditions
From the passage, identify three categories: (1) condition of the right becoming due (e.g., sold item reclaimed); (2) condition for possibility of recovery (e.g., principal debtor returns); (3) condition of impossibility (e.g., debtor absent from city).
Unsuitable conditions are those not harmonizing with the contract, like 'if a lion eats you'.
3
Examine the Quranic Proof
The author cites the verse of Surah Yusuf (12:72) where one brother says 'I am a surety for it' for the reward of bringing the missing cup. This shows that kafalah can be suspended on a condition (bringing the cup) that is a cause for obligation.
The dalil is Quranic (qati al-thubut, zanni al-dalala). The wajh al-dalalah is that the condition is a cause (sabab) for the obligation of the reward.
4
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Some scholars (e.g., from al-'Inayah) restrict suitable conditions to those involving the principal debtor; they argue that a stranger's return is like the blowing of the wind. Others (like Abu Nasr and from al-Bada'i') permit any condition that is a means to payment, even if the condition involves a third party who is a partner, agent, etc.
The commentator sides with the broader view from al-Bada'i'.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position is that suspension is valid if the condition is a means to payment, even if the person is not the principal debtor. The tarjih is from Ibn Nujaym favoring al-Bada'i'.
Practical effect: if Zaid (stranger) returns, surety is liable according to the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ويصح تعليق الكفالة بشرط ملائم كشرط وجوب الحق كأن استحق المبيع أو لإمكان الاستيفاء كأن قدم زيد وهو مكفول عنه أو لتعذره كان غاب عن المصر ولا يصح معلقة ضامن لك ) أي ملائم لمقتضي العقد والملاءمة فيه بكونه سبباً لوجوبه عبر عنه بالشرط مجازاً لأن استحقاقه سبب لوجوب الثمن على البائع للمشتري، ومن هذا القبيل ما في الآية فإن الكفالة بالجعل بسبب وجوبه وهو المجيء بالصاع فإنه سبب وجوب الجعل، وقدمنا الكلام على الآية ومنه ما في الخلاصة ناقلاً . : قال للمودع إن أتلف المودع وديعتك أو جحدك فأنا عن الأصل : وكذا إن قتلك أو ابنك فلان خطأ فأنا ضامن للدية صح بخلاف إن أكلك صح، سبع ونحوه مما ليس ملائماً ا هـ . والإضافة إلى سبب الوجوب حقيقي كما في الكتاب وحكمي كما إذا كفل بالأجرة فإنها لا تجب على الكفيل إلا باستيفاء الأصيل أو التمكن أو شرط التعجيل كأنه مضاف إلى سبب الوجوب، وتمامه إجارة البزازية. قوله : (أو لإمكان الاستيفاء كأن قدم زيد وهو مكفول عنه فإن قدومه سبب موصل للاستيفاء منه، ولم يذكر الشارحون للمختصر مفهوم قوله وهو مكفول عنه ومفهومه أنه لو علقها بقدوم زيد الأجنبي لم يصح وظاهر ما في القنية الصحة على الأصح قال فيهما لا التعليق بشرط يصح غير متعارف كدخول الدار أو قدوم زيد إلا أن الأصح ما ذكر أبو نصر أنه يصح بقدوم زيد؛ ذكره في تحفة الفقهاء ا هـ. وهو بإطلاقه شامل للأجنبي ولكن ينبغي أن يحمل على أنه مكفول عنه لقوله في العناية : قيد بكون زيد مكفولاً عنه لأنه كان أجنبياً كان التعليق به باطلاً كما في هبوب الريح ا.هـ. وهكذا في فتح القدير. والحق أنه لا يلزم أن يكون مكفولاً عنه . قال في البدائع : لأن قدومه وسيلة إلى الأداء في الجملة الجواز أن يكون مكفولاً . عنه أو مضار به ويدل عليه أيضاً ما قدمناه من الأصح وعبارة البدائع أزالت اللبس وأوضحت كل ا هـ.
His statement: (It is valid to suspend kafalah on a suitable condition, such as a condition of the right becoming due, like if the sold item is reclaimed, or for the possibility of recovery, like if Zaid returns while he is the one guaranteed, or for its impossibility, like if he was absent from the city. And it is not valid to say 'I am your surety' conditionally.) That is, suitable to the requirement of the contract, and the suitability in it is that it is a cause for its obligation; it is expressed as a condition metaphorically because the reclamation is a cause for the obligation of the price on the seller to the buyer. Of this kind is what is in the verse, for the kafalah for the reward is due to its cause, which is bringing the measurement, for it is a cause for the obligation of the reward. And we have previously discussed the verse. And of this kind is what is in al-Khulasah, quoting: He said to the depositary: 'If the depositary destroys your deposit or denies it, then I am [responsible] for the property'; likewise 'If he kills you or your son so-and-so by mistake, then I am surety for the diyah' – it is valid, unlike 'if a lion eats you' and the like which are not suitable. And the addition to the cause of obligation is real as in the book, and legal (hukmi) as when he becomes surety for the rent, for it is not obligatory on the surety except upon the principal's recovery or the ability or a condition of acceleration, as if it is added to the cause of obligation. Its completion is in Ijarah of al-Bazzaziyyah. His statement: (or for the possibility of recovery, like if Zaid returns while he is the one guaranteed) – for his return is a cause leading to recovery from him. And the commentators of al-Mukhtasar did not mention the implication of his statement 'while he is the one guaranteed'; its implication is that if he suspended it on the return of Zaid the stranger, it would not be valid. But what appears in al-Qunyah is validity on the most correct view: 'Suspension on a condition that is not customary is not valid, like entering the house or Zaid's return, except that the most correct view is what Abu Nasr mentioned: that it is valid upon Zaid's return; it is mentioned in Tuhfat al-Fuqaha.' And it is general, including a stranger, but it should be interpreted as [Zaid being] the one guaranteed because of his statement in al-'Inayah: 'He restricted it to Zaid being the one guaranteed because if he were a stranger, the suspension on it would be invalid, like the blowing of the wind.' And similarly in Fath al-Qadir. But the truth is that it is not necessary that he be the one guaranteed. He said in al-Bada'i': 'Because his return is a means to payment in general, it is permissible whether he is the one guaranteed or someone beneficial to him.' And this is supported by what we have mentioned from the most correct view. The wording of al-Bada'i' removed the confusion and clarified everything.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on suspending kafalah (guarantee) on a condition. The author states that it is valid to suspend kafalah on a condition that is suitable (mulaim) to the contract, meaning a condition that is a cause for the obligation of the debt or that enables the creditor to recover the debt. Examples: (1) Suspension on the condition that the sold item is reclaimed (istihqaq al-mabi') – this makes the price due from the seller, so the surety's obligation becomes due. (2) Suspension on the return of the principal debtor (Zaid) because his return makes it possible to demand payment from him. (3) Suspension on the impossibility of recovery (e.g., if the debtor has left the city) is also valid. But kafalah cannot be suspended on conditions that are not suitable (e.g., if a lion eats someone). The commentator then discusses a dispute: if the condition is 'if Zaid returns' but Zaid is not the principal debtor (i.e., a stranger), is it valid? Some scholars (like the author of al-'Inayah) say no, because there is no direct link to the obligation. Others (like Abu Nasr and the author of al-Bada'i') say yes, as long as Zaid's return is a means to payment (e.g., if he is a partner, debtor, or agent of the principal). The commentator sides with the latter, noting that the text from al-Bada'i' clarifies that the condition need not be strictly limited to the principal debtor himself; any person whose presence facilitates recovery suffices.

Key Terms
تعليق الكفالة (ta'liq al-kafalah): Suspending the effect of a kafalah contract on the occurrence of a future condition.
شرط ملائم (shart mulaim): A condition that is harmonious with the contract, meaning it is a cause for the obligation or enables execution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view, which of the following suspensions of kafalah is valid?
A) 'If the sun rises, I am surety for the debt of Amr.'
B) 'If Zaid, a business partner of Amr, returns, I am surety for Amr's debt.'
C) 'If you eat this apple, I am surety for the debt of Bakr.'
D) 'I am surety for the debt, but only if the debtor asks me.'

Key Takeaway
A kafalah suspended on a condition that is a cause for the obligation or a means to recovery is valid, even if the condition involves a third party whose presence facilitates payment.

Invalid Suspension of Kafalah on Irrelevant Condition
Ruling with proof on invalid suspension of kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a kafalah suspended on an irrelevant condition and apply the principle of suitability (الملاءمة) to determine the validity of the suspension.

Core Idea
A guarantee is valid immediately if conditioned on an irrelevant event — the condition is void, not the contract.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi ruling on suspending a guarantee on a condition that has no connection to the purpose of suretyship. The matn states that such a suspension is invalid, and the commentator clarifies the reason: the condition is not suitable (غير ملائم). The guarantee itself is sahih, and the money becomes wajib immediately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the condition
The act is kafalah (suretyship) for a debt. The condition suspending it is something irrelevant like 'if the wind blows' or 'if it rains'.
The condition must be unrelated to the purpose of securing payment.
2
Understand the ruling on the suspension
The matn states 'لا يصح' meaning the suspension is invalid. The consequence is that the guarantee is effective immediately, and the money becomes wajib at once.
The guarantee itself is valid; only the suspension is void.
3
Analyse the reason: suitability of the condition
The condition is not suitable (غير ملائم) for kafalah because it does not facilitate payment. The Hanafi principle: only conditions that are means to the obligation's fulfillment can validly suspend the contract.
Example of a suitable condition: arrival of a debtor who owes money to the principal — that is a means to payment.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi textual dispute
The commentator mentions that in al-Hidayah and al-Kafi, the matn is different and would imply the money is not due. He corrects this as an oversight, affirming the majority position that the money is due immediately.
This illustrates the importance of accurate transmission within the madhhab.
5
Apply the principle to similar conditions
Other irrelevant conditions (e.g., entering a house, arrival of a stranger) receive the same ruling: the guarantee is valid and the money is due immediately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا يصح بنحو إن هبت الريح فتصح الكفالة ويجب المال حالا) ومثله التعليق بنزول المطر ودخول الدار وقدوم زيد وهو غير مكفول عنه. وذكر الشارح أن المذكور في المختصر مذكور في الهداية والكافي وهو سهو فإن الحكم فيه أن التعليق لا يصح ولا يلزم المال لأن الشرط غير ملائم فصار كما لو علقه بدخول الدار ونحوه مما ليس بملائم؛ ذكره قاضيخان وغيره. ولو جعل الأصل في الكفالة إلى هبوب الريح لا يصح التأجيل ويجب المال حالا.
His saying: (And it is not valid by something like 'if the wind blows', so the suretyship becomes valid and the money is obligatory immediately). Similar to it is suspension by the descent of rain or entering the house or the arrival of Zayd while he is not the one on whose behalf the guarantee is given. And the commentator mentioned that what is mentioned in al-Mukhtasar is mentioned in al-Hidayah and al-Kafi, and it is an oversight, for the ruling therein is that the suspension is not valid and the money is not obligatory because the condition is not suitable, so it becomes like if he suspended it on entering the house and the like which are not suitable; Qadikhan and others mentioned it. And if one makes the basis of the suretyship until the wind blows, the deferment is not valid and the money is obligatory immediately.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanatory proof from the commentary on the Matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The matn states: 'It is not valid' (i.e., the suspension of a guarantee) by a condition like 'if the wind blows' meaning if a person says to the creditor: 'I guarantee the debt of so-and-so if the wind blows.' The ruling is that such a suspended guarantee is not valid as a suspension; rather, the guarantee becomes immediately effective and the money is due at once (حالاً). The commentator (al-Zayla'i) notes that in al-Mukhtasar, al-Hidayah, and al-Kafi, the ruling is that the suspension is invalid and the money does not become obligatory because the condition is not appropriate (غير ملائم) for the purpose of suretyship, which is to secure payment. It is analogous to suspending it on entering a house—both are unrelated conditions. Qadikhan and others mention this. However, if the condition is something that facilitates payment, like the arrival of a person who owes money to the principal debtor, then the suspended guarantee can be valid. In the present case, since the condition (wind blowing) is not a means to payment, the guarantee becomes due immediately. The Hanafi position is that the guarantee itself is valid (sahih) but the suspension is void (fasid); the mal (amount guaranteed) becomes wajib (obligatory) at once. This ruling is from the zahir al-riwayah and is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): A contract of suretyship where a person (kafil) guarantees the debt or obligation of another (asil) to a creditor (makful lahu).
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspending the effect of a contract (here kafalah) on the occurrence of a future condition.
الشرط الملائم (al-shart al-mula'im): A condition that is appropriate and relevant to the purpose of the contract, such that it facilitates or ensures the fulfillment of the obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a guarantee is suspended on 'If the wind blows', what is the status of the guarantee?
A) The guarantee is completely invalid.
B) The guarantee is valid and the money is due immediately.
C) The guarantee is valid only when the wind blows.
D) The guarantee is valid but the money is deferred until the wind blows.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, suspending a kafalah on an irrelevant condition does not delay the obligation; the guarantee is valid and the money is due at once.

Kafalah with Invalid Conditions: Intra-Hanafi Positions
Critical analysis of manuscript variants and rulings on kafalah with non-commensurate conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two intra-Hanafi positions on the validity of kafalah with a non-commensurate condition (e.g., 'if the wind blows') and identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Core Idea
A kafalah conditioned on a future event like 'if the wind blows' is either valid with the condition void, or entirely invalid—two authoritative Hanafi positions.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute over whether a kafalah (surety) can be valid if tied to a non-commensurate condition. The commentator examines manuscript variants and reconciles seemingly contradictory texts from al-Hidayah, al-Mabsut, and others, revealing two distinct positions within the school. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to handle textual variations and to recognize the scope of valid debate within Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the contested condition
The act is kafalah (suretyship) tied to a non-commensurate condition, e.g., 'if the wind blows' or 'if it rains'. The condition is a future event that does not normally govern a financial obligation.
Non-commensurate (ghayr mulaim) conditions are those not customary for the contract type.
2
State the first position (validity) and its textual basis
From al-Hidayah: the kafalah is valid and the condition is void; the money is due immediately. The rationale is that kafalah can be validly suspended (ta'liq) like divorce and manumission, so invalid conditions do not invalidate the contract. The word 'ta'liq' is interpreted metaphorically as 'ta'jil' (setting a term).
This is supported by early Hanafi texts and by the author of Anfa' al-Wasa'il.
3
State the second position (invalidity) and its textual basis
From al-Mabsut and Fatawa Qadikhan: the kafalah is entirely invalid because the suspension (ta'liq) with a non-commensurate condition is itself invalid. The text should be taken literally, not reinterpreted as a term. Al-Zayla'i, al-Shurunbulali, and others support this view.
This position argues that a condition that is impossible or inappropriate renders the contract void from the start.
4
Analyze the rationale behind each position
Position 1 draws an analogy to divorce and manumission where ta'liq is valid, and treats the condition as a term (ajal) to preserve the contract. Position 2 insists that kafalah is closer to a binding obligation (iltizam) that cannot be suspended by a whimsical condition; the contract is void because the condition is not commensurate and cannot be reinterpreted.
The commentator notes that both positions are based on reliable sources and the manuscripts themselves vary.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the lack of a single mufta bihi
If a person says 'I am surety for you if the wind blows', under Position 1 he is immediately liable for the debt; under Position 2 he is not liable because the kafalah is void. The commentator leaves the issue as two valid qawls (positions) and does not give a definitive tarjih.
Later scholars like al-Shurunbulali and al-Ramli differed; the mufta bihi is not settled, and the mufti must be aware of both views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة
حالاً . ا هـ. وهو سهو منه فإن المصنف لم يقل فتصح الكفالة ويجب المال حالاً والموجود في النسخ المعتمدة الاقتصار على قوله : ولا تصح بنحو إن هبت الريح ولذا لم ينسب العيني السهو إلى المصنف وإنما نسبه إلى الهداية، فعلى هذا الأنسب أن يقرأ ولا تصح بالتاء أي الكفالة لا بالياء ليكون للتعليق، وكل منهما مخطئ في نسبته إلى الهداية وعبارة الهداية هكذا؛ فأما ما لا يصح بمجرد الشرط كقوله إن هبت الريح أو جاء المطر، وكذا إذا جلع كل واحد منهما أجلاً إلا أنه تصح الكفالة ويجب المال حالاً لأن الكفالة لما صح تعليقها بالشرط لم تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة كالطلاق والعتاق ا هـ . لأن قوله إلا أنه تصح الكفالة إنما يعود إلى الأجل بنحو إن هبت الريح لا إلى التعليق بالشرط. وقوله «لما تعليقها» معناه لما تأجيلها بأجل متعارف مجازاً ومجوزه عدم الثبوت في الحال في كل واحد منهما، وإنما صحت مع الأجل الغير المتعارف ولم تصح مع التعليق بغير المتعارف لأن التعليق يخرج العلة العلية كما عرف في الأصول والأجل عارض بعد العقد فلا يلزم من انتفائه انتفاء معروضه

المال حالاً.
كل وجه بقرينة استدلاله بعبارة البدائع تأمل قوله : (وهو سهو منه الخ) النسخة التي شرح عليها الزيلعي هكذا: ولا يصح بنحو إن هبت الريح وإن جعلا أجلاً فتصح وهكذا في النهر. فتحصل أن النسخ ثلاثة هذه والتي شرح عليها المؤلف بإسقاط «وإن جعلا أجلا» والذي عزاه إلى النسخ المعتمدة من الاقتصار على قوله ولا تصح بنحو إن هبت الربح» إذا علمت ذلك فاعلم أن الأخيرة لا إشكال فيها وكذا الأولى لأن قوله فتصح الكفالة الخ» جواب قوله «وإن جعلا أجلاً ويعلم منه أن الكفالة لا تصح في الأول إن كانت النسخة بالياء المثناة التحتية في قوله ولا يصح وإن كانت بالفوقية فهي نص في ذلك . وأما الثانية فاعتراض الزيلعي وارد عليها ولا يمكن الجواب عنه بما أجيب به عن الهداية أصلاً والعجب من الزيلعي حيث أورد الاعتراض على النسخة الأولى اللهم إلا أن يقال حمله على ذلك ما ذكره المصنف في الكافي فشرح كلامه بكلامه لأنه أدرى بمرامه فيتعين حينئذ أن تكون إن في قوله وإن جعلا أجلاً وصلية لا شرطية ليطابق الشرح المشروح والعجب ما في النهر حيث شرح على ما في الزيلعي وقال : هكذا وقع في نسخة الزيلعي . ثم ذكر عبارة الهداية وذكر أن التعليل ظاهر فيما ذكره الزيلعي ثم ذكر تأويله بما ذكره المؤلف هنا ثم قال : وهذا الحمل ممكن في كلام المصنف إلا أن عدم ذكر التأجيل في كلامه يبعده بخلافه في عبارة الهداية، وإذا تحققت هذا علمت أن ما في البحر من أن ما قاله الشارح سهو مما لا تحرير فيه، وذلك لأن اعتراض الشارح على ما وقع في نسخته وهو صحيح، وكلام الهداية ظاهر فيما فهمه كما علمت والتأويل خلاف الأصل فكيف ينسب إلى السهو ما هذا إلا كبير سهو. نعم الثابت في أكثر النسخ ولا يصح بنحو إن هبت الريح أو جاء المطر وإن جعلا أجلاً فتصح الكفالة ويجب المال حالاً أي لا يصح تعليقها بشرط غير ملائم ويعلم من قوله وإن جعلا أجلاً فتصح» أنها في التعليق لا تصح لعدم صحته، وحينئذ فكون الأنسب أن تقرأ بالفوقية مع أن الكلام في التعليق

معه
كما أشار إليه في العناية. وفي فتح القدير: فالحاصل أن الشرط الغير الملائم لا تصح . ومع الأجل الغير الملائم تصح حالاً ويبطل الأجل لكن تعليل المصنف هذا بقوله «لأن الكفالة لما صح تعليقها بالشرط يقتضي أن في التعليق بغير الملائم تصح الكفالة حالة وإنما يبطل الشرط، والمصرح به في المبسوط وفتاوى قاضيخان أن الكفالة باطلة فتصحيحه أن يحمل لفظ تعليقها على معنى تأجيلها بجامع أن في كل منهما عدم ثبوت الحكم في الحال. وقلد المصنف في هذا الاستعمال لفظ المبسوط فإنه ذكر التعليق وأراد التأجيل. هذا وظاهر شرح الأتقاني المشي على ظاهر اللفظ وفي الخلاصة : كفل بماله على أن يجعل له الطالب جعلاً فإن لم يكن مشروطاً في الكفالة فالشرط باطل، وإن كان مشروطاً فيها فالكفالة
عدول عن الظاهر بما لا داعي إليه .اهـ. فانظر هل في هذا شيء من التحرير سوى الكلام الأخير؟ هذا وذكر في الحواشي اليعقوبية أن ما ذكروه من التوجيه لكلام الهداية في غاية البعد ثم قال: فالظاهر فيه روايتان وما ذكر في الفصولين من أن الكفالة لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة موافق للرواية المذكورة في الهداية إلا أن قولهم الكفالة بالمال تشبه النذر ابتداء باعتبار الالتزام، وتشبه البيع باعتبار المعاوضة انتهاء إذ الكفيل يرجع على الأصيل بما أدى عنه فقلنا لا يصح بمطلق الشرط كهبوب الريح ونحوه، ويصح بشرط ملائم عملاً بالشبهين يقتضي صحة الرواية المنقولة عن المبسوط. وأيضاً الكفيل لم يلتزم الكفالة إلا معلقة فلو جعل كفيلاً في الحال يلزم أن يكلف بما لم يلتزمه، والأصل أن المتبرع لا يلزمه ما لم يلتزمه اهـ ملخصاً ويأتي بعده عن الرملي ما يؤيده ولكن يمكن تأويله بأن يجعل الشرط بمعنى التأجيل، ويدل عليه قوله لو كفل إلى أن تهب الريح فهذا صريح في التأجيل لا في التعليق والتأجيل كما سيذكره المؤلف وهكذا يؤول كلام الفصولين بحمل قوله لا تبطلها الشروط الفاسدة على ما إذا جعل الشرط أجلاً وللعلامة الشرنبلالي رسالة في هذه المسألة أشبع فيها الكلام سماها «بسط المقالة في تحقيق تعليق الكفالة فراجعها إن رمت المزيد وتكلم عليها في أنفع الوسائل وأطال ونقل عن كتب كثيرة في بعضها التصريح بعدم صحة الكفالة لتعليقها بالشرط الغير الملائم كما قاله الزيلعي، وفي بعضها التصريح بصحة الكفالة ولزوم المال حالاً وأيد هذا الأخير وارتضاه وارجع الأول إليه لكن خالفه الشرنبلالي في رسالته وأيد كلام الزيلعي والفتح والخانية من بطلان الكفالة وعدم لزوم المال، ورد على من جعل في المسألة قولين أقول والإنصاف أنهما قولان، فإن من اطلع على ما نقله في أنفع الوسائل من النقول لم يشك في أن العبارات متناقضة بعضها مصرح بصحة الكفالة ولزوم المال حالاً وبطلان التعليق وبعضها مصرح بعدم صحة الكفالة وارتكاب التأويل عدول عن سواء السبيل لأن بعض العبارات لا يحتمله قوله : (وفي الخلاصة كفل بماله على أن يجعل له الطالب جعلاً الخ قال الرملي : ولو كفل رجل عن رجل على أن يجعل له جعلاً فهذا على وجهين : إما أن يكون الجعل مشروطاً في الكفالة أو لا، فإن لم يكن مشروطاً في الكفالة فالجعل باطل والكفالة جائزة، أما الجعل باطل لأن الكفيل مقرض في حق المطلوب وإذا شرط له الجعل مع
Book of Kafalah. ... It is an oversight from him, for the author did not say 'thus the kafalah is valid and the money is due immediately.' The found in reliable copies is the restriction to his statement: 'It is not valid by something like if the wind blows.' ... The correct reading is 'laysa bi-ta' (i.e., al-kafalah) with ta' not ya' to indicate suspension... The version that al-Zayla'i commented on is: 'It is not valid by something like if the wind blows, and if they both make it a term, then it is valid...' Thus three versions are established... The commentator [Zayla'i]'s objection is valid... The relied-upon in most copies is: 'It is not valid by something like if the wind blows or rain comes, and if they both make it a term, then the kafalah is valid and the money is due immediately'... In Fath al-Qadir: The conclusion is that a non-commensurate condition does not make it valid, but with a non-commensurate term, it is valid immediately and the term is void... However, in al-Mabsut and Fatawa Qadikhan it is explicitly stated that the kafalah is invalid... So his correction is to interpret the word 'suspension' to mean 'setting a term'... In al-Khulasa: 'He gave surety with his property on condition that the claimant give him a reward...' and al-Ramli said: if the reward is not conditioned in the kafalah, the reward is void and the kafalah is permissible...
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a detailed critical refinement by the commentator of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, analyzing different manuscript versions and scholarly opinions on the validity of kafalah when tied to a non-commensurate condition (e.g., 'if the wind blows'). The author identifies three major manuscript readings of Kanz al-Daqa'iq and shows how each leads to a different understanding. The core dispute is between the position of al-Hidayah (that the kafalah is valid and the condition void, interpreting it as a term) and the position of al-Mabsut and others (that the kafalah is entirely invalid). The commentator examines the reasoning of both sides, including the principle that suspension with a non-commensurate condition invalidates the contract because it separates the effect from its cause. He also discusses the opinion of later scholars like al-Ramli and al-Shurunbulali, noting that the issue has two established qawls (positions). The passage ends with an excerpt from al-Khulasa about kafalah with a reward, where al-Ramli distinguishes between a condition within the kafalah and one outside it. Throughout, the commentator emphasizes textual consistency and the importance of manuscript accuracy.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of a contract's effect on the occurrence of a future condition.
أجل (ajal): A fixed term or period for payment or performance.
شرط غير ملائم (shart ghayr mulaim): A condition that is not appropriate or customary for the contract (e.g., 'if the wind blows').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the dispute discussed in the passage, what is the ruling on a kafalah tied to a condition like 'if the wind blows'?
A) The kafalah is valid and the condition is void, so the surety is liable immediately.
B) The kafalah is invalid entirely, and the surety owes nothing.
C) The kafalah is valid only if the condition is written down.
D) Both A and B are positions within the Hanafi school.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has two well-supported positions on kafalah with non-commensurate conditions; the mufta bihi is not definitively settled, requiring careful attention to textual variants and scholarly reasoning.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Non-Suitable Conditions in Kafalah: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafalah with non-suitable condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on kafalah conditioned on a non-suitable condition and explain the proof and practical consequence of each.

Core Idea
A non-suitable condition in kafalah creates an intra-Hanafi dispute: some void the contract, others validate it with the condition null.

This lesson examines the disagreement among Hanafi jurists regarding the validity of kafalah when it is suspended on a condition that is not customary, such as 'when the wind blows.' The commentary from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents conflicting narrations: some say the kafalah is void, others say it is valid immediately with the condition deemed void. Understanding the reasoning behind each view is essential for determining the surety's liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition type
The condition is ghayr mula'im (non-suitable), e.g., 'when the wind blows.' This is not typical in suretyship contracts.
2
State the two positions
Position A: kafalah void (Al-Mabsut, Fatawa Qadikhan, Al-Zayla'i, Al-Fath, Al-Khaniyyah). Position B: kafalah valid immediately, condition void (Al-Hidayah, Al-Fusulayn).
3
Analyze the proofs
Position A uses qiyas comparing kafalah to a vow initially (requires immediate obligation) and to a sale eventually (requires suitable condition). Position B argues that conditions that do not benefit the surety (like weather) do not invalidate; the condition is treated as a deferment.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Position A, the surety is not liable and does not pay. Under Position B, the surety must pay immediately, and the condition is ignored.

أنه
ضمان المثل فقد شرط له الزيادة على ما أقرضه فهو باطل لأنه ربا والكفالة جائزة لأنها مطلقة غير معلقة بالجعل، فأما إذا كان الجعل مشروطاً في الكفالة ذكر أن الجعل باطل والكفالة باطلة، أما الجعل باطل لما بينا وكان يجب أن تصح الكفالة لأن الكفالة مما لا يبطلها الشروط الفاسدة ألا لو كفل إلى أن تهب الريح أو تمطر السماء كان الشرط باطلاً والكفالة صحيحة فيجب أن يكون الجواب ههنا كذلك. والجواب عنه أن الكفالة متى بطلت إنما بطلت لأنه شرط فيها شرط فاسد فإن لم تصح من حيث إنه علقها بشرط للكفيل فيه منفعة لأن الكفيل ممن ينتفع بالجعل فلا بد من مراعاة الشرط لتثبت الكفالة والشرط لم يثبت لما لم يستحق الجعل فلا تثبت الكفالة، وكان بطلان الكفالة من هذا الطريق لا من حيث إنه شرط بخلاف شرط هبوب الريح ومطر السماء لأنه شرط لا ينتفع به الكفيل، وإذا لم يكن للكفيل فيه منفعة لم تجب مراعاة هذا الشرط كما لو شرط في البيع شرط لا ينتفع به أحدهما، وإذا لم يثبت كانت الكفالة مرسلة. اهـ. من كفالة خواهر زاده. قول المصنف:

باطلة ا هـ . وهذا يفيد أنها تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة إذا كانت في صلبها ا هـ . وهكذا في معراج الدراية ونقل في البناية ما في العناية والمعراج ولم يتعقبه، وقد ظهر لي أنه لا حاجة إلى جعل التعليق بمعنى التأجيل بل المراد إنما صحت الكفالة هذا التأجيل لأن الكفالة لما مع تعليقها بشرط في الجملة وهو الملائم لم تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة والتأجيل بغير المتعارف شرط فاسد فلم تبطل ،به ولا يخالفه فرع الخلاصة لأنه الأجل بعد العقد كما قدمناه فليس في صلبها. وفي الخانية : كفل عن رجل بدين له على أن فلاناً وفلاناً يكفلان عنه بكذا وكذا من هذا المال فأبى الآخران أن يكفلا . قال الفقيه أبو بكر البلخي : الكفالة الأولى لازمة ولا خيار له في ترك الكفالة ا هـ . قوله : (فإن كفل بماله عليه فبرهن على ألف لزمه لأن الثابت بالبينة كالثابت عياناً ولا يكون قول الطالب حجة عليه كما لا يكون حجة على الأصيل لأنه مدع قوله : (وإلا صدق الكفيل فيما أقر بحلفه أي وإن لم يبرهن فالقول للكفيل فيما يقر به مع يمينه على نفي العلم لا على البتات كما في الايضاح ولا يكون قول المطلوب حجة عليه لأنه إقرار على الغير وهو معنى قوله ولا ينفذ. قال العيني بالتشديد. قيد بقوله على الكفيل» لأنه ينفذ على نفسه. قيد بقوله بماله عليه لأنه لو كفل بما ذاب لك على فلان أو بما ثبت فأقر المطلوب بمال لزم الكفيل لأن الثبوت حصل بقوله وذاب بمعنى حصل وقد حصل بإقراره بخلاف الكفالة بمالك عليه فإنها بالدين القائم في الحال وما ذاب ونحوه الكفالة بما سيجب والوجوب ثبت بإقراره وخرج أيضاً ما إذا كفل بما قضى لك عليه فلا يلزمه إلا بقضاء القاضي ومثل مالك عليه ما أقر لك به أمس، فلو قال المطلوب أقررت له بألف أمس لم يلزم الكفيل لأنه قبل مالاً واجباً عليه لا ما لا يجب عليه في الحال ولم يثبت أنه
The original: Kafalah. Book of Kafalah with it as indicated in Al-Inayah. And in Fath al-Qadir: So the conclusion is that the condition that is not suitable is not valid. And with a term that is not suitable, it is valid immediately and the term is void. But the author's reasoning with his statement: 'Because kafalah, since it is valid to suspend it on a condition, implies that in suspending with a non-suitable condition, the kafalah is valid immediately and only the condition is void.' And what is explicitly stated in Al-Mabsut and Fatawa Qadikhan is that the kafalah is void. So his correction is to carry the word 'suspension' to mean 'deferment' by the commonality that in each there is non-establishment of the ruling at the time. And the author followed in this usage the wording of Al-Mabsut, for he mentioned suspension and intended deferment. This, and the apparent meaning of Sharh al-Atghani is to follow the apparent wording. And in Al-Khulasah: He stood surety with his property on condition that the creditor give him a fee. If it is not conditioned in the kafalah, the condition is void; and if it is conditioned in it, then the kafalah... deviation from the apparent without reason. So consider whether there is anything in this of editing besides the last speech? This, and it was mentioned in Al-Hawashi al-Ya'qubiyyah that what they mentioned of the justification for the statement of Al-Hidayah is extremely far-fetched. Then he said: So the apparent is that there are two narrations. And what is mentioned in Al-Fusulayn, that kafalah is not invalidated by void conditions, is in agreement with the narration mentioned in Al-Hidayah, except that their statement: 'Kafalah with property resembles a vow initially with respect to obligation, and resembles a sale with respect to compensation in the end, since the surety has recourse against the principal for what he paid.' So we said it is not valid with an absolute condition like the blowing of the wind and the like, and it is valid with a suitable condition, acting on both similarities, which necessitates the validity of the narration reported from Al-Mabsut. Also, the surety did not undertake the kafalah except as suspended, so if he is made a surety immediately, he would be burdened with what he did not undertake, and the principle is that a volunteer is not obliged to do what he did not undertake. Summarized. And afterwards from Al-Ramli what supports it, but it can be interpreted by making the condition mean deferment, as indicated by his statement: 'If he stood surety until the wind blows' – this is explicit in deferment, not suspension. And deferment, as the author will mention, and thus the statement of Al-Fusulayn is interpreted by carrying his statement 'void conditions do not invalidate it' to the case where he makes the condition a term. And Al-Allamah Al-Shurunbulali has a treatise on this issue in which he discussed abundantly, calling it 'Bast al-Maqalah fi Tahqiq Ta'liq al-Kafalah', so refer to it if you desire more. And it was discussed in Anfa' al-Wasa'il, and he went on at length, and he quoted from many books. In some of them, it is explicitly stated that the kafalah is invalid due to its suspension on a non-suitable condition, as Al-Zayla'i said. And in some of them, it is explicitly stated that the kafalah is valid and the payment of the money is immediately binding, and he supported this latter and approved it, and referred the first to him. But Al-Shurunbulali differed with him in his treatise and supported the statement of Al-Zayla'i, Al-Fath, and Al-Khaniyyah of the invalidity of the kafalah and the non-binding of the money, and he responded to those who made two statements in the issue. I say: The fair view is that they are two statements, for whoever sees what is quoted in Anfa' al-Wasa'il from the quotations will not doubt that the expressions are contradictory. Some explicitly state the validity of the kafalah and the binding of the money immediately and the invalidity of the suspension, and some explicitly state the invalidity of the kafalah, and resorting to interpretation is a deviation from the right path because some expressions do not bear it. His statement: (And in Al-Khulasah: He stood surety with his property on condition that the creditor give him a fee, etc.) Al-Ramli said: If a man stands surety for a man on condition that he gives him a fee, this is two ways: Either the fee is conditioned in the kafalah or not. If it is not conditioned in the kafalah, the fee is void and the kafalah is permissible. As for the fee being void, because the surety is a lender with respect to the debtor, and if he stipulates a fee for him, along with the guarantee of the like, he has stipulated an increase on what he lent, so it is void because it is riba. And the kafalah is permissible because it is absolute, not suspended on the fee. But if the fee is conditioned in the kafalah, it is mentioned that the fee is void and the kafalah is void. As for the fee being void for what we explained, and it should be that the kafalah is valid because kafalah is not invalidated by void conditions. For example, if he stood surety until the wind blows or the rain falls, the condition is void and the kafalah is valid, so the answer should be the same here. The answer to this is that when the kafalah is invalidated, it is only invalidated because he stipulated in it a void condition. So if it is not valid because he suspended it on a condition in which the surety has a benefit, because the surety is one who benefits from the fee, so it is necessary to observe the condition for the kafalah to be established, and the condition is not established because he does not deserve the fee, so the kafalah is not established. The invalidity of the kafalah is from this path, not because it is a condition. Unlike the condition of the blowing of the wind and the rain, because it is a condition that does not benefit the surety, and if the surety has no benefit in it, it is not necessary to observe this condition, just as if one stipulates in a sale a condition that does not benefit either of them. And if it is not established, the kafalah is unconditional. End. From Kafalah Khawirzadeh. The statement of the author: Book of Kafalah 373: 'And otherwise, the surety is believed regarding what he acknowledges with his oath, and the statement of the principal is not enforced against the surety.' For if he stood surety by his order, he recovers what is correct? Void. End. And this indicates that it is invalidated by void conditions if they are in its substance. And similarly in Mi'raj al-Dirayah, and in Al-Binayah he quoted what is in Al-Inayah and Al-Mi'raj and did not comment on it. And it has become clear to me that there is no need to make 'suspension' mean 'deferment'. Rather, the intended meaning is only that the kafalah is valid with this deferment because the kafalah, when it is suspended on a condition in general that is suitable, is not invalidated by void conditions, and deferment that is not customary is a void condition, so it is not invalidated by it. And the branch of Al-Khulasah does not contradict it because the term is after the contract, as we have presented, so it is not in its substance. And in Al-Khaniyyah: He stood surety for a man for a debt he had, on condition that so-and-so and so-and-so stand surety for him for such-and-such of this money, but the other two refused to stand surety. Al-Faqih Abu Bakr al-Balkhi said: The first kafalah is binding, and he has no option to abandon the kafalah. End. His statement: 'For if he stood surety for what is upon him, and he provided proof for a thousand, it is binding upon him, because what is established by evidence is like what is established by sight, and the statement of the creditor is not proof against him, just as it is not proof against the principal because he is a claimant.' His statement: 'And otherwise, the surety is believed regarding what he acknowledges with his oath' – meaning if he did not provide proof, the saying is the surety's regarding what he acknowledges, with his oath of lack of knowledge, not of certainty, as in Al-Idah. And the statement of the principal is not proof against him because it is an admission against another, and that is the meaning of 'it is not enforced.' Al-Ayni said with tashdid. He restricted with 'against the surety' because it is enforced against himself. He restricted with 'for what is upon him' because if he stood surety for what is due to you from so-and-so or for what is established, and the principal admits a debt, it is binding on the surety because the establishment occurred by his statement, and 'what is due' means 'occurred', and it has occurred by his admission, unlike the kafalah for what is upon you, because it is for a debt existing at the moment. And 'what is due' and similar is kafalah for what will become obligatory, and the obligation is established by his admission. And also excluded is if he stood surety for what a judge has judged against him, then it is not binding on him except by the judge's decree. And like 'what is upon you' is 'what he admitted to you yesterday'. So if the principal said, 'I admitted to him a thousand yesterday,' it is not binding on the surety because he accepted what is obligatory upon him, not what is not obligatory at the moment, and it is not established that...
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the book of Kafalah (suretyship). It addresses two main issues: (1) the validity of kafalah when conditioned on a non-suitable condition, and (2) the ruling when a fee (ja'l) is promised to the surety. The author begins by discussing the disagreement among Hanafi scholars about conditions that are 'ghayr mula'im' (not suitable). According to some authorities like Al-Mabsut and Fatawa Qadikhan, such a condition invalidates the kafalah. However, the author of Al-Hidayah suggests that the condition is void but the kafalah is valid immediately, interpreting the condition as a deferment. The commentator mentions that Al-Shurunbulali wrote a treatise on this issue, and that there are two narrations: one supporting validity and one supporting invalidity. The second part deals with a specific case: a person stands surety on condition that the creditor gives him a fee. Al-Ramli distinguishes two scenarios: if the fee is not conditioned in the kafalah, the fee is void (as it constitutes riba) but the kafalah is valid; if the fee is conditioned in the kafalah, both are void because the surety benefits from the condition, unlike a condition like 'until the wind blows' which does not benefit him. The commentator then moves to a different ruling about the surety's admission when the creditor claims a debt. The surety is believed with his oath if he admits a lesser amount, and the principal's admission is not binding on the surety. The commentary explains various scenarios, such as when the principal admits a debt that was not previously established. The passage ends mid-sentence due to the page break.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where one person (the surety) guarantees the debt or obligation of another (the principal) to a creditor.
شرط غير ملائم (shart ghayr mula'im): A condition that is not suitable or not customary in such contracts, e.g., 'when the wind blows'.
جعل (ja'l): A fee or compensation promised to the surety by the creditor for standing surety.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT an authority that holds kafalah void with a non-suitable condition?
A) Al-Mabsut
B) Fatawa Qadikhan
C) Al-Hidayah
D) Al-Zayla'i

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has two valid narrations on kafalah with non-suitable conditions; the practical difference is whether the surety is immediately liable.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
7 min
Fees (Ja'l) in Kafalah: Distinction and Ruling
Ruling on fees in kafalah and the distinction based on benefit to surety

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a fee conditioned in the kafalah and one outside it, and state the corresponding rulings and reasoning.

Core Idea
If a fee for the surety is conditioned in the kafalah, the contract is void due to riba; if not, the fee is void but the kafalah stands.

This lesson addresses a common scenario: a creditor promises a fee to a surety for standing guarantee. The passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, quoting Al-Ramli, lays out two possibilities: either the fee is conditioned in the kafalah contract or it is not. The ruling hinges on whether the condition benefits the surety. Understanding this distinction is crucial for avoiding riba in suretyship arrangements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Scenario A: The fee is conditioned in the kafalah contract (e.g., 'I stand surety on condition that you pay me a fee'). Scenario B: The fee is separate and not a condition of the kafalah.
2
State the ruling for each
Scenario A: Both the fee and the kafalah are void (batil). Scenario B: The fee is void (as riba), but the kafalah is valid (sahih) and the surety must pay.
3
Trace the rationale
In Scenario A, the surety benefits from the condition (the fee), so the condition must be observed for the kafalah to be established. Since the fee is void (riba), the condition fails, and thus the kafalah also fails. In Scenario B, the fee is separate, so the kafalah is unconditional and valid; the fee is void because the surety is a lender and any extra is riba.
4
Differentiate from non-suitable conditions
A condition like 'until the wind blows' does not benefit the surety, so it does not invalidate the kafalah. The fee condition benefits the surety, so it invalidates. This shows the principle: conditions that benefit the surety may nullify the contract.
