كما أشار إليه في العناية. وفي فتح القدير: فالحاصل أن الشرط الغير الملائم لا تصح . ومع الأجل الغير الملائم تصح حالاً ويبطل الأجل لكن تعليل المصنف هذا بقوله «لأن الكفالة لما صح تعليقها بالشرط يقتضي أن في التعليق بغير الملائم تصح الكفالة حالة وإنما يبطل الشرط، والمصرح به في المبسوط وفتاوى قاضيخان أن الكفالة باطلة فتصحيحه أن يحمل لفظ تعليقها على معنى تأجيلها بجامع أن في كل منهما عدم ثبوت الحكم في الحال. وقلد المصنف في هذا الاستعمال لفظ المبسوط فإنه ذكر التعليق وأراد التأجيل. هذا وظاهر شرح الأتقاني المشي على ظاهر اللفظ وفي الخلاصة : كفل بماله على أن يجعل له الطالب جعلاً فإن لم يكن مشروطاً في الكفالة فالشرط باطل، وإن كان مشروطاً فيها فالكفالة
عدول عن الظاهر بما لا داعي إليه .اهـ. فانظر هل في هذا شيء من التحرير سوى الكلام الأخير؟ هذا وذكر في الحواشي اليعقوبية أن ما ذكروه من التوجيه لكلام الهداية في غاية البعد ثم قال: فالظاهر فيه روايتان وما ذكر في الفصولين من أن الكفالة لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة موافق للرواية المذكورة في الهداية إلا أن قولهم الكفالة بالمال تشبه النذر ابتداء باعتبار الالتزام، وتشبه البيع باعتبار المعاوضة انتهاء إذ الكفيل يرجع على الأصيل بما أدى عنه فقلنا لا يصح بمطلق الشرط كهبوب الريح ونحوه، ويصح بشرط ملائم عملاً بالشبهين يقتضي صحة الرواية المنقولة عن المبسوط. وأيضاً الكفيل لم يلتزم الكفالة إلا معلقة فلو جعل كفيلاً في الحال يلزم أن يكلف بما لم يلتزمه، والأصل أن المتبرع لا يلزمه ما لم يلتزمه اهـ ملخصاً ويأتي بعده عن الرملي ما يؤيده ولكن يمكن تأويله بأن يجعل الشرط بمعنى التأجيل، ويدل عليه قوله لو كفل إلى أن تهب الريح فهذا صريح في التأجيل لا في التعليق والتأجيل كما سيذكره المؤلف وهكذا يؤول كلام الفصولين بحمل قوله لا تبطلها الشروط الفاسدة على ما إذا جعل الشرط أجلاً وللعلامة الشرنبلالي رسالة في هذه المسألة أشبع فيها الكلام سماها «بسط المقالة في تحقيق تعليق الكفالة فراجعها إن رمت المزيد وتكلم عليها في أنفع الوسائل وأطال ونقل عن كتب كثيرة في بعضها التصريح بعدم صحة الكفالة لتعليقها بالشرط الغير الملائم كما قاله الزيلعي، وفي بعضها التصريح بصحة الكفالة ولزوم المال حالاً وأيد هذا الأخير وارتضاه وارجع الأول إليه لكن خالفه الشرنبلالي في رسالته وأيد كلام الزيلعي والفتح والخانية من بطلان الكفالة وعدم لزوم المال، ورد على من جعل في المسألة قولين أقول والإنصاف أنهما قولان، فإن من اطلع على ما نقله في أنفع الوسائل من النقول لم يشك في أن العبارات متناقضة بعضها مصرح بصحة الكفالة ولزوم المال حالاً وبطلان التعليق وبعضها مصرح بعدم صحة الكفالة وارتكاب التأويل عدول عن سواء السبيل لأن بعض العبارات لا يحتمله قوله : (وفي الخلاصة كفل بماله على أن يجعل له الطالب جعلاً الخ قال الرملي : ولو كفل رجل عن رجل على أن يجعل له جعلاً فهذا على وجهين : إما أن يكون الجعل مشروطاً في الكفالة أو لا، فإن لم يكن مشروطاً في الكفالة فالجعل باطل والكفالة جائزة، أما الجعل باطل لأن الكفيل مقرض في حق المطلوب وإذا شرط له الجعل مع
أنه
ضمان المثل فقد شرط له الزيادة على ما أقرضه فهو باطل لأنه ربا والكفالة جائزة لأنها مطلقة غير معلقة بالجعل، فأما إذا كان الجعل مشروطاً في الكفالة ذكر أن الجعل باطل والكفالة باطلة، أما الجعل باطل لما بينا وكان يجب أن تصح الكفالة لأن الكفالة مما لا يبطلها الشروط الفاسدة ألا لو كفل إلى أن تهب الريح أو تمطر السماء كان الشرط باطلاً والكفالة صحيحة فيجب أن يكون الجواب ههنا كذلك. والجواب عنه أن الكفالة متى بطلت إنما بطلت لأنه شرط فيها شرط فاسد فإن لم تصح من حيث إنه علقها بشرط للكفيل فيه منفعة لأن الكفيل ممن ينتفع بالجعل فلا بد من مراعاة الشرط لتثبت الكفالة والشرط لم يثبت لما لم يستحق الجعل فلا تثبت الكفالة، وكان بطلان الكفالة من هذا الطريق لا من حيث إنه شرط بخلاف شرط هبوب الريح ومطر السماء لأنه شرط لا ينتفع به الكفيل، وإذا لم يكن للكفيل فيه منفعة لم تجب مراعاة هذا الشرط كما لو شرط في البيع شرط لا ينتفع به أحدهما، وإذا لم يثبت كانت الكفالة مرسلة. اهـ. من كفالة خواهر زاده. قول المصنف:
كتاب الكفالة
۳۷۳
وإلا صدق الكفيل فيما أقر بحلفه ولا ينفذ قول المطلوب على الكفيل فإن كفل بأمره رجع بما
صح
باطلة ا هـ . وهذا يفيد أنها تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة إذا كانت في صلبها ا هـ . وهكذا في معراج الدراية ونقل في البناية ما في العناية والمعراج ولم يتعقبه، وقد ظهر لي أنه لا حاجة إلى جعل التعليق بمعنى التأجيل بل المراد إنما صحت الكفالة هذا التأجيل لأن الكفالة لما مع تعليقها بشرط في الجملة وهو الملائم لم تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة والتأجيل بغير المتعارف شرط فاسد فلم تبطل ،به ولا يخالفه فرع الخلاصة لأنه الأجل بعد العقد كما قدمناه فليس في صلبها. وفي الخانية : كفل عن رجل بدين له على أن فلاناً وفلاناً يكفلان عنه بكذا وكذا من هذا المال فأبى الآخران أن يكفلا . قال الفقيه أبو بكر البلخي : الكفالة الأولى لازمة ولا خيار له في ترك الكفالة ا هـ . قوله : (فإن كفل بماله عليه فبرهن على ألف لزمه لأن الثابت بالبينة كالثابت عياناً ولا يكون قول الطالب حجة عليه كما لا يكون حجة على الأصيل لأنه مدع قوله : (وإلا صدق الكفيل فيما أقر بحلفه أي وإن لم يبرهن فالقول للكفيل فيما يقر به مع يمينه على نفي العلم لا على البتات كما في الايضاح ولا يكون قول المطلوب حجة عليه لأنه إقرار على الغير وهو معنى قوله ولا ينفذ. قال العيني بالتشديد. قيد بقوله على الكفيل» لأنه ينفذ على نفسه. قيد بقوله بماله عليه لأنه لو كفل بما ذاب لك على فلان أو بما ثبت فأقر المطلوب بمال لزم الكفيل لأن الثبوت حصل بقوله وذاب بمعنى حصل وقد حصل بإقراره بخلاف الكفالة بمالك عليه فإنها بالدين القائم في الحال وما ذاب ونحوه الكفالة بما سيجب والوجوب ثبت بإقراره وخرج أيضاً ما إذا كفل بما قضى لك عليه فلا يلزمه إلا بقضاء القاضي ومثل مالك عليه ما أقر لك به أمس، فلو قال المطلوب أقررت له بألف أمس لم يلزم الكفيل لأنه قبل مالاً واجباً عليه لا ما لا يجب عليه في الحال ولم يثبت أنه
The original: Kafalah. Book of Kafalah with it as indicated in Al-Inayah. And in Fath al-Qadir: So the conclusion is that the condition that is not suitable is not valid. And with a term that is not suitable, it is valid immediately and the term is void. But the author's reasoning with his statement: 'Because kafalah, since it is valid to suspend it on a condition, implies that in suspending with a non-suitable condition, the kafalah is valid immediately and only the condition is void.' And what is explicitly stated in Al-Mabsut and Fatawa Qadikhan is that the kafalah is void. So his correction is to carry the word 'suspension' to mean 'deferment' by the commonality that in each there is non-establishment of the ruling at the time. And the author followed in this usage the wording of Al-Mabsut, for he mentioned suspension and intended deferment. This, and the apparent meaning of Sharh al-Atghani is to follow the apparent wording. And in Al-Khulasah: He stood surety with his property on condition that the creditor give him a fee. If it is not conditioned in the kafalah, the condition is void; and if it is conditioned in it, then the kafalah... deviation from the apparent without reason. So consider whether there is anything in this of editing besides the last speech? This, and it was mentioned in Al-Hawashi al-Ya'qubiyyah that what they mentioned of the justification for the statement of Al-Hidayah is extremely far-fetched. Then he said: So the apparent is that there are two narrations. And what is mentioned in Al-Fusulayn, that kafalah is not invalidated by void conditions, is in agreement with the narration mentioned in Al-Hidayah, except that their statement: 'Kafalah with property resembles a vow initially with respect to obligation, and resembles a sale with respect to compensation in the end, since the surety has recourse against the principal for what he paid.' So we said it is not valid with an absolute condition like the blowing of the wind and the like, and it is valid with a suitable condition, acting on both similarities, which necessitates the validity of the narration reported from Al-Mabsut. Also, the surety did not undertake the kafalah except as suspended, so if he is made a surety immediately, he would be burdened with what he did not undertake, and the principle is that a volunteer is not obliged to do what he did not undertake. Summarized. And afterwards from Al-Ramli what supports it, but it can be interpreted by making the condition mean deferment, as indicated by his statement: 'If he stood surety until the wind blows' – this is explicit in deferment, not suspension. And deferment, as the author will mention, and thus the statement of Al-Fusulayn is interpreted by carrying his statement 'void conditions do not invalidate it' to the case where he makes the condition a term. And Al-Allamah Al-Shurunbulali has a treatise on this issue in which he discussed abundantly, calling it 'Bast al-Maqalah fi Tahqiq Ta'liq al-Kafalah', so refer to it if you desire more. And it was discussed in Anfa' al-Wasa'il, and he went on at length, and he quoted from many books. In some of them, it is explicitly stated that the kafalah is invalid due to its suspension on a non-suitable condition, as Al-Zayla'i said. And in some of them, it is explicitly stated that the kafalah is valid and the payment of the money is immediately binding, and he supported this latter and approved it, and referred the first to him. But Al-Shurunbulali differed with him in his treatise and supported the statement of Al-Zayla'i, Al-Fath, and Al-Khaniyyah of the invalidity of the kafalah and the non-binding of the money, and he responded to those who made two statements in the issue. I say: The fair view is that they are two statements, for whoever sees what is quoted in Anfa' al-Wasa'il from the quotations will not doubt that the expressions are contradictory. Some explicitly state the validity of the kafalah and the binding of the money immediately and the invalidity of the suspension, and some explicitly state the invalidity of the kafalah, and resorting to interpretation is a deviation from the right path because some expressions do not bear it. His statement: (And in Al-Khulasah: He stood surety with his property on condition that the creditor give him a fee, etc.) Al-Ramli said: If a man stands surety for a man on condition that he gives him a fee, this is two ways: Either the fee is conditioned in the kafalah or not. If it is not conditioned in the kafalah, the fee is void and the kafalah is permissible. As for the fee being void, because the surety is a lender with respect to the debtor, and if he stipulates a fee for him, along with the guarantee of the like, he has stipulated an increase on what he lent, so it is void because it is riba. And the kafalah is permissible because it is absolute, not suspended on the fee. But if the fee is conditioned in the kafalah, it is mentioned that the fee is void and the kafalah is void. As for the fee being void for what we explained, and it should be that the kafalah is valid because kafalah is not invalidated by void conditions. For example, if he stood surety until the wind blows or the rain falls, the condition is void and the kafalah is valid, so the answer should be the same here. The answer to this is that when the kafalah is invalidated, it is only invalidated because he stipulated in it a void condition. So if it is not valid because he suspended it on a condition in which the surety has a benefit, because the surety is one who benefits from the fee, so it is necessary to observe the condition for the kafalah to be established, and the condition is not established because he does not deserve the fee, so the kafalah is not established. The invalidity of the kafalah is from this path, not because it is a condition. Unlike the condition of the blowing of the wind and the rain, because it is a condition that does not benefit the surety, and if the surety has no benefit in it, it is not necessary to observe this condition, just as if one stipulates in a sale a condition that does not benefit either of them. And if it is not established, the kafalah is unconditional. End. From Kafalah Khawirzadeh. The statement of the author: Book of Kafalah 373: 'And otherwise, the surety is believed regarding what he acknowledges with his oath, and the statement of the principal is not enforced against the surety.' For if he stood surety by his order, he recovers what is correct? Void. End. And this indicates that it is invalidated by void conditions if they are in its substance. And similarly in Mi'raj al-Dirayah, and in Al-Binayah he quoted what is in Al-Inayah and Al-Mi'raj and did not comment on it. And it has become clear to me that there is no need to make 'suspension' mean 'deferment'. Rather, the intended meaning is only that the kafalah is valid with this deferment because the kafalah, when it is suspended on a condition in general that is suitable, is not invalidated by void conditions, and deferment that is not customary is a void condition, so it is not invalidated by it. And the branch of Al-Khulasah does not contradict it because the term is after the contract, as we have presented, so it is not in its substance. And in Al-Khaniyyah: He stood surety for a man for a debt he had, on condition that so-and-so and so-and-so stand surety for him for such-and-such of this money, but the other two refused to stand surety. Al-Faqih Abu Bakr al-Balkhi said: The first kafalah is binding, and he has no option to abandon the kafalah. End. His statement: 'For if he stood surety for what is upon him, and he provided proof for a thousand, it is binding upon him, because what is established by evidence is like what is established by sight, and the statement of the creditor is not proof against him, just as it is not proof against the principal because he is a claimant.' His statement: 'And otherwise, the surety is believed regarding what he acknowledges with his oath' – meaning if he did not provide proof, the saying is the surety's regarding what he acknowledges, with his oath of lack of knowledge, not of certainty, as in Al-Idah. And the statement of the principal is not proof against him because it is an admission against another, and that is the meaning of 'it is not enforced.' Al-Ayni said with tashdid. He restricted with 'against the surety' because it is enforced against himself. He restricted with 'for what is upon him' because if he stood surety for what is due to you from so-and-so or for what is established, and the principal admits a debt, it is binding on the surety because the establishment occurred by his statement, and 'what is due' means 'occurred', and it has occurred by his admission, unlike the kafalah for what is upon you, because it is for a debt existing at the moment. And 'what is due' and similar is kafalah for what will become obligatory, and the obligation is established by his admission. And also excluded is if he stood surety for what a judge has judged against him, then it is not binding on him except by the judge's decree. And like 'what is upon you' is 'what he admitted to you yesterday'. So if the principal said, 'I admitted to him a thousand yesterday,' it is not binding on the surety because he accepted what is obligatory upon him, not what is not obligatory at the moment, and it is not established that...
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the book of Kafalah (suretyship). It addresses two main issues: (1) the validity of kafalah when conditioned on a non-suitable condition, and (2) the ruling when a fee (ja'l) is promised to the surety. The author begins by discussing the disagreement among Hanafi scholars about conditions that are 'ghayr mula'im' (not suitable). According to some authorities like Al-Mabsut and Fatawa Qadikhan, such a condition invalidates the kafalah. However, the author of Al-Hidayah suggests that the condition is void but the kafalah is valid immediately, interpreting the condition as a deferment. The commentator mentions that Al-Shurunbulali wrote a treatise on this issue, and that there are two narrations: one supporting validity and one supporting invalidity. The second part deals with a specific case: a person stands surety on condition that the creditor gives him a fee. Al-Ramli distinguishes two scenarios: if the fee is not conditioned in the kafalah, the fee is void (as it constitutes riba) but the kafalah is valid; if the fee is conditioned in the kafalah, both are void because the surety benefits from the condition, unlike a condition like 'until the wind blows' which does not benefit him. The commentator then moves to a different ruling about the surety's admission when the creditor claims a debt. The surety is believed with his oath if he admits a lesser amount, and the principal's admission is not binding on the surety. The commentary explains various scenarios, such as when the principal admits a debt that was not previously established. The passage ends mid-sentence due to the page break.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where one person (the surety) guarantees the debt or obligation of another (the principal) to a creditor.
جعل (ja'l): A fee or compensation promised to the surety by the creditor for standing surety.
شرط غير ملائم (shart ghayr mula'im): A condition that is not suitable or not customary in such contracts, e.g., 'when the wind blows'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling when a fee is conditioned in the kafalah contract?
A) Fee void, kafalah valid
B) Both void
C) Both valid
D) Fee valid, kafalah void

Key Takeaway
A fee conditioned in kafalah makes the entire contract void due to riba, unlike non-suitable conditions that do not benefit the surety.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Surety's Acknowledgment and the Principal's Denial
Explains the Hanafi ruling on the surety's liability based on evidence or acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the surety's liability when he acknowledges a debt, with or without evidence, and explain why the principal's denial does not affect the surety.

Core Idea
The surety's own acknowledgment binds him; the principal's denial does not affect his liability.

In suretyship (kafalah), the surety guarantees a debt owed by the principal. A key question arises: if the surety acknowledges the debt but the principal denies it, is the surety still liable? This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi position based on evidence and the nature of acknowledgment. The ruling hinges on whether the surety has proof or relies on his own statement, and it clarifies that one person's acknowledgment does not bind another.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Scenarios
The text distinguishes two cases: (1) the surety has clear evidence (bayyinah) of the debt, and (2) he does not have evidence. In each, the ruling differs.
Evidence here means two male witnesses or equivalent.
2
Apply the Principle of Evidence
When evidence is presented, the surety is bound because 'what is established by evidence is like what is seen with one's own eyes.' This is a rational proof (dalil 'aqli) that establishes certainty.
This principle is a legal maxim in Hanafi fiqh, elevating testimony to near-certainty.
3
Explain the Surety's Acknowledgment
Without evidence, the surety's own acknowledgment (iqrar) is sufficient to bind him, provided he swears an oath that he has no knowledge of the debt (yamin 'ala nafi al-'ilm). The principal's denial or acknowledgment does not affect this.
The oath is on negation of knowledge because the surety may not know the exact details.
4
Note the Principle 'Iqrar 'ala al-Ghayr'
The principal's statement is not proof against the surety because it is an acknowledgment against another person. This is a core Hanafi principle: one person's iqrar does not bind another.
This is why the surety's liability is independent of the principal's stance.
5
Consider Special Wordings and Edge Cases
The text also addresses cases where the suretyship wording is 'what has accrued' or 'what a judge ruled.' These affect whether the debt is considered existing at the time of guarantee.
Precise wording matters in Hanafi contracts of guarantee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن كفل بماله عليه فبرهن على ألف لزمه لأن الثابت بالبينة كالثابت عياناً ولا يكون قول الطالب حجة عليه كما لا يكون حجة على الأصيل لأنه مدع قوله : (وإلا صدق الكفيل فيما أقر بحلفه ولا ينفذ قول المطلوب على الكفيل أي وإن لم يبرهن فالقول للكفيل فيما يقر به مع يمينه على نفي العلم لا على البتات كما في الايضاح ولا يكون قول المطلوب حجة عليه لأنه إقرار على الغير وهو معنى قوله ولا ينفذ. قال العيني بالتشديد. قيد بقوله على الكفيل» لأنه ينفذ على نفسه. قيد بقوله بماله عليه لأنه لو كفل بما ذاب لك على فلان أو بما ثبت فأقر المطلوب بمال لزم الكفيل لأن الثبوت حصل بقوله وذاب بمعنى حصل وقد حصل بإقراره بخلاف الكفالة بمالك عليه فإنها بالدين القائم في الحال وما ذاب ونحوه الكفالة بما سيجب والوجوب ثبت بإقراره وخرج أيضاً ما إذا كفل بما قضى لك عليه فلا يلزمه إلا بقضاء القاضي ومثل مالك عليه ما أقر لك به أمس، فلو قال المطلوب أقررت له بألف أمس لم يلزم الكفيل لأنه قبل مالاً واجباً عليه لا ما لا يجب عليه في الحال ولم يثبت أنه واجب عليه، فلو قال ما أقر به فأقر به للحال لزمه ولو قامت بينة أنه أقر له قبل الكفالة بالمال لم يزمه لأنه لم يقل ما كان أقر لك، ولو أبى المطلوب اليمين فألزمه القاضي اليمين فنكل لم يلزم الكفيل لأن النكول ليس بإقرار بل بذل.
His saying: (If he becomes surety for a debt owed to him and he proves it with evidence, then it is binding upon him because what is established by evidence is like what is seen with the eye, and the claimant's statement is not proof against him just as it is not proof against the principal because he is a claimant. His saying: (Otherwise, the surety is believed in what he acknowledges with his oath, and the statement of the principal does not take effect against the surety) i.e., if he does not provide evidence, then the say is with the surety in what he acknowledges, with his oath, regarding the negation of knowledge, not a definitive denial as in al-Iydah. The statement of the principal is not proof against him because it is an acknowledgment against another, and that is the meaning of his saying 'does not take effect'. Al-Ayni said with emphasis. He restricted by his saying 'against the surety' because it takes effect against himself. He restricted by his saying 'for a debt owed to him' because if he became surety for what has accrued to you from so-and-so or for what is established, and the principal acknowledged a debt, then it is binding on the surety because the establishment occurred by his saying, and 'accrued' means 'occurred', and it has occurred by his acknowledgment, unlike the suretyship for a debt owed to you because it is for a debt existing at the present. And what is like 'accrued' is suretyship for what will become obligatory, and the obligation is established by his acknowledgment. Also excluded is the case where he became surety for what a judge ruled against him, then it is only binding by the judge's ruling. And similar to 'for a debt owed to you' is 'what he acknowledged to you yesterday'. So if the principal said, 'I acknowledged to him a thousand yesterday,' it is not binding on the surety because he undertook a debt that was obligatory on him, not what is not obligatory at present, and it is not established that it is obligatory on him. So if he said 'what he acknowledged,' and he acknowledged it at present, it is binding on him. And if evidence is established that he acknowledged to him before the suretyship, it is not binding on him because he did not say 'what he had acknowledged to you'. And if the principal refused the oath and the judge imposed an oath on him and he declined, it is not binding on the surety because declining is not an acknowledgment but a surrender.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains two scenarios regarding the surety's liability. First, if the surety (kafil) provides clear evidence (bayyinah) that the principal (maktuul 'anhu) owes the debt, then the surety is bound to pay. The reason given is the principle that 'what is established by evidence is like what is seen with one's own eyes.' This is a rational proof (dalil 'aqli) based on the evidentiary strength of testimony. The claimant's (the creditor's) statement alone is not proof against the surety, just as it is not proof against the original debtor. Second, if the surety does not have evidence, then his own acknowledgment (iqrar) is taken as binding on him, and he must swear an oath that he has no knowledge of the debt (not a definitive denial). The principal's (maktuul 'anhu) acknowledgment or denial does not affect the surety because it is an acknowledgment against someone else (iqrar 'ala al-ghayr). The commentary then gives detailed examples: if the surety uses wording like 'what has accrued (dhab)' or 'what is established,' and the principal acknowledges, then the surety is liable because the debt is considered to have existed at the time of suretyship. But if the wording is 'what you have against him' (ma laka 'alayh), it refers to a current debt only. The ruling also covers cases where the principal refuses an oath; his refusal (nukul) is not equivalent to an acknowledgment, so it does not bind the surety. This lesson establishes that in Hanafi fiqh, the surety's liability is determined by his own actions and statements, not by the principal's, due to the principle that one person's acknowledgment does not bind another.

Key Terms
كفيل (kafil): The surety or guarantor who undertakes to pay a debt owed by another (the principal).
مطلوب (matlub): The original debtor (principal) for whom the surety is given.
إقرار (iqrar): A voluntary acknowledgment or confession of a right or debt, which is binding on the person making it.
بينة (bayyinah): Clear evidence, typically the testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females in financial matters.
يمين على نفي العلم (yamin 'ala nafi al-'ilm): An oath that one has no knowledge of the claim, as opposed to a definitive denial (yamin 'ala al-batat).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a surety (kafil) is sued for a debt he guaranteed, and the principal (maktuul 'anhu) denies the debt, but the surety acknowledges it, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The surety is not liable because the principal denied the debt.
B) The surety is liable only if he can produce two witnesses.
C) The surety is believed with his oath, and his acknowledgment binds him regardless of the principal's denial.
D) The principal's denial frees the surety from liability.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the surety's own acknowledgment is binding on him, and the principal's denial does not affect the surety's liability, due to the principle that one person's iqrar does not bind another.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
10 min
Conditions for Surety's Recourse Against Principal Debtor
Ruling with proof – conditions for surety's recourse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a surety (kafil) has the right to recover (ruju') from the principal debtor (asil) in Hanafi fiqh, and apply these conditions to different scenarios.

Core Idea
A surety recovers from the principal only if he acted at the principal's order; without order, it is a voluntary payment with no recourse.

In Hanafi fiqh, the right of a surety to recover amounts paid from the principal debtor is not automatic. The key determinant is whether the surety acted at the principal's order. This lesson unpacks the concept of 'order' (amr), its explicit and constructive forms, and applies the ruling to various real-life scenarios, including the special case of the regular business partner (khalit) and the intra-madhhab dispute with Imam Zufar.

Guided Path
1
State the Core Ruling
Identify the ruling: a surety who pays a debt recovers from the principal if the suretyship was at the principal's order; otherwise, he does not. This is the foundational principle.
The ruling derives from the idea that paying by order is akin to agency, while paying without order is a voluntary donation.
2
Define 'Order' (Amr)
Explain what constitutes a valid order. The passage clarifies that explicit wording like 'on my behalf' is sufficient but not strictly necessary. Constructive order (hukmi) also suffices, e.g., when a father stands surety for his minor son's dowry due to guardianship.
The khalit (regular business partner) is deemed to have an implied order by custom, allowing recourse even without explicit words.
3
Identify Exceptions and Limitations
The order must be from someone whose order is valid. Thus, no recourse if the principal is a minor or a restricted slave (except after emancipation). Also, the surety recovers what he guaranteed, not necessarily what he paid if there is a difference in quality or amount.
Settlements and partial payments are treated specially: the surety recovers the settlement amount, not the original debt.
4
Analyze the Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Zufar disagrees with Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the case where the surety initially denies the suretyship but is later proven. Zufar denies recourse; the majority grants it. Understand the reasoning on both sides.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
5
Apply to Scenarios
Test your understanding by applying the rules to various scenarios: explicit order, constructive order, no order, minor principal, settlement, etc. Use the practice tasks for structured application.
This step consolidates the lesson by moving from theory to practical application.

His statement: (If he stood surety by his order, he recovers what he paid because he paid his debt by his order. The meaning of 'order' is that his speech includes the word 'on my behalf' such as saying 'stand surety on my behalf' or 'guarantee on my behalf for so-and-so'. If he said 'Guarantee the thousand that so-and-so owes me' he does not recover from him upon payment because it is possible the intention was for him to recover or to seek gratuitous payment, so the money is not binding. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And there is an exception from that in al-Khaniyya: If he said 'Pay him every day a dirham on my credit provided that is on me,' and he paid every day until a large sum accumulated, everything is on the surety. End. And from this it is known that the word 'on my behalf' is not a condition, rather it or what takes its place, namely 'provided that is on me,' and likewise the regular business partner (khalit) recovers by consensus even if he did not say 'on my behalf.' The khalit is the one with whom a man customarily deals, takes from, places dirhams with, and seeks credit. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. He made 'order' absolute, so it includes actual order as we gave as an example, and constructive order as if a father stood surety for his young son for his wife's dowry, then the father died and took from his estate; the heirs have recourse in the son's share because it is suretyship by the young child's order constructively due to the existence of guardianship. Unlike if the father paid himself and did not have witnesses, then he has no recourse because it is possible he paid gratuitously as is customary. Unlike if he had witnesses, because the explicit overrides the implicit. Thus in Sharh al-Majma' by the author concerning dowry. Among constructive orders is what is in Talkhis al-Jami' al-Kabir: If the surety denied the suretyship after being sued, then the plaintiff proved the order, the judge ruled against the surety, and he paid, then he recovers from the debtor, even though this contradicts his being rendered a denier by law through the judgment. Zufar said: He has no recourse because he acknowledged that he has no right when he denied it. End. And the statement of the defendant 'Guarantee on my behalf for so-and-so' is an acknowledgment of the debt as in al-Khaniyya. He made absolute in his statement 'stood surety by his order' and it is restricted to one whose order is valid. So there is no recourse against a minor or a restricted slave if their surety paid by their order because their order is not valid, but he does recover from the slave after his emancipation. As for the minor, he has no recourse ever. If the surety stood surety with the permission of his guardian as in al-Mabsut, unlike the case of the permitted slave in both because his order is valid even if he is not qualified for it. He made absolute in his statement 'what he paid' and it is restricted to paying what he guaranteed. But if he paid something else, like if the guaranteed item was good and he paid poor, or vice versa, then his recourse is for what he guaranteed, not what he paid, because he owns the debt by payment, so he takes the place of the creditor, as if the surety owned it by gift or inheritance. And it is not objected that this is transferring a debt from someone who does not owe it, because we transfer the debt to him by virtue of the gift out of necessity, and he can transfer it by hawala or by making one debt into two debts. Unlike the one ordered to pay a debt, who recovers what he paid if he paid something worse than the debt, and if he paid something better he recovers only the debt, because his right of recourse is by payment by his order, so he does not own it if it is given to him as a gift, thus he recovers what he paid as long as he did not violate the order by an increase. His statement 'recovers what he paid' is restricted to when he paid what was obligatory for the principal. If he stood surety for the lessee for rent and paid before it was due, he has no recourse as in Ijarat al-Bazzaziyya. He made absolute 'what he paid', so it includes if the surety settled with the creditor for the guaranteed thousand for five hundred: he recovers the five hundred, not what he guaranteed (the thousand), because it is dropping or remission of part of the debt, so part falls and nothing else is transferred. In Fath al-Qadir from the sale by a non-owner: If he stood surety for the commodity sold on salam and paid it from a lender so that he does not recover its value if it was a garment, because a garment is a fungible in salam transactions, thus in what is made subordinate to it. End. In Rahn of al-Khaniyya: He sold something and took a surety for the price by the buyer's order; the surety paid the price, then the sold item perished with the seller; the surety does not sue the seller and does not recover the price from him; he only sues the buyer, then the buyer recovers from the seller what the surety paid to him. End. His statement: (If he stood surety without his order, he does not recover) because he is a volunteer in paying on his behalf. He made it absolute, so it includes if he stood surety without his order then later ratified it, because the suretyship is binding and executed on him without an order that is not obligating recourse, so it does not change the ruling even if he acknowledged that he owes him a dirham, it is not accepted from him. Thus in al-Nahr. His statement: (al-'Ayni said with tashdid) said in al-Nahr: It is not determined. His statement: (And that he stood surety for him by the order of so-and-so the absent, it is accepted...) is restricted by his saying 'by the order of so-and-so' because without his order it is...
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the ruling of the surety's recourse against the principal debtor. It states the core principle: if the surety stood guarantee with the principal's order (amr), he may recover what he paid; if without order, he cannot recover, as he is a volunteer. The passage then elaborates on the definition of 'order'—typically containing the word 'on my behalf' ('anni). However, an explicit order is not strictly required. The example from al-Khaniyya shows that if the principal says 'pay on my credit provided it is on me,' the surety recovers even without 'anni'. Similarly, the regular business partner (khalit) recovers by consensus even without the specific wording, because custom implies an order. The passage distinguishes actual order (e.g., saying 'stand surety on my behalf') from constructive order (hukmi), such as a father standing surety for his minor son's dowry; since the father has guardianship, the son is deemed to have ordered, and the surety (father) can recover from the son's estate. However, if the father simply paid without witnesses, it is presumed voluntary. Another case: if the surety initially denied the suretyship but later proof established it and the court ordered him to pay, he still recovers from the principal—contradicting his denial—according to Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn; Zufar disagreed, saying the denial is an admission of no right. The passage also addresses the capacity of the principal: the order must be from someone whose order is valid. Thus, if the principal is a minor or a restricted slave, the surety pays at his peril—no recourse against the minor ever, and recourse against the slave only after emancipation. However, a 'permitted slave' (ma'dhun) can give a valid order because he is allowed to trade. As for what the surety pays, he recovers what he guaranteed, not necessarily what he paid if it differs (e.g., paid poor quality for a good debt). If he settles for less, he recovers the settlement amount, not the original debt. If he pays before the debt is due, he cannot recover until it becomes due. The passage also notes that if the surety pays and the sold item perishes with the seller, the surety's recourse is against the buyer (principal) who then claims against the seller. The final lines reiterate that without order, there is no recourse, even if the surety later ratifies the guarantee, because the initial act was voluntary. The mention of 'al-'Ayni' refers to a variant reading of a word.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship/guarantee; a contract where the surety (kafil) assumes liability for a debt or obligation of the principal (asil).
أمر (amr): The order or authorization of the principal to the surety to stand guarantee. This is the key condition for the surety's right of recourse.
رجوع (ruju'): Recourse; the surety's right to claim reimbursement from the principal after paying the debt.
خليط (khalit): A regular business partner or someone with whom one has customary financial dealings, such that his standing surety is deemed to be by implied order.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, which of the following statements is correct regarding a surety's right of recourse (ruju') against the principal debtor?
A) The surety always recovers, regardless of whether he was asked to guarantee.
B) The surety recovers only if the principal used the word 'anni' (on my behalf) in authorizing the suretyship.
C) The surety recovers if he acted at the principal's order, which can be explicit or constructive.
D) The surety recovers only if he pays before the debt is due.

Key Takeaway
A surety's right to recover from the principal debtor depends entirely on whether the suretyship was at the principal's order; without order, the payment is voluntary and non-recoverable.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Recourse of the Guarantor: Payment and Quality
Explains when the guarantor (kafil) has recourse against the principal debtor based on order or lack thereof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on a kafil's recourse when the quality of payment differs and distinguish the kafil from a mere agent (al-ma'mur bi qada' al-dayn).

Core Idea
The kafil recovers what he guaranteed, not what he paid, if the goods differ in quality.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the general rule that a guarantor (kafil) who pays with the debtor's order has recourse for what he actually paid. However, the text introduces a key qualification: if the payment differs in quality from the guaranteed debt, the recourse shifts to the guaranteed amount. This distinction hinges on the kafil's ownership of the debt upon payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its qualification
Start by noting that the matn broadly states 'he has recourse for what he paid'. The commentary restricts this to cases where the payment matches the guarantee. If the quality differs, the rule changes.
This is the first analytical move: spotting the restriction in the commentary.
2
Understand the talil (reasoning)
The kafil becomes owner of the debt by payment, stepping into the creditor's shoes. This ownership is analogized to owning the debt by gift or inheritance. Therefore, he can claim the full guaranteed amount, not just his payment.
The talil is crucial: ownership is the effective cause (illa).
3
Distinguish from al-ma'mur bi qada' al-dayn
A person simply ordered to pay the debt (not a kafil) does not own the debt. His recourse is based solely on the order. He recovers only what he paid (if less than the debt) or the debt amount (if he paid more).
This contrast sharpens the kafil's unique position.
4
Apply restrictions on recourse
The kafil's recourse is valid only if payment was due (not before) and if he did not exceed the order. If he settles via sulh for less, he recovers the lessor amount because the difference is deemed an isqat (abandonment).
These are practical conditions modifying the general rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق في قوله «بما أدى» وهو مقيد بأن يؤدي ما ضمن، أما إذا أدى خلافه بأن كان المكفول به جيداً فأدى رديئاً أو بالعكس فإن رجوعه بما ضمن لا بما أدى لكونه ملك الدين بالأداء فنزل منزلة الطالب كما إذا ملكه الكفيل بالهبة أو بالإرث ولا يرد عليه أنه تمليك الدين من غير من عليه الدين لأننا ننقل الدين إليه بمقتضى الهبة للضرورة وله نقله بالحوالة أو بجعل الدين الواحد كدينين بخلاف المأمور بقضاء الدين فإنه يرجع بما أدى إن أدى أردأ من الدين وإن أدى أجود لم يرجع إلا بالدين لأن حق رجوعه إنما بالأداء هو بأمره، ولذا لا يملكه لو وهب له فيرجع بما أدى ما لم يخالف أمره بالزيادة أو وقوله «رجع بما أدى» مقيد بما إذا دفع ما وجب دفعه على الأصيل، فلو كفل عن المستأجر بالأجرة فدفع الكفيل قبل الوجوب لا رجوع له كما في إجارات البزازية. وأطلق فيما أدى فشمل ما إذا صالح الكفيل الطالب عن الألف المكفول بها على خمسمائة فإنه بالخمسمائة يرجع لا بما ضمن وهو الألف لأنه إسقاط أو هو إبراء عن بعض الدين فيسقط البعض ولا ينتقل بجنس آخر يصير ماله إلى الكفيل.
And he said broadly 'with what he paid' and it is restricted to his paying what he guaranteed. As for if he paid differently, such that the guaranteed item was good and he paid defective, or the opposite, then his recourse is for what he guaranteed, not what he paid, because he becomes owner of the debt by payment, so he takes the position of the claimant, just as if the kafil owned it by gift or inheritance. And it is not objected that this is transferring a debt from someone other than the debtor, because we transfer the debt to him by virtue of the gift out of necessity, and he can transfer it by hawala or by making one debt into two. This is contrary to the one ordered to pay the debt (al-ma'mur bi qada' al-dayn); he has recourse for what he paid if he paid something inferior to the debt, and if he paid something superior he only has recourse for the debt, because his right of recourse is by payment based on the order. Hence, if it is gifted to him, he does not own it, so he has recourse for what he paid as long as he does not exceed the order by increase. And his statement 'he has recourse for what he paid' is restricted to when he pays what was obligatory upon the principal. So if he guarantees for the hirer (musta'jir) the rent and pays before it is due, he has no recourse, as in Ijarat al-Bazzaziyyah. And he broadly stated 'what he paid', so it includes if the kafil compromises with the claimant for five hundred regarding the guaranteed thousand: he has recourse for the five hundred, not for what he guaranteed (the thousand), because it is an abandonment (isqat) or remission (ibra') of part of the debt, so part falls away and does not transfer in another type to become the kafil's property.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling regarding what the kafil recovers when he pays. The general rule is that a kafil who pays with the debtor's order (bi amrihi) has recourse for what he actually paid (ma adda). However, the text clarifies that the default is recourse for what he guaranteed (ma damina) if the quality differs: if the debt was of good quality and the kafil pays poor quality, or vice versa, his recourse is for the guaranteed amount, not the paid amount. This is because by paying, he becomes the owner of the debt and steps into the shoes of the creditor. He can then deal with that debt like any other debt (gift, hawala). The exception is al-ma'mur bi qada' al-dayn (the person ordered to pay a debt, who is not a kafil but a mere agent): he recovers what he paid if he pays less quality, but if he pays better quality, he only recovers the debt amount. The reason is that his right is based on the order alone, not ownership of the debt. The kafil's recourse is also restricted: if he pays before the debt is due (e.g., rent before the term), he has no recourse. If he settles the debt via sulh (compromise) for less than the full amount, he only recovers what he paid (the lesser sum), because the difference is deemed an isqat (abandonment) that does not transfer to him.

Key Terms
الرجوع بما ضمن (ar-ruju' bi ma damina): The kafil's right to recover from the principal debtor the amount he guaranteed, not necessarily the amount he actually paid if the quality differs.
المأمور بقضاء الدين (al-ma'mur bi qada' al-dayn): A person ordered to pay a debt on behalf of the debtor, distinguishing him from a kafil (surety). He has recourse for what he paid if he paid less quality, but only for the debt if he paid better.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A kafil guarantees a debt of 1000 dirhams of good quality. He pays the creditor 800 dirhams of poor quality in settlement. According to the Hanafi school, how much can he recover from the debtor?
A) 800 dirhams, because that is what he paid.
B) 1000 dirhams, because he guaranteed that amount.
C) Nothing, because he did not pay the exact debt.
D) 900 dirhams, the average between the two.

Key Takeaway
The kafil's recourse is for the guaranteed amount (ma damina) when the payment differs in quality; this is due to his ownership of the debt upon payment.

Legal Stratagem for Surety's Right of Recourse
Legal stratagem for preserving surety's right of recourse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi legal stratagem for a surety to pay a debt with recourse and apply it to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
A surety can pay the debt and still have recourse through a gift-then-agency stratagem.

This lesson explores a scenario from the Hanafi legal stratagems (hiyal) where a surety (kafil) is pressured to pay the debt of the principal debtor. Without proper arrangement, such payment would be considered a voluntary payment (mutatawwi') with no right of recourse. The text from al-Walwalijiyya provides a clever three-step method to preserve the surety's right to recover from the debtor. Understanding this stratagem reveals how Hanafi law balances contractual obligations with fairness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Recognize the parties: surety (kafil), creditor (talib), debtor (makful anhu). The surety cannot produce the person guaranteed, and the creditor demands payment of the debt.
2
State the desired outcome
The surety wants to pay the debt but still have the right to claim reimbursement from the debtor (recourse). Direct payment would make him a volunteer.
3
Outline the stratagem steps
Step 1: Surety pays the debt to the creditor. Step 2: Creditor gifts property of equal value to the surety. Step 3: Creditor appoints the surety as agent (wakil) to take possession of that gift.
This three-step process is the core of the hiyal.
4
Explain the legal effect
After the gift and agency, the surety now owns the property and has the right to demand it from the debtor (through the agency). In practice, he can take possession and thus recover his payment.
5
Clarify alternatives and prohibitions
Direct payment without the stratagem makes the surety a volunteer (mutatawwi') with no recourse. Paying with a condition of no recourse is invalid.
These alternatives preserve the general rule that a volunteer cannot claim back.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي آخر الولوالجية من الحيل : رجل كفل بنفس رجل ولم يقدر على تسليمه فقال له الطالب ادفع إلي مالي على المكفول عنه حتى تبرأ عن الكفالة فأراد أن يؤديه على وجه يكون له حق الرجوع على المطلوب، فالحيلة في ذلك أن يدفع الدين إلى الطالب ويهب الطالب مال المطلوب ويوكله بقبضه فيكون له حق المطالبة، فإذا قبضه يكون له حق الرجوع لأنه لو دفع إليه المال بغير هذه الحيلة يكون متطوعاً، ولو أدى بشرط أن لا يرجع لا يجوز ا هـ .
And at the end of al-Walwalijiyya, from the legal stratagems: A man stood surety for the person of a man and was unable to hand him over. The claimant said to him: 'Pay me my money owed by the guaranteed person so that you are released from the suretyship.' He wanted to pay it in a way that he would have the right of recourse against the debtor. The stratagem for that is that he pays the debt to the claimant, and the claimant gifts the debtor's property to him, and appoints him as agent to take possession of it, so he has the right to demand it. When he takes possession of it, he has the right of recourse. Because if he paid the money to him without this stratagem, he would be a volunteer. And if he paid on condition that he would not have recourse, it is not permissible. End.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage presents a legal stratagem (hiyah) from the Hanafi tradition. The scenario involves a surety (kafil bil-nafs) who is unable to produce the person he guaranteed. The creditor demands payment from the surety. The surety wants to pay but still retain the right to recover from the principal debtor. Direct payment without the stratagem would make him a volunteer (mutatawwi') with no right of recourse. The hiyal involves a three-step process: (1) The surety pays the debt to the creditor, (2) The creditor then gifts the equivalent of the principal's property to the surety, and (3) The creditor appoints the surety as his agent (wakil) to take possession of that same gift. Through this mechanism, the surety acquires ownership of the gift in exchange for his payment, effectively securing his right to demand reimbursement from the principal. The passage also notes that if the payment is made with a condition of no recourse, it is invalid. The source is al-Walwalijiyya, a collection of legal stratagems.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; a contract guaranteeing the person or debt of another.
حيلة (hiyah): A permissible legal stratagem to achieve a lawful objective while avoiding prohibited consequences.
متطوع (mutatawwi'): A volunteer; one who pays a debt without being legally obligated and without right of recourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the hiyal in this passage?
A) To allow the surety to avoid payment entirely
B) To allow the surety to pay and still have recourse against the debtor
C) To allow the creditor to receive payment twice
D) To release the surety without any payment

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi hiyal allows a surety to pay the debt and still retain recourse by receiving a gift from the creditor and acting as his agent to take possession.

The Condition of Command and the Meaning of 'Alayya in Suretyship
Sharh explaining the condition of command and the effect of the word 'alayya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for a surety's recourse in the Hanafi school and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on the effect of the word 'alayya.

Core Idea
The surety's right of recourse depends on the debtor's command; 'alayya grants recourse in the relied-upon view.

In suretyship (kafalah), the surety who pays the debt generally has the right to seek reimbursement from the principal debtor. However, this right is not automatic; it depends on whether the surety acted on the debtor's command (amr) and on the specific wording used in the guarantee. This lesson examines the condition of 'amr' and a key dispute among the Hanafi imams regarding the word 'alayya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the surety paying the debt. The ruling is whether he has recourse to the debtor. The condition is the debtor's command or its equivalent (e.g., ratification in the same session).
The command must be from the debtor; ratification substitutes if done in the same session.
2
Locate the dalil
The original narration (riwayat al-asl) states that 'alayya is like 'anni, giving recourse. Abu Hanifa in al-Mujarrad holds that 'alayya does not give recourse.
The dalil is a difference in interpretation of the word 'alayya.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The original narration treats 'alayya as an acknowledgment of debt and authorization to pay, while Abu Hanifa treats it as a mere guarantee without authorization.
The key is whether the wording implies a command or just a description of obligation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Intra-Hanafi: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad vs Abu Yusuf. Abu Yusuf's view is supported by the original narration.
The disagreement is about the legal effect of the word 'alayya.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the original narration, which grants recourse. This is the view of Abu Yusuf and is adopted in the school.
The author seems to lean towards Abu Hasan, but the mufta bihi is the original narration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ومعنى الأمر أن يشتمل الخ) الظاهر أن هذا شرط فيما إذا كانت بصيغة الأمر من المطلوب وإلا فسيأتي في القولة الآتية أنه لو كفل بغير أمره ثم أجازها في المجلس تصير موجبة للرجوع بقيد آخر سنذكره تأمل. قوله: (وبه علم أن لیست شرطاً الخ) قال في النهر : وفيها أي في الخانية علي كعني فلو قال اكفل لفلان بألف درهم علي أو انقده ألف درهم علي أو اضمن له الألف التي علي أو اقضه ماله علي ونحو ذلك بما دفع في رواية الأصل. وعن أبي حنيفة في المجرد إذا قال لفلان اضمن لفلان الألف التي له علي فضمنها وأدى إليه لا يرجع اهـ. وتأمله ما ذكره المؤلف عن فتح القدير نعم ذكر في الفتح بعدما نقله المؤلف عنه وهذا قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد فلعل رواية الأصل قول أبي يوسف تأمل.
His statement: 'and the meaning of command is that it includes [the condition]' – apparently this condition applies when the command is in the form of an order from the debtor. Otherwise, it will come in the following statement that if he became a surety without his order and then he ratified it in the session, it becomes a cause for recourse with another condition we will mention. Reflect. His statement: 'and by this it is known that it is not a condition' – al-Nahr said: In al-Khaniyya, 'alayya is like 'anni. So if he said 'Become surety for so-and-so for one thousand dirhams on my account' or 'Pay him one thousand on my account' or 'Guarantee to him the thousand that he has against me' or 'Pay him his due that I owe' and similar, based on what is paid in the original narration. And from Abu Hanifa in al-Mujarrad: if he said to someone 'Guarantee to so-and-so the thousand that he has against me', and he guaranteed and paid, he does not have recourse. End. Reflect on what the author mentioned from Fath al-Qadir. Yes, it is mentioned in al-Fath after what the author quoted from him, and this is the statement of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Perhaps the original narration is the statement of Abu Yusuf. Reflect.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage from the sharh discusses the condition of 'amr' (command). The commentator notes that the condition that the command be from the principal is specifically when the command is given by the debtor. If the surety acts without command and the principal ratifies in the same session, it becomes a basis for recourse with a different condition. The commentator also discusses a nuance regarding the word 'alayya: in al-Khaniyya, it is equivalent to 'anni, so the payer has recourse. However, in al-Mujarrad, Abu Hanifa holds that if someone says 'Guarantee the thousand that he has against me' and the guarantor pays, he does not have recourse. The commentator suggests that the ruling in the original narration (zahir al-riwaya) may be the view of Abu Yusuf, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad have a different view. This shows an intra-Hanafi disagreement on the interpretation of 'alayya.

Key Terms
أمر (amr): Command from the principal debtor to the surety to pay, which is a condition for the surety's right of recourse.
مجرد (mujarrad): A book by Abu Hanifa detailing his sole opinions, as distinct from the zahir al-riwaya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is like 'anni' and gives recourse
B) It does not give recourse unless the payer is a khalit
C) It gives recourse only if the payer specifies 'anni'
D) It prohibits any recourse

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the surety's right of recourse depends on the debtor's command or its equivalent; the word 'alayya, in the relied-upon view, grants recourse.

Paying with Base Coins: Khalit vs Kafil and Hawil
Distinction between khalit (mixed partner) and kafil/hawil regarding payment in base coins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal consequences for a khalit versus a kafil/hawil when paying with base coins and explain the underlying legal reasoning.

Core Idea
A khalit recovers what he paid; a kafil/hawil recovers good coins because they own the debt.

This passage from al-Walwalijiyya clarifies a nuanced distinction in Hanafi fiqh regarding payment with debased coins. The key is the nature of the payer's relationship to the debtor: agency (khalit) vs. ownership of the debt (kafil/hawil). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of suretyship and hawala.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three roles
Note the three types of payers: khalit (mixed partner), kafil (surety), and hawil (transferee). The khalit is not a surety but an agent commanded to pay.
2
State the scenario
The debt is in good coins (jiyad), but the payer pays with base coins (zuyuf) or counterfeit (nubharajah).
3
Recall the rulings
For the khalit: he recovers only the base coins he paid. For the kafil/hawil: they recover good coins from the principal debtor.
This is the core distinction.
4
Analyze the reasoning (talil)
The difference is that the khalit is commanded to pay on behalf of the principal, so his payment is treated as a loan; he recovers what he lent. The kafil/hawil own the debt in the principal's liability; when they pay base coins, they are effectively purchasing the good coins from the debtor, so they recover good coins.
The key is ownership of the debt vs. agency.
5
Confirm the implication
Thus, the khalit is not a surety but a mere agent. This distinction affects how they recover from the debtor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وأطلق في قوله بما أدى (الخ قال في الولوالجية: ولو دفع الخليط زيوفاً أو نبهرجة لم يرجع على صاحب الأصل إلا بهما، ولو أدى الكفيل أو الحويل زيوفاً والدين جياد رجع على المكفول عنه بالجياد وكذا الحويل. والفرق أن الخليط مأمور بقضاء الدين عن الآمر فيرجع بحكم الإقراض، وأما الكفيل والحويل إنما يرجعان من حيث إنهما يملكان ما في ذمتهما، ويجوز أن يملك الجياد بالزيوف لأنها تصلح بدلاً عنها فكان لهما أن يرجعا بما ملكا في ذمتهما .اهـ. فعلم أن الخليط غير كفيل بل مأمور
His statement: 'And he made it absolute in his statement: with what he paid' – al-Walwalijiyya said: If the khalit (mixed partner) paid base coins or counterfeit coins, he does not recover from the principal except those same coins. If the surety (kafil) or the transferee (hawil) paid base coins while the debt was of good coins, he recovers from the guaranteed person in good coins, and similarly the hawil. The difference is that the khalit is commanded to pay the debt on behalf of the commander, so he recovers by virtue of a loan. As for the surety and the hawil, they only recover from the standpoint that they own what is in the principal's liability, and it is permissible to own good coins in exchange for base coins because they are suitable as substitutes. So they can recover what they have owned in his liability. End. Thus it is known that the khalit is not a surety but is a commanded person.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage from al-Walwalijiyya distinguishes between three types of payers: khalit (mixed business partner), kafil (surety), and hawil (transferee in hawala). When the debt is of good coins but the payer pays with base (debased) coins, the khalit can only recover the same base coins from the principal, because he is acting as an agent paying on behalf of the commander; his payment is essentially a loan to the commander, so he recovers what he paid. However, the kafil and hawil own the debt in the principal's liability. When they pay base coins, they are effectively purchasing the good coins from the principal, and since base coins can substitute for good ones, they can recover good coins from the principal. This ruling highlights the different legal relationships: the khalit is a mere agent, while the kafil and hawil are subrogated to the creditor's rights.

Key Terms
زيوف (zuyuf): Debased or base coins, of lesser value than good coins.
نبهرجة (nubharajah): Counterfeit or very low quality coins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only the same base coins
B) Good coins equivalent to the debt
C) Nothing, because he paid voluntarily
D) Half of the debt in good coins

Key Takeaway
The legal relationship (agency vs. ownership of debt) determines whether a payer who uses base coins can recover good coins from the debtor.

Kafalah — Surety

Surety Cannot Demand Payment Before Paying
Ruling that the surety cannot demand money from the principal before paying the creditor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that a surety (kafil) cannot demand payment from the principal (asil) before paying the creditor, and distinguish this from the case of an agent for purchase using the concept of constructive exchange (mubadalah hukmiyyah).

Core Idea
The surety's right of recourse arises only after payment; no constructive exchange exists beforehand.

This lesson delves into a specific rule in Hanafi suretyship (kafalah): the surety cannot demand the money from the principal before he himself pays the creditor. The reason lies in the nature of ownership — the surety only owns the debt by paying it. However, the principal may voluntarily give a pledge (rahn) as security even before payment. We will contrast this with an agent for purchase, who can demand payment upfront due to a constructive exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
State the core ruling: the surety cannot demand payment from the principal before paying the creditor. The condition is that the surety has not yet paid.
2
Understand the reason: ownership by payment
The surety only owns the debt by paying it. Before payment, he has no ownership and thus no right to demand.
This is based on the principle that recourse follows ownership.
3
Compare with the agent for purchase
An agent for purchase (wakil bi'l-shira) can demand payment from the principal before paying the seller because of the constructive exchange (mubadalah hukmiyyah) between them — they are treated as having exchanged conceptually.
This distinction highlights the difference in legal relationships.
4
Note the exception: pledge from principal is permissible
If the principal voluntarily gives a pledge to the surety before payment, it is permissible and valid. This is not a right but a voluntary act.
The surety cannot compel the principal to give a pledge.
5
Analyze the qiyas reasoning
The ruling is derived by reasoning (talil): the surety's case is compared to the agent, but the illa (effective cause) of constructive exchange is absent in suretyship.
This qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) is used to justify the difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولا يطالب الكفيل بالمال قبل أن يؤدي عنه لأنه إنما التزم المطالبة وإنما يتملك الدين بالأداء فلا يرجع قبل التملك. فإن قلت : هل للكفيل أخذ الرهن من الأصيل قبل أن يؤدي عنه ؟ قلت : نعم. قال في الخانية : كفل عن رجل بمال ثم إن المكفول عنه أعطى الكفيل رهناً ذكر في الأصل أنه لو كفل بمال مؤجل على الأصيل فأعطاه المكفول عنه رهناً بذلك جاز ا هـ . قيد بالكفيل لأن الوكيل بالشراء له الرجوع على الموكل قبل الأداء لما بينهما من المبادلة الحكمية حتى تحالفا لو اختلفا في مقدار الثمن وللوكيل حبس المبيع إلى استيفاء الثمن
His statement: 'And the surety is not demanded for the money before he pays on his behalf' because he only undertook the demand, and he only owns the debt by paying, so he does not have recourse before ownership. If you say: Does the surety have the right to take a pledge from the principal before he pays? I say: Yes. It is mentioned in al-Khaniyya: He became surety for a man for some money, then the guaranteed person gave the surety a pledge. It is mentioned in al-Asl that if he became surety for a deferred debt on the principal, and the guaranteed person gave him a pledge against that, it is permissible. End. This is restricted to the surety because the agent for purchase has recourse against the principal before payment due to the constructive exchange between them, so much so that they would take an oath if they disputed the price, and the agent has the right to retain the purchased item until receiving the price.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage explains why a surety cannot demand payment from the principal before he himself pays the creditor. The reason is that the surety's right of recourse only arises after he pays and thereby owns the debt. Before payment, he has no ownership, so he cannot demand. However, an exception is that the principal may voluntarily give a pledge (rahn) to the surety as security, and this is permissible even before payment. The passage contrasts with an agent for purchase (wakil bi'l-shira), who can demand payment from the principal before paying because there is a constructive exchange (mubadalah hukmiyyah) between them; the agent is seen as having already paid conceptually. This distinction highlights the different nature of agency in purchase versus suretyship.

Key Terms
مبادلة حكمية (mubadalah hukmiyyah): Constructive exchange; a legal fiction where the parties are treated as having exchanged even before actual payment.
كفيل (kafil): Surety; a person who guarantees a debt or obligation of another.
أصيل (asil): Principal; the original debtor or person whose obligation is guaranteed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can the surety not demand payment from the principal before he pays the creditor?
A) Because the surety has no right of recourse at all
B) Because the surety only owns the debt after paying
C) Because the principal is always insolvent
D) Because the creditor has priority

Key Takeaway
A surety's right to claim from the principal only arises after payment; before that, the principal may voluntarily give a pledge but cannot be compelled.

Mulazamah in Kafalah: Conditions for Tracking the Principal
Ruling with conditions from hanafi fatwa works

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions under which a surety may track the principal debtor and explain the rationale behind each.

Core Idea
A surety can track the principal only if the suretyship was by order, the debt is due on both, and no counterclaim exists.

When a creditor pursues a surety, the surety may wish to turn to the principal debtor to be relieved. However, Hanafi jurists have restricted this right of mulazamah to prevent injustice. In this lesson, we examine the precise conditions as derived from the commentaries of al-Bazzaziyya and al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal nature
The act is the surety's right to track (mulazamah) the principal when the creditor tracks him. The ruling is that it is permissible only under specific conditions.
This is a right, not an obligation.
2
State the primary condition: suretyship by order
The suretyship must have been by the principal's order (bi amrih). If the surety volunteered, he cannot track because he entered the contract voluntarily without the principal's request.
The rationale: the principal brought the surety in, so the surety can return to him.
3
State the second condition: debt due on both
The debt must be mature (hal) on both the principal and the surety equally. If the debt is deferred on the principal, the surety cannot accelerate it.
The text says 'when the debt is due on the principal like on the surety'.
4
State the third condition: no counterclaim
The surety must not be owed a similar debt by the principal. If he is, the tracking right is blocked because the debts could offset.
From al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: 'when the surety is not owed a similar debt'.
5
Summarize the practical effect
If all conditions are met, the surety can have the principal imprisoned; if not, he cannot track him.
The creditor can also imprison both when tracking is allowed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن لوزم لازمه) أي إن لازم الكفيل الطالب لازم الأصيل ليخلصه من هذه العهدة. وأشار إلى أنه لو حبس الكفيل حبس المطلوب، وقدمنا عن البزازية أنه مقيد بما إذا كانت الكفالة بأمره وإلا فلا يلازم الأصيل لأنه ما أدخله ليخلصه، وقدمنا أن للطالب حبسهما . وينبغي أن يقيد أيضاً بما إذا كان المال حالاً على الأصيل كالكفيل وإلا فليس له ،ملازمته وسيأتي بيان الحلول على الكفيل وحده. في السراج الوهاج أيضاً بما إذا لم يكن على الكفيل للمطلوب دين مثله وإلا فلا يلازمه. وأشار المؤلف إلى أن المحال عليه إذا لوزم وكانت الحوالة بأمر المحيل كان له أن يلازم المحيل ليخلصه عن ملازمة المحال ،له وإذا حبسه كان له أن يحبسه إلا أن يكون للمحيل على المحال مثله وقد احتال بماله عليه مقيداً فليس للمحال عليه أن يلازم المحيل إذا لوزم ولا يحبسه إذا حبس ا هـ .
His statement: 'For if he is tracked, he tracks' – meaning if the creditor tracks the surety, the surety tracks the principal to free him from this liability. He indicated that if the surety is imprisoned, the principal is imprisoned. We have already mentioned from al-Bazzaziyya that this is restricted to when the suretyship was by his order; otherwise he does not track the principal because he did not bring him in to free him. And we have already mentioned that the creditor may imprison both. It should also be restricted to when the debt is due on the principal like on the surety; otherwise he does not have tracking. The explanation of maturity on the surety alone will come. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj also [it is restricted] to when the surety is not owed a similar debt by the principal; otherwise he does not track him. And the author indicated that the transferee (muhal 'alayh) in hawala, if he is tracked and the hawala was by order of the transferor (muhil), he may track the transferor to free him from the tracking of the transferee, and if he is imprisoned he may imprison him, except if the transferor has a similar debt against the transferee and he has taken hawala on that debt restrictively, then the transferee may not track the transferor nor imprison him if imprisoned.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of mulazamah (tracking) in suretyship and hawala. When the creditor tracks (lazama) the surety, the surety can in turn track the principal debtor to relieve himself. This tracking includes the right to have the principal imprisoned. However, this right is conditional: (1) The suretyship must have been by the principal's order; (2) The debt must be due on the principal as well (i.e., mature); (3) The surety must not be owed a similar debt by the principal (because then tracking would be to recover a debt that is offset). The passage also applies similar rules to hawala: the transferee (muhal 'alayh) can track the transferor (muhil) if the hawala was by the transferor's order, unless the transferor has a claim against the transferee that offsets the debt. This ensures that the tracking right is not abused.

Key Terms
ملازمة (mulazamah): The right of a surety to pursue the principal debtor when the creditor pursues the surety, including imprisonment.
حلول (hulul): Maturity of debt; the condition that the debt becomes due and payable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under which condition does the surety have the right to track the principal when tracked by the creditor?
A) The suretyship was without the principal's order
B) The debt is deferred on the surety only
C) The suretyship was by the principal's order and the debt is due on both
D) The surety has a similar debt against the principal

Key Takeaway
The surety's right to track the principal is conditional: it only applies when the suretyship was by order, the debt is due on both, and no counterclaim exists.

Mulazamah in Hawala: Tracking the Transferor
Extension of mulazamah rule to hawala with exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule of mulazamah in hawala and identify the exception when the transferor has a claim against the transferee.

Core Idea
In hawala, the transferee can track the transferor only if the hawala was by order, and cannot track if the transferor has a counterclaim.

The principle of tracking (mulazamah) also applies to hawala (transfer of debt). When the creditor (muhal) tracks the transferee (muhal 'alayh), the transferee may in turn track the transferor (muhil) to relieve himself. However, Hanafi jurists note an important exception: if the transferor has a claim against the transferee, the tracking right is suspended.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the transferee's right to track the transferor when the creditor tracks him. The ruling: it is permissible if the hawala was by order.
This parallels the kafalah rule.
2
State the primary condition: hawala by order
The transferee can track the transferor only if the hawala was by the transferor's order (bi amrih). If not, no tracking.
The text says 'if the hawala was by order of the transferor'.
3
State the exception: existence of a counterclaim
If the transferor has a debt against the transferee equal to the transferred amount and the hawala was on that debt restrictively, the transferee cannot track the transferor.
This prevents circular litigation.
4
Apply the rule to imprisonment
If the transferee is imprisoned, he can imprison the transferor unless the exception applies.
The text extends tracking to imprisonment as well.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن لوزم لازمه) أي إن لازم الكفيل الطالب لازم الأصيل ليخلصه من هذه العهدة. وأشار إلى أنه لو حبس الكفيل حبس المطلوب، وقدمنا عن البزازية أنه مقيد بما إذا كانت الكفالة بأمره وإلا فلا يلازم الأصيل لأنه ما أدخله ليخلصه، وقدمنا أن للطالب حبسهما . وينبغي أن يقيد أيضاً بما إذا كان المال حالاً على الأصيل كالكفيل وإلا فليس له ،ملازمته وسيأتي بيان الحلول على الكفيل وحده. في السراج الوهاج أيضاً بما إذا لم يكن على الكفيل للمطلوب دين مثله وإلا فلا يلازمه. وأشار المؤلف إلى أن المحال عليه إذا لوزم وكانت الحوالة بأمر المحيل كان له أن يلازم المحيل ليخلصه عن ملازمة المحال ،له وإذا حبسه كان له أن يحبسه إلا أن يكون للمحيل على المحال مثله وقد احتال بماله عليه مقيداً فليس للمحال عليه أن يلازم المحيل إذا لوزم ولا يحبسه إذا حبس ا هـ .
His statement: 'For if he is tracked, he tracks' – meaning if the creditor tracks the surety, the surety tracks the principal to free him from this liability. He indicated that if the surety is imprisoned, the principal is imprisoned. We have already mentioned from al-Bazzaziyya that this is restricted to when the suretyship was by his order; otherwise he does not track the principal because he did not bring him in to free him. And we have already mentioned that the creditor may imprison both. It should also be restricted to when the debt is due on the principal like on the surety; otherwise he does not have tracking. The explanation of maturity on the surety alone will come. Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of mulazamah (tracking) in suretyship and hawala. When the creditor tracks (lazama) the surety, the surety can in turn track the principal debtor to relieve himself. This tracking includes the right to have the principal imprisoned. However, this right is conditional: (1) The suretyship must have been by the principal's order; (2) The debt must be due on the principal as well (i.e., mature); (3) The surety must not be owed a similar debt by the principal (because then tracking would be to recover a debt that is offset). The passage also applies similar rules to hawala: the transferee (muhal 'alayh) can track the transferor (muhil) if the hawala was by the transferor's order, unless the transferor has a claim against the transferee that offsets the debt. This ensures that the tracking right is not abused.

Key Terms
ملازمة (mulazamah): The right of a surety or transferee to pursue the principal or transferor when the creditor pursues them.
حوالة (hawala): Transfer of debt; a contract by which a debt is transferred from one party to another.

Key Terms from Source Passage
