Automatic Discharge of Surety upon Principal's Payment
Short ruling with causal reasoning (talil)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the surety is automatically discharged when the principal debtor pays the debt and explain the underlying reasoning (talil).

Core Idea
The surety is automatically discharged when the principal pays – the debt's extinguishment ends the accessory obligation.

This lesson covers a fundamental principle in Hanafi kafalah: the discharge of the surety (kafil) when the principal debtor (asil) fulfills the debt. The text from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the automatic nature of this discharge and the logical basis behind it. Understanding this rule prevents unjust double payment and clarifies the accessory nature of suretyship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal actors
Identify the principal debtor (asil), the surety (kafil), and the creditor in the kafalah arrangement. The principal is the primary obligor; the surety is the accessory obligor.
2
Identify the triggering event
The key event is the principal's payment (ada') of the debt. This extinguishes the debt obligation.
3
Identify the legal consequence
The consequence is the discharge (bara'ah) of the surety. The surety is released from the guarantee automatically.
4
Analyze the reasoning (talil)
The underlying reasoning is that the debt is extinguished by the principal's payment. Since the surety's obligation is accessory to the principal's, it falls away when the principal's obligation disappears.
This is a causal link: discharge of principal necessitates discharge of surety.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a case where the debtor pays the creditor but the creditor later demands payment from the surety. According to the rule, the surety is discharged and not liable, even if the creditor was unaware of the payment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبرئ بأداء الأصيل أي برئ الكفيل لأن براءة الأصيل توجب براءته لأنه لا بقضاء الدين.
His statement: 'And he is discharged by the performance of the principal' – meaning the surety is discharged because the discharge of the principal necessitates his discharge, because the debt is extinguished.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This short passage explains that when the principal debtor pays the debt, the surety is automatically discharged. The reasoning is that the debt is extinguished by the principal's payment, and since the surety's obligation is accessory to the principal's, it falls away. This is a straightforward application of the principle that the discharge of the principal debtor releases the guarantor.

Key Terms
براءة (bara'ah): Discharge or release from a debt obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the surety become discharged from his guarantee according to this passage?
A) Only when the surety himself pays
B) When the principal debtor pays the debt
C) When the creditor releases the surety
D) After a certain period

Key Takeaway
The surety is automatically discharged when the principal debtor pays the debt, because the debt is extinguished.

Ratification of Unauthorized Suretyship and Recourse
Ruling on ratification in the session

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions (session and order) for a principal's ratification to give the surety recourse, and apply them to a hypothetical scenario.

Core Idea
Recourse from unauthorized suretyship requires ratification in the same session and in correct order.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school about suretyship (kafalah) when the surety acts without the principal's prior authorization. The key question is: when does the principal's subsequent ratification grant the surety the right to seek reimbursement? The answer hinges on the timing and sequence of declarations in the contractual session.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act
The act is an unauthorized suretyship (kafalah bilā amr) – the surety guaranteed the debt without being asked by the principal (the debtor). The principal then ratifies (ijāzah) after the fact.
This is a case of 'al-kafālah bilā amr'.
2
Determine the main ruling
The Hanafi ruling: If the principal ratifies in the same contractual session (majlis al-'aqd), the surety gains the right of recourse against the principal. But there is a condition: the ratification must occur before the creditor's acceptance.
The order matters: principal ratifies first, then creditor accepts.
3
Analyze the consequence of reversed order
If the creditor accepts first, then the principal ratifies, no recourse is established. The passage states: 'if in reverse there is no recourse'.
This shows the session and sequence are both essential.
4
Understand the rationale (talīl)
The ruling is based on the idea that the contract (suretyship) is not fully formed until both the principal's ratification and the creditor's acceptance occur in the same session. The ratification must be part of the contract formation process.
The session (majlis) is a binding context for contract options in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Given a scenario: A surety guarantees a loan without the debtor's order. Later, in the same meeting, the debtor says 'I approve' and then the creditor says 'I accept'. The surety pays. Can the surety claim reimbursement from the debtor?
Answer: Yes, because the order was correct: principal ratified first, then creditor accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (أما إذا أجاز في المجلس فإنها تصير موجبة للرجوع) أي إذا أجازها المطلوب أولاً ثم الطالب وإن بالعكس فلا رجوع كما سيذكره المؤلف عن السراج في شرح قوله و«بلا قبول الطالب في مجلس العقد
His statement: 'But if he ratifies in the session, it becomes a cause for recourse' – meaning if the principal ratifies first and then the creditor, even if in reverse there is no recourse, as the author will mention from al-Siraj in the explanation of his statement: 'and without the acceptance of the creditor in the contract session.'
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where a surety acted without the principal's order, and the principal subsequently ratifies the action. The ruling is that if the ratification occurs in the same session (majlis) as the contract, the surety gains the right of recourse against the principal. However, the order matters: the principal must ratify first, then the creditor accepts. If the creditor accepts first and then the principal ratifies, there is no recourse. This demonstrates the importance of sequence in forming the contract and the role of the session of contract.

Key Terms
إجازة (ijazah): Ratification; approval of a contract after the fact, which can validate it and create rights.
مجلس العقد (majlis al-'aqd): The session of contract; the time and place where the contract is made, allowing immediate ratification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the ratification of unauthorized suretyship give the surety recourse against the principal?
A) When the principal ratifies at any time before the debt is paid
B) When the principal ratifies in the same session and before the creditor's acceptance
C) When the creditor accepts first and then the principal ratifies
D) When the principal ratifies in writing even if after the session

Key Takeaway
For an unauthorized surety to have recourse, the principal's ratification must occur in the same contractual session and before the creditor's acceptance.

The Fourth Division of Command to Pay: Effect of 'Anni' on Recourse
Clarification of the fourth division of command to pay

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that mentioning 'anni' in a command to pay gives the payer recourse even without being a khalit, and apply this rule to determine when a payer may recover funds.

Core Idea
When the principal says 'on my behalf', the payer's right of recourse is automatic — no need for a mixed partnership.

In the discussion of command to pay (al-amr bi naqd al-mal), the Hanafi school distinguishes several scenarios. The earlier parts of Al-Bahr al-Raiq enumerated three divisions, but the fourth was left implicit. This passage clarifies that the fourth division is the inclusion of the word 'anni' (on my behalf), which has a unique legal effect: it guarantees recourse for the payer even if he is not a mixed partner (khalit) of the principal. Understanding this distinction is crucial for properly determining the rights of parties in kafalah transactions.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three prior divisions
The first three divisions of command to pay involve cases where the payer is a khalit, or the command is general, etc. This context helps situate the fourth division.
2
Define 'khalit'
A mixed partner (khalit) is someone who shares assets with the principal, giving him implied agency to pay and thereby have recourse.
3
Identify the significance of the word 'anni'
The word 'anni' (on my behalf) explicitly indicates agency, so the payer acts as a representative and automatically acquires the right of recourse against the principal.
This is the key point of the passage.
4
State the ruling
If the command includes 'anni', the payer has recourse even without being a khalit. If 'anni' is absent, recourse is not automatic and depends on other factors.
5
Distinguish from similar phrases
Various terms like 'alayya (on me) or 'anni have different effects; only 'anni clearly establishes agency and grants automatic recourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولم يقل عني مفهومه أنه إن قال عني يرجع وإن لم يكن خليطاً وهذا هو القسم الرابع فافهم
His statement: 'And he did not say: on my behalf' – its implication is that if he says 'on my behalf', he has recourse even if he is not a khalit. And this is the fourth division, so understand.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This short passage identifies the fourth division of the command to pay. Earlier, the four divisions were listed, but the fourth was not clearly stated. Here, the commentator clarifies that if the command contains the word 'anni' (on my behalf), the payer has recourse even if he is not a khalit (mixed partner). Thus, the fourth division consists of cases where 'anni' is mentioned, giving automatic recourse. This fills the gap left in the earlier enumeration.

Key Terms
عني ('anni): On my behalf; indicates agency and entitles the agent to recourse against the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What effect does the word 'anni' have in a command to pay?
A) It gives recourse only if the payer is a khalit
B) It gives recourse even if the payer is not a khalit
C) It has no effect
D) It makes the payer a volunteer

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of 'anni' in a command to pay automatically grants the payer a right of recourse, without the need for a mixed partnership.

The Pledge from Principal to Surety: Voluntary or Enforceable?
Refinement of legal expression regarding pledge between principal and surety

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct Hanafi position that the principal may voluntarily give a pledge to the surety and distinguish this from a right of the surety to demand it.

Core Idea
A surety cannot compel a pledge; it is a voluntary act by the principal.

In this short passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the commentator refines a legal expression about a surety and a principal debtor. The subtle shift from 'the surety takes a pledge' to 'the principal gives a pledge' carries important legal weight: it preserves the voluntary nature of the rahn contract. Understanding this distinction prevents imposing an obligation where none exists in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two phrasings
The passage contrasts two ways to describe the relationship: the surety 'taking' (akhdh) a pledge vs. the principal 'giving' (daf') a pledge. Identify each phrasing in the Arabic.
The phrasing 'akhdh' suggests a right of the surety; 'daf'' emphasizes the principal's act.
2
Understand the legal consequence
Explain why the commentator prefers 'the principal gives'. Recognize that if the surety had a right to take, the principal would be obligated to give upon demand. Hanafi law does not impose such an obligation; the principal cannot be forced.
A pledge is a contract; it requires the owner's consent (offer and acceptance).
3
Apply the principle of voluntary contracts
Relate this to the Hanafi rule that rahn is a muta'awwilah (dependent) contract, not a right of the creditor or surety. Confirm that the principal may choose to give a pledge, but the surety cannot demand it as an entitlement.
This aligns with general Hanafi contract law: no compulsion to enter contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن قلت هل للكفيل أخذ الرهن من الأصيل) الأحسن والأوفق لعبارة الخانية أن يقال للأصيل دفع الرهن للكفيل لئلا يوهم إلزام الأصيل بذلك إذا طلبه الكفيل وعبارة الخانية لا تفيد ذلك تأمل .
His statement: 'For if you say: Does the surety have the right to take a pledge from the principal?' – the better and more consistent with the wording of al-Khaniyya is to say that the principal gives the pledge to the surety, to avoid giving the impression that the principal is obliged to do so if the surety demands it. And the wording of al-Khaniyya does not suggest that. Reflect.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage refines the earlier statement about the surety taking a pledge from the principal. The commentator notes that the correct formulation is that the principal may give (yadfa') a pledge to the surety, not that the surety has the right to take (akhdh) it. This subtle distinction avoids implying that the principal is compelled to give a pledge upon the surety's demand. The original source, al-Khaniyya, is phrased in a way that does not suggest compulsion. This reflects the Hanafi principle that a pledge is a voluntary contract, not a right of the surety to demand.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A pledge; an item given as security for a debt, which may be sold to recover the debt if unpaid.
كفيل (kafil): Surety; one who guarantees payment of a debt or performance of an obligation.
أصيل (asil): Principal debtor; the person primarily liable for the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say is the better expression regarding a pledge between principal and surety?
A) The surety has the right to take a pledge from the principal.
B) The principal gives a pledge to the surety.
C) The surety may force the principal to give a pledge.
D) The pledge is only valid after default.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a pledge from the principal to the surety is a voluntary act, not an enforceable right of the surety.

When the Principal is an Ancestor: Tracking vs Imprisonment
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mulazamah with ancestor-principals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on mulazamah when the principal is an ancestor of the creditor, and distinguish between the right to track and the prohibition of imprisoning ancestors.

Core Idea
Tracking allowed; imprisonment forbidden for ancestor-principals.

In kafalah, the surety has the right to pursue the principal for reimbursement. But what if the principal is a parent or grandparent of the creditor? Some later Hanafi scholars argued that even tracking is barred because it necessarily leads to imprisonment, which is prohibited. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the rebuttal based on al-Qahistani, clarifying that only imprisonment is prohibited, not the act of tracking itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Disputed Condition
Recognize that the passage addresses whether the surety's right to track (mulazamah) is restricted when the principal debtor is an ancestor (parent or grandparent) of the creditor.
2
State the Restrictive View
The restrictive view (from al-Sharnabaliyyah) holds that the surety cannot track such a principal at all, because tracking would eventually lead to imprisonment of the ancestor, which is prohibited.
The basis is the prohibition of harming ancestors.
3
Analyse the Counter-Argument
The author, following al-Qahistani, argues that only the imprisonment itself is prohibited, not the preceding steps like tracking. Therefore, the surety may track but cannot have the ancestor imprisoned.
The evidence from Qada' al-Khulasah is cited.
4
Derive the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position is that the surety may track an ancestor-principal, but the ancestor cannot be imprisoned. This preserves the surety's right while respecting the prohibition against harming ancestors.
The author prefers al-Qahistani over al-Sharnabaliyyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وينبغي أن يقيد أيضاً بما إذا كان المال حالاً الخ يقيد أيضاً بما في القهستاني حيث قال : وإن حبس حبس هو المكفول عنه إلا إذا كان كفيلاً عن أحد الأبوين أو الجدين فإنه إن حبس لم يحبسه به يشعر قضاء الخلاصة .اهـ. وفي حاشية أبي السعود وقيده في الشرنبلالية بما إذا لم يكن من أصول الدائن، فإذا كان المدين أصلاً لا يحبس كفيله ولا يلازم لما يلزم من فعل ذلك بالأصيل وهو ممتنع اهـ. أقول : في دعوى اللزوم نظر بدليل ما في القهستاني وساق عبارته ثم قال : فهذا صريح في أن - الكفيل لا يمتنع وإن كان المدين من أصول رب الدين إنما الممتنع حبس الأصيل فقط
His statement: 'And it should also be restricted to when the money is due' – he also restricts it according to what is in al-Qahistani, where he said: And if he is imprisoned, the guaranteed person is imprisoned, unless he is a surety for one of the parents or grandparents, then if he is imprisoned, he does not imprison him. This is indicated by Qada' al-Khulasah. End. And in the marginalia of Abu al-Su'ud, it is restricted in al-Sharnabaliyyah to when the principal is not an ancestor of the creditor. If the debtor is an ancestor, his surety is not imprisoned and he does not track, because that would entail an act against the ancestor, which is forbidden. I say: There is consideration in the claim of prohibition, evidenced by what is in al-Qahistani, and he quoted his statement then said: This is explicit that the surety is not prevented, even if the debtor is an ancestor of the creditor; what is prevented is only the imprisonment of the ancestor.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage adds further conditions to the right of mulazamah. The first condition, already mentioned, is that the debt must be due (halal) on the principal. The commentator then cites al-Qahistani who adds an exception: if the surety has guaranteed a parent or grandparent, and the surety is imprisoned, the parent is not imprisoned. This is because imprisoning a parent is prohibited in Islamic law. There is a difference of opinion: some later scholars (e.g., in al-Sharnabaliyyah) extend this to any case where the principal is an ancestor of the creditor, arguing that tracking would lead to imprisoning an ancestor, which is prohibited. However, the author (likely Ibn Nujaym) disagrees, citing al-Qahistani to show that only the imprisonment of the ancestor is prohibited, not the principle of tracking itself. The surety can still track, but the ancestor cannot be imprisoned. This exemplifies the Hanafi method of resolving conflicts between usul and furu'.

Key Terms
ملازمة (mulāzamah): The surety's right to pursue the principal debtor for reimbursement, including tracking and legal action.
أصول الدائن (uṣūl al-dā'in): Ancestors (parents, grandparents) of the creditor.
الأصيل (al-aṣīl): The principal debtor, the one originally liable for the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The surety cannot track the debtor at all.
B) The surety can track the debtor but cannot have him imprisoned.
C) The surety can both track and imprison the debtor.
D) The suretyship is invalid from the start.

Key Takeaway
The surety's right to track an ancestor-principal remains intact, but the prohibition against imprisoning ancestors limits the enforcement mechanism.

Effect of Discharging or Delaying the Principal on the Surety
Explaining rulings on discharge and delay of principal and surety

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that discharging or delaying the principal debtor automatically discharges or delays the surety, and explain why the reverse does not apply.

Core Idea
The debt is one; discharging the principal extinguishes the surety's liability, but not vice versa.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the consequential effects in the contract of suretyship (kafalah). It establishes a clear asymmetry: the surety's liability is wholly derivative of the principal's debt. Therefore, any act that extinguishes or defers the principal's obligation—such as a creditor's discharge (ibra') or grant of delay (ta'khir)—automatically applies to the surety. However, the reverse does not hold: the surety's release does not affect the principal's duty. The passage also addresses nuanced exceptions and intra-Hanafi disagreements, including whether a surety can be imprisoned when the principal is the creditor's parent.

Guided Path
1
State the main ruling and its condition
Identify that if the creditor discharges (ibra') the principal debtor or grants him a delay, the surety is also discharged or receives the same delay. This applies even if the surety is considered to have a separate debt in theory.
The surety's liability is contingent on the existence of the principal's debt.
2
Understand the reasoning (talil)
The surety's obligation is essentially tied to the principal's debt. The multiplicity of debts is theoretical (hukmi); practically, there is one debt. Therefore, extinguishing the principal's debt necessarily extinguishes the surety's liability.
This reasoning leads to the automatic discharge of the surety.
3
Note the exception to the rule
If the principal pays the debt before the suretyship and proves it, only the principal is discharged. The surety remains liable because his liability attached at the moment of the contract, even though the debt was already paid.
This is based on the surety's own admission or contract.
4
Examine the non-reversibility of the ruling
Discharging or delaying the surety does not affect the principal. The principal remains liable because the debt is primarily his. The surety's discharge only affects his own obligation to pay the creditor.
If the creditor forgives the surety, the surety cannot seek recourse from the principal.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on imprisonment of the surety
The text reconciles an apparent conflict between Shurunbulali and Qahistani. Shurunbulali ruled that a surety cannot be imprisoned if the principal is the creditor's parent (e.g., a mother), because imprisonment of the surety would indirectly cause mental harm to the parent. Qahistani's view is not contradictory when properly contextualized: the harm only arises when the principal is serving as the 'origin' (asl) of the creditor.
This shows how different fact patterns lead to different applications of the general rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة
لازمه وبرئ بأداء الأصيل ولو أبرأ الأصيل أو أخر عنه برئ الكفيل وتأخر عنه ولا ينعكس ولو دين عليه في الصحيح وإنما عليه المطالبة فيستحيل بقاؤها بلا دين؛ هكذا ذكر الشارح تبعاً للهداية. وظاهره أن القائل بأن الكفيل عليه دين لا يبرأ بأداء الأصيل وليس كذلك بل يبرأ إجماعاً لأن تعدد الدين عند القائل به حكمي فيسقط بأداء واحد ا هـ . قوله : (ولو أبرأ الأصيل أو أخر عنه برئ الكفيل وتأخر عنه لما قدمناه أنه يلزم من إبراء الأصيل إبراؤه والتأخير إبراء موقت فتعتبر بالإبراء المؤيد. وإنما قال أبرأ الأصيل أي أبرأ الطالب ولم يقل لو برئ الأصيل لأنه لا يلزم من براءته براءته لما في الخانية ضمن له ألفاً على فلان فرهن فلان أنه كان قضاه إياها قبل الكفالة فإنه يبرأ الأصيل دون الكفيل، ولو برهن أنه قضاه بعدها يبر آن ا هـ. فقد برئ الأصيل في الوجه الأول فقط ولكن يخرج عنه حينئذ مسألة في الخانية لو مات الطالب والأصيل وارثه برئ الكفيل أيضاً لكون المطلوب ملك في ذمته فبرأ هي وبراءته توجب براءته، فعلى هذا لو عبر ببريء الشملها. ويجاب عما ذكرناه من فرع الخانية السابق بأنه ليس من باب البراءة وإنما تبين أن لا دين على الأصيل والكفيل عومل بإقراره كما استثناء فلا يعول على ما في الشرنبلالية وإن تبعه بعضهم لكونه مخالفاً للمنقول .اهـ. قلت: وبالله التوفيق لا مخالفة بينهما عند التحقيق لأن ما في القهستاني فيما إذا كان الدائن أجنبياً والمكفول أصلاً للكفيل وهو من حبس الكفيل للمكفول إذا حبسه الطالب وما في الشرنبلالية فيما إذا كان الكفيل أجنبياً والمكفول أصلاً للدائن وهو استثناء من ملازمة الدائن وهو الطالب للكفيل. وحاصل الكلام حينئذ الطالب له ملازمة الكفيل إلا إذا كان المكفول أصلاً للطالب لما يلزم من ملازمته له وحبسه إياه أصله بدينه بواسطة حبسه للكفيل وهذا ظاهر، وقد ذكره الشرنبلالي تفقهاً منه، وله في ذلك حبس رسالة خاصة سماها «النعمة المجددة بكفيل «الوالدة ومبناها على سؤال صورته في امرأة استدانت من ابنها مالاً وكفلها بإذنها فيه أجنبي ثم إن الابن أراد حبس كفيل أمه فهل له ذلك؟ قال: فأجبت بأنه ليس له حبسه إذ يلزم من حبسه حبس الأم وأنه لا يجوز ولكني أعجب من العلامة الشرنبلالي حيث فهم مخالفة كلام القهستاني لكلامه فأورده سؤالاً على ما قرره ثم أجاب بأني لم أر في الخلاصة ما يفيده، ومن ادعى إفادته فعليه البيان وأنت قد علمت عدم المنافاة لأنه إذا كان الدائن أجنبياً وحبس الكفيل عن أصله أي أصل الكفيل لا يلزم محذور نعم المحذور في حبس الكفيل مكفوله الذي هو أصله فلذا استثناه. هذا ما ظهر لي بعون الله تعالى فتأمله يظهر لك حقيقته إن شاء الله تعالى. وسيأتي في باب الحبس من كتاب القضاء عند قول المتن ويحبس الرجل في نفقة زوجته لا في دين ولده» عن الخير الرملي أنه وقع الاستفتاء فيما ذكره الشرنبلالي من الصورة وذكر الرملي هناك أن للكفيل حبس المديون الذي هو أصل الدائن لأنه إنما حبس لحق الكفيل ولذلك يرجع عليه بما أدى فهو محبوس بدينه الذي يثبت له أو سيثبت على قول من يجعلها ضماً في الدين وعلى قول من يجعلها ضماً في المطالبة فلم يدخل في قولهم لا يحبس أصل في دين فرعه لأنه إنما حبسه أجنبي فيما ثبت له عليه اهـ. ومفاده أن للدائن الذي هو فرع المديون حبس الكفيل الأجنبي وإن لزم منه حبس أصله وهو لا يخفى . وخرج عن مسألة الكتاب ما إذا تكفل بشرط براءة الأصيل فإن الأصيل يبرأ دون الكفيل لكونها صارت مجازاً عن الحوالة. وفي جامع الفصولين: باع المديون بيع وفاء برئ كفيله فلو تفاسخا لا تعود الكفالة ا هـ . وهو يدل على أن الدين إذا عاد إلى الأصيل بما هو فسخ لا يعود على الكفيل، وسيأتي عن التتارخانية بيانه وفي السراج الوهاج : ويشترط قبول الأصيل البراءة، فإن ردها ارتدت. وهل يعود الدين على الكفيل؟ فيه قولان وموت الأصيل كقبوله وإنما قال أو أخر عنه للاحتراز عما إذا تأخر المطالبة عن الأصيل لا بتأخير الطالب كالعبد المحجور إذا لزمه شيء بعد عتقه فكفل به إنسان فإن الأصيل تتأخر المطالبة عنه إلى إعتاقه ويطالب كفيله للحال، ومنه المكاتب إذا صالح عن دم عمد وكفل به رجل ثم عجز تأخرت المطالبة عن الأصيل دون الكفيل والمسألتان في الخانية معللاً بأن الأصيل إنما تأخرت عنه لإعساره، ومفهومه أن الأصيل لو كان معسراً ليس للطالب مطالبته ويطالب الكفيل لو موسراً. وفي التتارخانية : لو أجل الطالب الأصيل فلم يقبل صار حالاً عليهما، ولو أجله شهراً ثم سنة دخل الشهر في السنة والآجال إذا اجتمعت انقضت بمرة ا هـ . وفي النهاية : إن إبراء الأصيل وتأجيله يرتدان بالرد وإبراء الكفيل يرتد بالرد وأما تأجيله فلا يرتد بالرد
اه.
قوله : (ولا) ينعكس أي براءة الكفيل لا توجب براءة الأصيل ولا التأخير عنه يوجب التأخير عن الأصيل لأن عليه المطالبة وبقاء الدين على الأصيل بدونه جائز. قيد بالتأخير أي التأجيل بعد الكفالة بالمال حالاً لأنه لو كفل بالمال الحال مؤجلاً إلى شهر فإنه يتأجل عن الأصيل لأنه لا حق له إلا الدين حال وجود الكفالة فصار الأجل داخلاً فيه، أما ها هنا بخلافه ؛ كذا في الهداية. أطلقه في براءة الكفيل فشمل ما إذا قبل أو لم يقبل كما في السراج الوهاج، وأشار باقتصاره على عدم براءة الأصيل إلى أن الكفيل إذا أبرأه الطالب فلا رجوع ا عليه بخلاف ما إذا وهبه الدين أو تصدق به عليه فإن له الرجوع على الأصيل. ولا بد من له عن مخالف لما أفتى به الشرنبلالي فليتأمل قوله : (وهو يدل على أن الدين الخ) قال الرملي : تقدم في الكفالة ما هو صريح في ذلك فراجعه .اهـ. قلت : وسيأتي قريباً في شرح قوله «ولا ينعكس» ما يخالفه. قوله : (وفي السراج الوهاج ويشترط قبول الأصيل الخ) قال الرملي وفي التتارخانية نقلاً . المحيط ولو وهب الطالب المال من المطلوب أو أبرأه منه فمات قبل الرد فهو بريء، وإن لم يمت ورد الهبة فرده صحيح والمال على المطلوب والكفيل على حاله وإن رد الإبراء هل يبرأ الكفيل لا ذكر لهذه المسألة في شيء من الكتب واختلف المشايخ فيه منهم من قال لا يبرأ فهذا القائل سوى بين الهبة وبين الإبراء ومنهم من قال يبرأ الكفيل .اهـ. فقوله في الشرح وهل يعود الدين على الكفيل أي بعد رد الأصيل البراءة. قوله : (وفي التتارخانية لو أجل الطالب الأصيل الخ) قال في النهر : فيه
Book of Suretyship: It is necessary [that the surety is] discharged by the principal's payment, and if the creditor discharges the principal or grants him a delay, the surety is discharged and [the delay] applies to him, and it does not reverse. Even if the debt is upon him in the correct view, and he only has the right of claim, so it is impossible for it to remain without a debt. Thus mentioned the commentator following al-Hidayah. Its apparent meaning is that the one who says that the surety has a debt is not discharged by the principal's payment, but it is not so; rather he is discharged by consensus, because the multiplicity of debt according to that proponent is theoretical, so it falls with a single payment. ... His statement: (And if he discharges the principal or grants him a delay, the surety is discharged and [the delay] applies to him) because of what we have presented: that discharging the principal necessitates discharging him, and granting a delay is a temporary discharge, so it is considered like a permanent discharge. He only said 'discharges the principal' meaning the creditor discharges, and he did not say 'if the principal is discharged' because his discharge does not necessitate the surety's discharge, as in al-Khaniyya: He guaranteed for him a thousand against so-and-so, then so-and-so proved that he had paid it before the suretyship: then the principal is discharged without the surety; and if he proved that he paid after it, they both are discharged. ... He said: (And it does not reverse) i.e., the surety's discharge does not necessitate the principal's discharge, nor does delay for him necessitate delay for the principal, because he has the right of claim, and it is permissible for the debt to remain on the principal without it. He restricted 'delay' to deferment after suretyship when the debt is on demand, because if he stood surety for an on-demand debt to be deferred for a month, it is deferred for the principal, because he has no right except the debt at the time of suretyship, so the term enters into it. Here it is otherwise; thus in al-Hidayah. He made it absolute regarding the surety's discharge, so it includes whether he accepted or not, as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. And by confining to the non-discharge of the principal, he indicated that if the creditor discharges the surety, he has no recourse [against the principal] unlike if he gave the debt as a gift or charity to him, then he has recourse on the principal. ...
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rulings of kafalah (suretyship) from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It explains the effects of discharging (ibra') or granting a delay (ta'khir) to the principal debtor (asil) on the surety (kafil). The main ruling: if the creditor discharges the principal or delays payment, the surety is also discharged or gets the delay automatically. However, the reverse is not true: discharging or delaying the surety does not affect the principal. The reason is that the surety's obligation is only to pay if the principal defaults; his liability is based on the debt existing. If the principal is discharged, the debt disappears, so the surety is free. But the surety's discharge does not eliminate the principal's debt. The commentary then addresses potential objections and intra-Hanafi differences, such as cases where the principal is proven to have paid before suretyship (principal discharged, surety remains liable based on his own admission). It also discusses a fatwa from Shurunbulali regarding the imprisonment of a surety when the principal is the creditor's parent, and clarifies apparent contradictions between different Hanafi texts. The passage emphasizes that the rulings are based on the nature of the surety's obligation: the surety guarantees payment, but the debt remains primarily on the principal. The final part discusses conditions for acceptance of discharge by the principal, and the effect of the principal's death on the surety.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): A contract in which a person (kafil) guarantees the debt or obligation of another (asil) to a third party (creditor).
الإبراء (al-ibra'): A legal discharge or release from a debt, usually granted by the creditor voluntarily.
التأخير (al-ta'khir): Granting a deferment or extension of the payment deadline to the debtor.
الأصيل (al-asil): The principal debtor, the one who originally owes the debt.
الكفيل (al-kafil): The surety; the person who guarantees the debt of the asil.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the creditor grants a delay to the principal debtor, what is the effect on the surety according to the Hanafi ruling in this passage?
A) The surety is not affected and remains immediately liable.
B) The surety also gets the delay and is not liable during that period.
C) The surety is permanently discharged from the debt.
D) The surety becomes the primary debtor.

Key Takeaway
The surety's liability is contingent on the principal's debt; thus any action that extinguishes or delays the principal's debt automatically applies to the surety, but not vice versa.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
The Guarantor's Discharge Does Not Discharge the Principal
Explains that the guarantor's discharge does not affect the principal debtor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on the effect of the guarantor's discharge on the principal debtor and apply the reasoning (istihsan) to distinguish related cases.

Core Idea
The guarantor's discharge does not discharge the principal — the creditor's right remains against the principal alone.

In suretyship contracts, the creditor may discharge the guarantor. But does that release the principal debtor? According to the Hanafi school, it does not. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and related exceptions from the classical commentary of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the main rule: the creditor's discharge of the guarantor does not cause the discharge of the principal debtor. Similarly, deferment granted to the guarantor does not defer the debt for the principal.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited in the text: the reasoning (istihsan) that the creditor's right to claim remains against the principal, and it is legally permissible for the debt to remain on the principal without the guarantor.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the proof links to the ruling: because the principal is the original obligor, releasing the guarantor does not extinguish the debt; the creditor can still claim from the principal.
4
Note exceptions
Identify cases where the rule differs: if the guarantee was made conditional on the principal's discharge, the principal is discharged but not the guarantor. Also, in bay' al-wafa, the guarantor is discharged upon sale and does not return upon rescission.
5
Explore related rulings
Consider scenarios involving insolvency of the principal, deferment rejection, and the difference between discharging the guarantor and gifting the debt — affecting the guarantor's right of recourse.
6
Evaluate the thamarah
The practical consequence: the creditor can still pursue the principal, and the guarantor has no recourse against the principal after being discharged, unless the debt was gifted or given as charity.

And similarly, the mukatib who settles for intentional homicide and a man guarantees it, then becomes unable: the claim is deferred from the principal but not from the guarantor. Both cases are in al-Khaniyya, reasoned that the principal is deferred due to insolvency. Its implication is that if the principal is insolvent, the creditor cannot sue him, but can sue the guarantor if he is solvent. And in al-Nihaya: the discharge and deferment of the principal revert upon rejection, and the discharge of the guarantor reverts upon rejection, but his deferment does not revert upon rejection. Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on kafalah (suretyship) explaining the legal principle that the discharge (ibra') of the guarantor (kafil) by the creditor does not automatically discharge the principal debtor (asil). Similarly, a deferment (ta'jil) granted to the guarantor does not defer the debt for the principal. The author, al-Marghinani, is commenting on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. He begins by noting an exception: if the guarantee was conditioned on the principal's discharge, then the principal is discharged but not the guarantor, because such a condition transforms the contract into an indirect hawala (transfer of debt). He then cites various authorities (Jami' al-Fusulayn, al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, al-Tatarkhaniyya, al-Nihaya) to support the rule and its nuances. The key reasoning is that the creditor's right to claim remains against the principal, and it is legally permissible for the debt to remain on the principal without the guarantor. The text also distinguishes between discharging the guarantor (which gives him no recourse against the principal) and gifting the debt (which allows recourse). This passage is a typical expansion of a dense matn, clarifying conditions and implications.

Key Terms
براءة (bara'a): Discharge from a debt; the creditor's act of releasing the debtor from liability.
كفيل (kafil): The guarantor or surety; a person who assumes liability for the debt of another.
أصيل (asil): The principal debtor; the original obligor in the debt.
تأخير (ta'khir): Deferment; granting additional time for payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The principal is also discharged.
B) The principal remains liable for the debt.
C) The guarantee contract is void.
D) The debt is transferred to the guarantor only.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the creditor's discharge of the guarantor does not affect the principal debtor's liability; the principal remains obligated unless explicitly discharged.

Effect of Principal's Rejection of Discharge on the Guarantor
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ibra' rejection and its effect on the guarantor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two intra-Hanafi positions regarding the effect of the principal's rejection of discharge on the guarantor and identify the practical consequence of the disagreement.

Core Idea
Principal's rejection of discharge splits Hanafis: some treat it like a rejected gift, others see ibra' as irrevocable.

This lesson explores a nuanced disagreement among Hanafi jurists: when a creditor discharges the principal debtor, but the principal rejects that discharge, does the guarantor also remain liable? The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents two scholarly views and links the discussion to a related dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the effect of rejection by heirs. Understanding this dispute clarifies the boundaries of ibra' (unilateral discharge) and its interaction with contract-like elements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognize that the question is whether the guarantor (kafil) is discharged when the principal debtor (asil) rejects the creditor's discharge (ibra').
This is not about discharge being ineffective altogether; it's about its effect on a third party.
2
State the two mashayikh positions
One group says the guarantor is not discharged, equating ibra' to a gift (hiba) where rejection nullifies the gift. The other says the guarantor is discharged because ibra' is a pure unilateral act (isqat) that cannot be undone.
The text does not name specific scholars; it attributes views to 'mashayikh'.
3
Connect to the Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad dispute on heirs
The text mentions that if the creditor dies after giving ibra', and the heirs reject, Abu Yusuf says the ibra' reverts (treating it like a gift to heirs), while Muhammad says it does not revert (ibra' is effective immediately).
This parallel dispute informs the reasoning behind the two positions on the guarantor.
4
Examine the exceptions
The passage lists cases where the principal's discharge does follow from the guarantor's discharge, such as hawala (transfer of debt) where both are discharged, or when the creditor dies and the guarantor is his heir (the principal is discharged).
These exceptions show that the general rule—that discharging the guarantor does not discharge the principal—has limits.
5
Distinguish ibra' from rescission of guarantee
The text clarifies that a statement 'I have removed you from the guarantee' is not ibra' but a proposed rescission (iqala) requiring acceptance. Therefore, the guarantor's rejection invalidates it, unlike ibra' which is unilateral.
This distinction highlights why ibra' is treated differently from contract-based cancellations.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: (And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, the principal's acceptance of discharge is required...) al-Ramli said: And in al-Tatarkhaniyya quoting al-Muhit: If the creditor gifted the money to the debtor or discharged him, and he died before rejection, he is discharged. If he did not die and rejected the gift, his rejection is valid, and the money remains on the debtor and the guarantor as before. If he rejected the discharge, is the guarantor discharged? No mention of this issue in any book, and the mashayikh differed: some said he is not discharged, equating gift and discharge; others said the guarantor is discharged. So his statement in the sharh 'does the debt return to the guarantor' means after the principal rejects the discharge. His statement: (And in al-Tatarkhaniyya, if the creditor deferred the principal...) he said in al-Nahr: acceptance of the guarantor in gift and charity. So if the discharge and gift occur after his death and the heir accepts, it is valid; if the heirs reject, it reverts according to Abu Yusuf, and the discharge becomes invalid because it was a discharge for them. Muhammad said: it does not revert by their rejection, just as if he had discharged him during his life and then died. And an exception to his statement 'the guarantor's discharge does not cause the principal's discharge' is what is in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: if the guarantor transferred the creditor to a man and the creditor and the transferee accepted, the guarantor and principal are discharged because the hawala occurred on the original debt, and the debt originally is on the principal, so the hawala implies the discharge of both. If the creditor stipulated at the time of hawala the discharge of only the guarantor, the guarantor is discharged but not the principal, and the creditor may claim his debt from whichever he wishes, the principal or the transferee, but has no recourse against the guarantor until the money is lost from the transferee. And similarly from al-Khaniyya: if the creditor and the guarantor who is his heir die, the guarantor is discharged from the guarantee and the debt remains on the principal as before; if the guarantee was without the principal's request, the principal is also discharged because upon the creditor's death the money becomes inheritance for his heirs. And if the guarantor acquired the money during the creditor's lifetime by court order or gift, he has recourse against the principal if the guarantee was with his request; if without, no recourse. So when the creditor dies and the guarantor is his heir and the guarantee was without the principal's request, the guarantor's discharge necessarily entails the principal's discharge. Then know that the statement of the author of al-Hidaya earlier that if he guaranteed a present debt deferred to a month, it is deferred from the principal as well, is restricted to non-loan debts, as in al-Tatarkhaniyya: if he guarantees a loan deferred to a named term, the guarantee is valid, the money is on the guarantor until the named term, and on the principal it is immediate. He attributed this to al-Dhakhira, then attributed to al-Ghiyathiyya: if he guaranteed a loan and deferred the guarantor, it is valid and does not defer the principal. And it contradicts what is stated in Talkhis al-Hidaya, supporting the saying that the rejected discharge causes the debt to return to the guarantor as well. His statement: (And the guarantor's discharge reverts upon rejection...) He mentioned the like in al-Fath, and the author will mention it in his explanation of his statement 'and the suspension of discharge becomes void'. Then he mentioned after it from al-Khaniyya: if he said to the guarantor 'I have removed you from the guarantee' and the guarantor said 'I do not accept removal', he does not become removed. The author said there: it is established that the guarantor's discharge also reverts upon rejection. He said in al-Nahr: there is consideration in this, meaning because his statement 'I removed you' is not discharge but is in the meaning of rescission of the guarantee contract, and rescission is concluded by both parties, so since the guarantor did not accept it, it is invalid and the guarantee remains, unlike discharge which is pure relinquishment and is effected by the relinquisher alone. Thus in Sharh al-Maqdisi on Nazm al-Kanz.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the ruling that the principal's acceptance of discharge is required. The author presents a dispute among later Hanafi scholars (mashayikh) regarding the effect of the principal's rejection of discharge: some say the guarantor is also not discharged, while others say he is discharged. The passage also includes exceptions to the general rule (e.g., hawala, death of creditor when guarantor is heir) and a clarification about loans: deferment of the guarantor does not defer the principal in loan cases. The intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad concerning the effect of heirs' rejection is highlighted. The text also distinguishes between 'removal' from the guarantee (which is like rescission) and 'discharge' (which is a unilateral relinquishment).

Key Terms
إبراء (ibra'): Legal discharge from a debt; a unilateral act of the creditor that extinguishes the debt.
رد الإبراء (radd al-ibra'): Rejection of the discharge by the debtor.
حوالة (hawala): Transfer of debt; a contract where the creditor transfers the debt to a third party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Critiquing Exceptions to the Non-Reciprocity Rule in Kafalah
Critique of an exception to the kafalah rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Ramli's critique of the exception involving hawala and identify the reasoning underlying his objection.

Core Idea
An exception is invalid if the guarantor's discharge merely follows the principal's discharge.

The Hanafi rule in kafalah is clear: discharging the guarantor does not discharge the principal. Commentators list several apparent exceptions. In this passage, al-Ramli scrutinizes one such exception—where the guarantor is discharged through hawala (debt transfer). He argues that this is not a genuine exception because the guarantor's release is directly caused by the principal's release. Understanding this critique sharpens your ability to distinguish genuine exceptions from cases that merely appear to depart from the rule.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the baseline axiom: the guarantor's discharge does not cause the principal's discharge. This is the proposition that the exception is meant to challenge.
The rule is rooted in the independence of the two obligations.
2
Identify the claimed exception
Locate in the passage the case being presented as an exception. The text refers to a situation involving hawala (transfer of debt) where the guarantor is discharged.
The excerption is from the statement 'barā'at al-kafīl lā tūjibu barā'at al-aṣīl'.
3
Analyze al-Ramli's critique
Al-Ramli argues that in this case the guarantor's discharge is not independent; it directly follows the principal's discharge. Thus the exception lacks logical basis because the rule's condition (independence) is absent.
He says: 'lā ma'nā li-hādhā al-istithnā''—this exception has no meaning.
4
Trace the reasoning chain
Follow the logic: In hawala, when the principal is discharged (by transferring the debt), the guarantor's obligation is secondary and hence also ends. So the apparent exception is actually a confirmation of the rule.
The passage also hints that the chapter on sulh (settlement) will further clarify this.
5
Note the incomplete text
The passage ends abruptly at 'wa yukhālifuhu mā...'. Recognize that additional exceptions and counterarguments are missing. This incomplete state requires caution when citing this passage.
The editor flags high OCR risk and incompleteness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And his statement 'And if he guarantees a deferred debt...' this he mentioned in al-Tatarkhaniyya attributed to al-Ghiyathiyya after his statement 'and it does not defer the principal'; be alert. Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the exceptions to the general rule that the guarantor's discharge does not affect the principal. The author, al-Ramli, critiques the exceptions noted earlier, arguing that in the first exception (hawala) the guarantor was discharged because the principal was discharged, so it is not a true exception. The passage also includes technical attributions to previous works (al-Dhakhira, al-Muhit, al-Ghiyathiyya) and ends abruptly with a note that something contradicts. Due to the abrupt ending, the full discussion is incomplete.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): Exception; a case where the general rule does not apply.
صلح (sulh): Settlement; a contract of compromise.
إبراء (ibra'): Relinquishment of a debt or right; discharge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ramli's critique, why is the exception involving hawala not a true exception?
A) Because the guarantor is not actually discharged.
B) Because the guarantor's discharge directly causes the principal's discharge.
C) Because the guarantor's discharge follows from the principal's discharge, making it derivative.
D) Because the exception applies only to loans.

Key Takeaway
Not every apparent exception truly contradicts a rule; examine causation to distinguish genuine exceptions from derivative cases.

Discharge of Debt After Creditor's Death: When Heirs Disagree
Intra-Hanafi dispute on posthumous discharge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the effect of rejecting heirs on a discharge, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
One heir's acceptance is not enough; Abu Yusuf voids the discharge if other heirs reject.

When a creditor dies, his right to claim a debt passes to his heirs. If the creditor had discharged the debt before death, the discharge is final. But what if the discharge occurs after his death, and one heir accepts while others reject? This passage presents a disagreement between the two great disciples of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding their reasoning reveals how principles of inheritance and unilateral acts interact in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage discusses a situation where the creditor has died, and the discharge (ibra) of a debt is made after his death. One heir accepts the discharge, but the other heirs reject it. The question is whether the discharge becomes void due to the rejection.
The discharge is from the creditor's estate—so it affects the heirs since they are now the creditors.
2
State Abu Yusuf's position
Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the discharge becomes void (yartadd) and is nullified if any of the other heirs reject it. He considers the discharge a single act that applies to all heirs collectively.
Reason: it is 'ibra lahum' (a discharge for them) meaning it was intended to benefit all heirs.
3
State Muhammad's position
Imam Muhammad argues that the discharge does not become void by the rejection of some heirs. He compares it to a discharge made during the creditor's lifetime: if the creditor discharged the debtor during his life and then died, the discharge stands regardless of the heirs' wishes.
The analogy is qiyas: the lifetime discharge is final, so posthumous discharge should be similarly final.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is clear: under Abu Yusuf, the debt is revived if any heir rejects, meaning the debtor must pay again. Under Muhammad, the debt remains discharged, so the debtor is freed.
Later Hanafi texts may provide a tarjih (preferred view), but this passage does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة قبول الكفيل في الهبة والصدقة فلو كان الإبراء والهبة بعد موته فقبل الوارث صح فإن رد ورثته ارتد في قول أبي يوسف وبطل الإبراء لأنه إبراء لهم. وقال محمد: لا يرتد بردهم كما لو أبرأه في حياته ثم مات
Book of Guarantee: Acceptance of the guarantor in gift and charity. If the discharge and gift were after his death, and one heir accepted, it is valid. If his other heirs reject, then in the opinion of Abu Yusuf, [the discharge] is void and the discharge becomes nullified because it is a discharge for them. Muhammad said: It does not become void by their rejection, just as if he had discharged him during his lifetime and then died.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani regarding the effect of a discharge (ibra) of a debt when the creditor has died and only some of his heirs accept the discharge. The scenario: the creditor discharges a debt after his death? Actually 'after his death' likely refers to the creditor's death, because then the heirs become owners of the debt. If one heir accepts the discharge, Abu Yusuf rules that the discharge is valid initially but becomes void if other heirs reject it, because the discharge was for all of them collectively. Muhammad argues that the discharge does not become void by the rejection of some heirs, drawing an analogy to a discharge given during the creditor's lifetime: if the creditor discharged the debtor and then died, the discharge is not affected by the heirs' wishes. The practical consequence: under Abu Yusuf's view, the debt is revived if heirs reject; under Muhammad, the debt remains discharged. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not stated here; later texts may prefer one view.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibra'): A legal act by which a creditor relinquishes his right to claim a debt, discharging the debtor.
كفيل (kafil): A guarantor who undertakes to pay a debt if the principal debtor fails.
وارث (warith): An heir who inherits the right to claim the debt from the deceased creditor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, what happens if a creditor dies, one heir accepts the discharge, and the other heirs reject?
A) The discharge is valid and the debt is extinguished.
B) The discharge is void and the debt is revived.
C) The discharge is valid but the guarantor is still liable.
D) The discharge is void only for the rejecting heirs.

Key Takeaway
A discharge after the creditor's death is initially valid upon one heir's acceptance, but Abu Yusuf voids it if other heirs reject; Muhammad upholds it by analogy to a lifetime discharge.

Exceptions to the Rule: Hawala and Inheritance
Two exceptions from Hanafi fiqh sources to the general rule on guarantor discharge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two exceptions to the Hanafi rule that discharge of a guarantor does not discharge the principal, and apply the conditions that determine when the principal is also discharged.

Core Idea
Discharging a guarantor also discharges the principal in hawala (with creditor's consent) and in inheritance when the guarantor is heir.

The general Hanafi principle is that if a guarantor is discharged from liability, the principal debtor remains liable. However, two important exceptions arise from the transfer of debt (hawala) and from inheritance scenarios. This lesson examines these exceptions from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and al-Khaniya, clarifying when the principal, too, is freed.

Guided Path
1
State the General Rule
Begin by recalling the base principle: discharge of a guarantor does not automatically discharge the principal. This is the default Hanafi position.
The rule is stated in the opening line 'براءة الكفيل لا توجب براءة الأصيل'.
2
Analyze the Hawala Exception
Read the first exception from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: when the guarantor transfers the debt to a third party (hawala) and the claimant accepts, both guarantor and principal are discharged. But if the claimant stipulates discharge of only the guarantor, then only the guarantor is freed.
The key condition is the absence of a stipulation limiting discharge.
3
Analyze the Inheritance Exception
Read the second exception from al-Khaniya: if the claimant dies and the guarantor inherits from him (i.e., is an heir), and the guarantee was without the principal's order, then the principal is also discharged. This is because the debt becomes an inheritance right owned by the guarantor.
If the guarantee was with the principal's order, the guarantor has recourse against the principal.
4
Distinguish the Two Scenarios
Compare the two exceptions: hawala transfers the debt to a new debtor; inheritance merges the creditor and guarantor roles. In both, the original debt is extinguished in a way that frees the principal.
In hawala, discharge of the principal is default unless stipulated otherwise; in inheritance, discharge of the principal occurs only when guarantee was without order.
5
Apply to a New Case
Given a scenario: A guarantees B's debt to C. C transfers the claim to D without stipulation. Is B discharged? Answer: Yes, both A and B are discharged because the hawala is based on the original debt.
Contrast with a stipulation: if C says 'only A is discharged', then B remains liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستثنى من قوله براءة الكفيل لا توجب براءة الأصيل ما في السراج الوهاج : لو أحال الكفيل الطالب على رجل فقبل الطالب والمحال عليه برئ الكفيل والأصيل لأن الحوالة حصلت بأصل الدين والدين أصله على المكفول عنه فتضمنت الحوالة براءتهما. ولو اشترط الطالب وقت الحوالة براءة الكفيل خاصة برئ الكفيل ولا يبرا المكفول عنه وللطالب أن يأخذ بدينه أيهما شاء إن شاء الأصيل وإن شاء المحال عليه، ولا سبيل له على الكفيل حتى يتوي المال على المحال عليه .ا.هـ. وكذا يستثنى منه ما في الخانية : إذا مات الطالب والكفيل وارثه برئ الكفيل عن الكفالة وبقي المال على المكفول عنه على حاله، وإن كانت الكفالة بغير أمره برئ المطلوب أيضاً لأنه لما مات الطالب صار ذلك المال ميراثاً لورثته . ولو ملك الكفيل المال في حياة الطالب بالقضاء أو الهبة يرجع على المكفول عنه إن كانت الكفالة بأمره وإن كانت بغير أمره لا رجوع ا اهـ. ففيما إذا مات الطالب والكفيل وارثه وكانت بغير أمره لزم من براءة الكفيل براءة الأصيل.
And an exception is made from his statement: 'Discharge of the guarantor does not necessitate discharge of the principal' what is in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If the guarantor transfers (hawala) the claimant to a man, and the claimant and the transferee accept, the guarantor and the principal are discharged because the transfer is based on the original debt, and the debt is originally owed by the principal, so the transfer entails the discharge of both. And if the claimant stipulates at the time of transfer the discharge of only the guarantor, the guarantor is discharged but the principal is not discharged, and the claimant has the right to take his debt from either of them: if he wishes from the principal, and if he wishes from the transferee, and he has no recourse against the guarantor until the money is lost from the transferee. End quote. Similarly, an exception is made from it what is in al-Khaniya: If the claimant and the guarantor die and the guarantor is his heir, the guarantor is discharged from the guarantee, and the debt remains upon the principal as it was. And if the guarantee was without the principal's order, the principal is also discharged because when the claimant died, that wealth became inheritance for his heirs. And if the guarantor came into possession of the wealth during the claimant's lifetime by court decree or gift, he has recourse against the principal if the guarantee was with his order, but if it was without his order, there is no recourse. End quote. So in the case where the claimant and the guarantor die and the guarantor is his heir, and the guarantee was without his order, it necessarily follows from the discharge of the guarantor the discharge of the principal.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage provides two exceptions to the general Hanafi principle that discharging the guarantor does not automatically discharge the principal debtor. The first exception is from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: When the guarantor transfers (hawala) the debt to a third party, and the claimant and the transferee accept, both the guarantor and the principal are discharged because the hawala is based on the original debt. However, if the claimant stipulates that only the guarantor is discharged, then only the guarantor is discharged and the principal remains liable; the claimant can still claim from the principal or the transferee. The second exception is from al-Khaniya: If the claimant dies and the guarantor inherits from him (i.e., the guarantor is the heir), then the guarantee is extinguished, and if the guarantee was without the principal's order, the principal is also discharged. This is because the debt becomes an inheritance right owned by the guarantor. The passage also discusses cases where the guarantor acquires the debt during the claimant's lifetime by court decree or gift. These exceptions show that in certain contexts, discharging the guarantor does lead to discharging the principal.

Key Terms
حوالة (hawala): A transfer of debt from one debtor to another, with the consent of the creditor.
براءة (bara'a): Discharge from liability or debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the inheritance exception, when is the principal discharged?
A) Always, whenever the guarantor inherits from the creditor.
B) Only if the guarantee was with the principal's order.
C) Only if the guarantee was without the principal's order.
D) Never, the principal is never discharged.

Key Takeaway
Discharge of a guarantor can lead to discharge of the principal in hawala (unless stipulated otherwise) and in inheritance when the guarantee was without the principal's order.

Guarantor's Deferment and Its Effect on the Principal Debtor
Critical refinement of al-Hidayah's ruling on guarantor's deferment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when a guarantor's deferment (ta'jil) extends to the principal debtor and when it does not, applying the Hanafi distinction between loan debts and other debts, and the effect of wording.

Core Idea
A guarantor's deferment does not automatically defer the principal's obligation; the debt type and wording determine the effect.

This passage resolves an apparent conflict in Hanafi sources regarding the effect of a guarantor's ta'jil on the principal debtor. While al-Hidayah suggests that deferment extends to the principal, later sources clarify that this applies only to non-loan debts. For loans, the principal remains immediately liable unless the guarantor explicitly includes the principal through specific wording. Understanding this nuance is crucial for correctly applying kafalah in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the guarantor deferring a debt that is already due. The ruling is that the deferment may or may not extend to the principal.
The act itself is permissible, but its legal effect varies.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage cites al-Hidayah's statement that deferment extends to the principal, then contrasts it with rulings from al-Tatarkhaniyya, al-Dhakhira, al-Ghiyathiyya, and al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya that refine this position.
These later sources are authoritative in the Hanafi school.
3
Trace the distinction
The key distinction is between loan debts (qard) and other debts (e.g., sale price). For loans, the guarantor's deferment does not defer the principal; for other debts, it does (subject to wording).
This distinction is based on the nature of the debt.
4
Analyze the wording factor
According to al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya, if the guarantor says 'defer for me,' only he benefits; if the term is mentioned generally and the claimant agrees, both benefit.
The wording determines whether the deferment is personal or mutual.
5
Evaluate the thamarah
The practical consequence is that a creditor cannot use the guarantor to circumvent the prohibition of deferring a loan; the principal must pay immediately unless the deferment explicitly includes him.
This protects the principal from unintended extensions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن قول صاحب الهداية فيما قدمناه لو كفل بالمال الحال مؤجلاً إلى شهر يتأجل عن الأصيل أيضاً محمول على غير القرض لما في التتارخانية : وإذا كفل بالقرض مؤجلاً إلى أجل مسمى فالكفالة جائزة والمال على الكفيل إلى الأجل المسمى وعلى الأصيل حال وعزاه إلى الذخيرة ثم عزا إلى الغياثية : لو كفل بالقرض فأخر عن الكفيل جاز ولا يتأخر عن الأصيل ويخالفه ما صرح به في تلخيص الجامع من أنه شامل للقرض فإن هذا هو الحيلة في تأجيل القروض وقدمناه في التأجيل. نقل بعض الفضلاء عن الفتاوى الهندية تفصيلاً فقال : وإذا كان لرجل على رجل ألف . حالة درهم من ثمن مبيع فكفل بها رجل إلى سنة فهذا على وجهين : إن أضاف الكفيل الأجل إلى نفسه بأن قال أجلني ثبت الأجل في حق الكفيل وحده، وإذا لم يضف الأجل إلى مطلقاً . نفسه بل ذكر ورضي به الطالب ثبت الأجل في حق الكفيل والأصيل جميعاً اهـ. فتأمل لعلك تحظى بالتوفيق.
Then know that the statement of the author of al-Hidayah, which we presented earlier, 'If he guarantees a present debt deferred for a month, it becomes deferred also for the principal,' is interpreted as referring to other than a loan, because in al-Tatarkhaniyya: 'If he guarantees a loan deferred to a named term, the guarantee is valid, and the debt is upon the guarantor until the named term, but upon the principal it is present.' And he attributed it to al-Dhakhira. Then he attributed to al-Ghiyathiyya: 'If he guarantees a loan and defers it for the guarantor, it is permissible, and it is not deferred for the principal.' And it contradicts what is explicitly stated in Talkhis al-Jami' that it includes loans, for this is the stratagem (hila) for deferring loans, and we have presented it in the section on deferment. Some virtuous people related from al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya a detailed explanation: 'If a man has a present debt of a thousand dirhams on another man from the price of a sale, and a man guaranteed it for one year, this has two aspects: If the guarantor added the term to himself by saying 'defer for me,' the term is established only for the guarantor. If he did not add the term to himself absolutely, but mentioned it and the claimant consented, the term is established for both the guarantor and the principal.' End quote. So reflect, perhaps you will attain success.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage resolves an apparent conflict in Hanafi sources regarding the effect of a guarantor's deferment of a debt on the principal debtor. The author of al-Hidayah stated that if a guarantor guarantees a present debt and defers it for a month, it becomes deferred for the principal as well. However, this is interpreted as referring to debts other than loans. For loans, the clear ruling from al-Tatarkhaniyya and al-Ghiyathiyya is that the guarantor's deferment does not defer the principal's obligation; the principal remains liable to pay the debt immediately. This is because the deferment of a loan by a guarantor is considered a legal stratagem (hila) to defer a loan, which is otherwise prohibited? Actually, the text says it is a hila for deferring loans. The detailed explanation from al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya clarifies that it depends on the wording: if the guarantor asks for deferment for himself, only he benefits; if he mentions the term generally and the claimant agrees, both benefit. The passage ends with 'Fata'ammal' encouraging the reader to contemplate.

Key Terms
تأجيل (ta'jil): Deferment of payment of a debt to a future date.
كفالة (kafalah): Guarantee; a contract where a person assumes liability for the debt of another.
قرض (qard): Loan; a contract where a lender gives property to a borrower to be repaid later.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The principal becomes the guarantor.

Key Takeaway
The guarantor's deferment of a debt does not automatically defer the principal's obligation; the nature of the debt and the wording of the deferment determine the effect.

Refutation of al-Hasiri and Rulings on Rescission in Kafalah
Critiquing al-Hasiri's view and discussing rescission effects on guarantee.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the majority Hanafi position on deferment in loan guarantee and identify the effect of rescission on guarantee according to different views.

Core Idea
Deferment of a loan guarantee applies only to the guarantor, not the principal – al-Hasiri's contrary view is rejected.

This passage addresses a strong intra-Hanafi dispute: does deferring a guaranteed loan also defer the principal's liability? The majority, led by al-Quduri, firmly reject al-Hasiri's claim that it does. Additionally, the text explores the consequences of rescission (faskh) on the guarantor's obligation, revealing a nuanced difference of opinion among later Hanafi authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Focus on the first part: whether the deferment (ajal) in a loan guarantee applies only to the guarantor or also to the principal (asil). Al-Hasiri says it extends; the majority says it does not.
The majority includes al-Quduri and all relied-upon books.
2
Assess the authority of each position
Note that the majority relies on multiple authoritative texts (Sharh Mukhtasar al-Karkhi, al-Muhit, etc.), while al-Hasiri's view is isolated and rejected by all. The passage calls it a 'mistake' (khatta') not permissible to act upon.
Some judges ruled by it without knowing its origin.
3
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is the majority view: the principal's debt remains immediately due. Fatwa is not given according to al-Hasiri.
The practical consequence: the principal can be sued immediately.
4
Analyze the rescission dispute
The second part deals with faskh (rescission) – e.g., returning a defective item by court decree. There is a disagreement: al-Ramli says full rescission revives the guarantee term but not the guarantee itself; al-Muhit says the guarantor is discharged regardless; al-Sighnaqi distinguishes between court decree (guarantor liable) and mutual consent (discharged).
The text ends with a problematic correction from al-Ghazzi, indicating unresolved differences.
5
Recognize the unresolved nature
The passage ends abruptly, but it is clear that there is valid ikhtilaf among Hanafi scholars on the effect of rescission. The student should note that later texts (e.g., al-Tatarkhaniyya) contain conflicting quotes.
This alerts the mufti to check multiple sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وللطرسوسي كلام الخ) حيث نقل أولاً عن شرح مختصر الكرخي للقدوري وعن المحيط وخزانة الأكمال وشرح التكملة وغيرها مثل ما في التتارخانية ثم قال : فتحرر لنا من هذا كله أن الكفالة بالقرض إلى أجل تصح وتكون مؤجلة على الكفيل وحده وعلى الأصيل حال كما كان ولا يلتفت إلى ما قاله الحصيري من قوله في التحرير إذا كفل بالقرض إلى أجل يتأجل على الأصيل، وهذه الحيلة في تأجيل القرض فإن كل الكتب ترد ذلك ولم يقل هذه العبارة أحد غيره وإذا دار الأمر بين أن يفتي بما قاله الحصيري وحده أو بما قاله القدوري وكل الأصحاب فلا يفتي إلا بما قاله القدوري وبقية الأصحاب ولا يفتي بما قاله الحصيري ولا يجوز أن يعمل به، وكان بعض القضاة يحكم بما قاله الحصيري من غير أن يعرف أن الحصيري ذكره وإنما كان يقول سمعنا ذلك . أنه هو الحيلة في تأجيل القرض وهو خطأ لا يجوز أن يعمل به قوله : (وبالفسخ من كل وجه يعود على الكفيل الخ) قال الرملي قدم في الإقالة عن الصغرى ولو رده بعيب بقضاء كان فسخاً من كل وجه فيعود الأجل كما كان، ولو كان بالدين كفيل لا تعود الكفالة في الوجهين اهـ. فهو مخالف لما هنا فتأمل وأقول : أعقب هذا في التتارخانية بنقول مخالفة لهذا فنقل عن المحيط أنه يبرأ الكفيل سواء كان الرد بعيب بقضاء أو برضا وما ذكره في هذا الشرح العتابية، ونقل بعده عن السغناقي عن المبسوط التفصيل بين الرد بالقضاء فيعود على الكفيل وبين الرد بالرضا فلا يعود الحاصل أن فيها خلافاً بينهم تنبه قوله : (وأما ما عليه بالكفالة يبقى مؤجلاً هو الصحيح) قال الغزي : هذا التصحيح مشكل فإن المنصوص عليه في الكتب المعتمدة وذكره المصنف المشايخ عنه نقله عن من الفتاوى
His statement: 'And for al-Tarsusi there is a discussion' where he first quoted from Sharh Mukhtasar al-Karkhi by al-Quduri, from al-Muhit, Khizanat al-Akmal, Sharh al-Takmila, and others, similar to what is in al-Tatarkhaniyya. Then he said: From all this it is established that the guarantee of a loan with a term is valid and is deferred only on the guarantor, and the principal remains liable immediately as before, and no attention is paid to what al-Hasiri said in al-Tahrir: 'If he guarantees a loan with a term, it becomes deferred for the principal.' And this is the stratagem for deferring loans, but all books reject this, and no one else said this statement. When the matter comes down to either giving fatwa according to al-Hasiri alone or according to al-Quduri and all the companions, fatwa is only given according to al-Quduri and the rest of the companions, and not according to al-Hasiri, and it is not permissible to act upon it. And some judges used to rule according to al-Hasiri without knowing that al-Hasiri mentioned it; they would say 'we heard that it is the stratagem for deferring loans,' but it is a mistake and not permissible to act upon. His statement: 'And by rescission (faskh) from every aspect, the debt returns upon the guarantor' – al-Ramli said: It was presented in al-Iqala from al-Sughra: If he returned it due to a defect by court decree, it is a rescission from every aspect and the term returns as it was. And if there was a guarantor for the debt, the guarantee does not return in either of the two aspects. So it contradicts what is here, so reflect. And I say: This was followed in al-Tatarkhaniyya by quotes contradicting this, for it is narrated from al-Muhit that the guarantor is discharged whether the return was due to a defect by court decree or by mutual consent. And what is mentioned in this sharh is from al-'Utabiyya. And after that, it is narrated from al-Sighnaqi from al-Mabsut the distinction: if the return is by court decree, then the debt returns on the guarantor; if by mutual consent, it does not return. The result is that there is a difference of opinion among them, so be alert. His statement: 'And what is owed by guarantee remains deferred is the correct view.' al-Ghazzi said: This correction is problematic, for what is stipulated in the relied-upon books and mentioned by the author is...' (text cuts off).
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage addresses two main issues. First, it strongly refutes the view of al-Hasiri (a Hanafi scholar) who claimed that if a loan is guaranteed with a deferred term, the deferment extends to the principal. The passage asserts that the correct position, held by al-Quduri and the majority of Hanafi scholars, is that the deferment applies only to the guarantor, and the principal's debt remains due immediately. The passage criticizes some judges who ignorantly applied al-Hasiri's view, considering it a stratagem (hila) to defer loans, but this is rejected. Second, it discusses the effect of rescission (faskh) of a sale on the guarantee. There is a difference of opinion: according to al-Ramli, if a defective item is returned by court decree, it is a full rescission and the term returns, but the guarantee does not revive. However, other sources like al-Muhit say the guarantor is discharged in any case, while al-Sighnaqi distinguishes between return by decree (guarantor liable) and by consent (not liable). The passage ends with a note that the ruling that a guaranteed debt remains deferred is problematic according to al-Ghazzi, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract, here referring to return of a defective item.
أجل (ajal): Term or deferment period in a loan contract.
الحيلة (al-hila): Stratagem or legal device to achieve a result indirectly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority refuting al-Hasiri, what is the ruling when a loan is guaranteed with a deferred term?
A) The principal's debt is also deferred.
B) The principal's debt remains immediately due.
C) The guarantee is invalid.
D) The guarantor is discharged immediately.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi kafalah, deferment of a guaranteed loan benefits only the guarantor; the principal's debt remains due immediately.
