8 min
Rulings on Kafalah: Sale, Rescission, and Co-Guarantors
States multiple rulings on kafalah with deferred debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and state the Hanafi ruling for each scenario involving a deferred debt under kafalah, including sale, rescission, and co-guarantors.

Core Idea
The guarantor's term and liability are independent of the principal's; early payment or death does not transfer the term.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a series of concrete rulings on kafalah with a deferred debt. Each scenario—sale by the guarantor, rescission, annulment of hawala, and co-guarantors—has a distinct legal outcome. Your task is to parse each rule and understand the underlying principle: the guarantor's obligations are separate from the principal's, and the term is personal to each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first scenario: sale by the guarantor
Read the opening sentence: If the guarantor sells something to the creditor for the debt before the term, what happens? The text says 'sutat' (the debt is discharged).
This is a straightforward discharge of the debt.
2
Contrast with rescission and hawala
If the sale is rescinded by mutual consent, the debt returns but the term does not. However, if a hawala (assignment) is annulled due to delay, the term returns. Note the distinction.
The type of annulment affects whether the term revives.
3
Analyze sale by the principal
If the principal himself sells to the creditor for his debt, the debt is also discharged. But if he returns a new property, the debt revives only against the principal, not the guarantor.
The guarantor is not liable if the principal reacquires the debt through a new property.
4
Examine 'rescission from every aspect'
If the rescission is complete (e.g., by court order due to a defect), the debt returns on the guarantor as well, reviving the original term.
This type of rescission treats the sale as if it never happened, restoring all original rights.
5
Apply co-guarantor rules and death scenarios
If co-guarantors have different terms and one pays when his term ends, he recovers from the principal only half; later, both cooperate. Death does not accelerate the term for the other party.
Each guarantor's term is personal; death ends only that person's term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كفل بدين مؤجل ثم باعه الكفيل شيئاً بالدين قبل حلوله سقط، ولو قال البيع أو رد بالتراضي عاد الدين ولم يعد الأجل، ولو انفسخت الحوالة بالتوي عاد الأجل، وكذا لو باع الأصيل الطالب بدينه سقط فلو رد عليه بملك جديد عاد الدين على الأصيل ولم يعد على الكفيل، وبالفسخ من كل وجه يعود على الكفيل. ولو كان الأجل لأحد الكفيلين أكثر فحل على الآخر وأدى رجع على الأصيل حتى يحل على الآخر أو يرجع الآخر بنصفه ثم يتبعان الأصيل بالنصف ا هـ. وإذا لم يكن تأجيل الكفيل تأجيلاً للأصيل فإذا أدى الكفيل قبل مضي الأجل لا رجوع له على الأصيل حتى يمضي الأجل باتفاق الروايات، وكذا إذا حل على الكفيل بموته لا يحل على الأصيل، وكذا إذا حل على الأصيل بموته لا يحل على الكفيل
If one guarantees a deferred debt and the guarantor sells something to the creditor for the debt before its term, the debt is discharged. If the sale is rescinded or returned by mutual consent, the debt returns but the term does not. If the hawala is annulled due to delay, the term returns. Similarly, if the principal sells to the creditor for his debt, it is discharged; if he returns a new property, the debt returns on the principal but not on the guarantor. With rescission from every aspect, it returns on the guarantor. If the term for one of two co-guarantors is longer and it becomes due on the other, he pays and then claims recourse from the principal until the other's term ends or the other claims half, then both pursue the principal for half. If the term of the guarantor is not a term for the principal, and the guarantor pays before the term ends, he has no recourse against the principal until the term ends by agreement of all narrations. Similarly, if it becomes due on the guarantor by his death, it does not become due on the principal; and if it becomes due on the principal by his death, it does not become due on the guarantor.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage presents multiple rulings on kafalah (suretyship) involving a deferred debt. First: If the guarantor sells something to the creditor in lieu of the debt before the term, the debt is discharged. Second: If that sale is annulled by agreement, the debt revives but the original term does not; however, if a hawala (assignment) is annulled, the term returns. Third: If the principal sells to the creditor for his debt, the debt is similarly discharged, but if he returns a new property, the debt revives only against the principal, not the guarantor. Fourth: If the rescission is from every aspect (e.g., by court order), then the debt revives against the guarantor. Fifth: If two co-guarantors have different terms and one pays, he can claim recourse from the principal only up to half, and both share the principal's liability. Sixth: The guarantor's term does not benefit the principal; if the guarantor pays early, he cannot claim reimbursement until the original term ends. Seventh: Death of the guarantor or principal does not accelerate the term for the other party. These rulings show the Hanafi principle that the guarantor's obligations are separate from the principal's.

Key Terms
الفسخ من كل وجه (al-faskh min kulli wajh): Rescission that is complete and irreversible, such as by court order due to defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a guarantor sells something to the creditor for the debt before the term, what happens?
A) The debt becomes due immediately.
B) The debt is discharged.
C) The term is extended.
D) The principal is released.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi kafalah, the guarantor's term and death do not affect the principal's obligations, and types of rescission determine whether the original term revives.

6 min
The Hila of Kafalah to Defer a Loan
Explains two scenarios for deferring a loan via kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two ways of structuring a kafalah to defer a loan and apply the ruling on the term for the guarantor and the principal.

Core Idea
The term in kafalah binds only the guarantor if he attributes it to himself.

The problem of deferring an immediate loan without riba arises frequently. The Hanafi school provides a legal device using kafalah. This passage from al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya explains two scenarios that determine whether the term applies only to the guarantor or also to the principal. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly structuring such transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base scenario
A has a debt due immediately to B. C guarantees the debt for a fixed term (e.g., one year).
2
Scenario A: Guarantor attributes term to himself
If C says 'ajjilni' (grant me a term), the term applies only to C. B cannot claim from C until the term ends. A remains immediately liable.
This is the intended hila: the creditor effectively defers payment without deferring the principal's obligation.
3
Scenario B: Guarantor does not attribute term to himself
If C simply mentions the term without attributing it to himself (e.g., 'I guarantee it for one year') and B agrees, the term applies to both C and A.
In this case, both the guarantor and principal benefit from the deferment.
4
Determine the ruling
The kafalah is valid in both cases. The difference lies in who is bound by the term: only the guarantor in Scenario A, both in Scenario B.
The practical result is that in Scenario A, the principal's debt remains technically immediate, but the creditor has agreed not to demand payment before the term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقل بعض الفضلاء عن الفتاوى الهندية تفصيلاً فقال: إذا كان لرجل على رجل ألف حالة درهم من ثمن مبيع فكفل بها رجل إلى سنة فهذا على وجهين: إن أضاف الكفيل الأجل إلى نفسه بأن قال أجلني ثبت الأجل في حق الكفيل وحده، وإذا لم يضف الأجل إلى نفسه بل ذكر ورضي به الطالب ثبت الأجل في حق الكفيل والأصيل جميعاً اهـ. فتأمل لعلك تحظى بالتوفيق. قوله: (وللطرسوسي كلام الخ) حيث نقل أولاً عن شرح مختصر الكرخي للقدوري وعن المحيط وخزانة الأكمال وشرح التكملة وغيرها مثل ما في التتارخانية ثم قال: فتحرر لنا من هذا كله أن الكفالة بالقرض إلى أجل تصح وتكون مؤجلة على الكفيل وحده وعلى الأصيل حال كما كان ولا يلتفت إلى ما قاله الحصيري من قوله في التحرير إذا كفل بالقرض إلى أجل يتأجل على الأصيل، وهذه الحيلة في تأجيل القرض فإن كل الكتب ترد ذلك ولم يقل هذه العبارة أحد غيره وإذا دار الأمر بين أن يفتي بما قاله الحصيري وحده أو بما قاله القدوري وكل الأصحاب فلا يفتي إلا بما قاله القدوري وبقية الأصحاب ولا يفتي بما قاله الحصيري ولا يجوز أن يعمل به، وكان بعض القضاة يحكم بما قاله الحصيري من غير أن يعرف أن الحصيري ذكره وإنما كان يقول سمعنا ذلك أنه هو الحيلة في تأجيل القرض وهو خطأ لا يجوز أن يعمل به
Some virtuous person transmitted from al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya a detailed explanation: If a man has an immediate debt of one thousand dirhams from a sale price, and another man guarantees it for one year, this is two ways: If the guarantor attributes the term to himself by saying 'grant me a term', then the term is established only for the guarantor; if he does not attribute the term to himself but mentions it and the creditor agrees, the term is established for both the guarantor and the principal. Reflect, perhaps you will attain success. His saying: (And al-Tarsusi has discussion etc.) when he first transmitted from the commentary of Mukhtasar al-Karkhi by al-Quduri, and from al-Muhit, Khizanat al-Akmal, Sharh al-Takmila, etc. like what is in al-Tatar Khaniyya, then he said: It is established for us from all of this that kafalah for a loan to a term is valid and is deferred upon the guarantor alone, while upon the principal it remains immediate as before; and no attention is paid to what al-Hasiri said in his saying in al-Tahrir: if one guarantees a loan to a term, it is deferred upon the principal. This is the legal device for deferring a loan, for all the books reject that, and no one else said that phrase. And if the matter is between giving fatwa according to al-Hasiri alone or according to al-Quduri and all the companions, one should only give fatwa according to al-Quduri and the rest of the companions, and not give fatwa according to al-Hasiri, and it is not permissible to act upon it. And some judges used to rule according to al-Hasiri without knowing that al-Hasiri mentioned it; rather they used to say 'we heard that it is the legal device for deferring a loan', which is an error not permissible to act upon.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific legal device (hila) for effectively deferring a loan through kafalah. The problem: a loan due immediately cannot be deferred by agreement (since that would be riba). The solution: have a third party guarantee the debt to a future term. The issue: does this term benefit the principal as well? According to the majority, including al-Quduri, the term only applies to the guarantor, so the principal remains immediately liable but the creditor agrees not to demand from him (practically deferring payment). Al-Hasiri argued that the term also applies to the principal. The author strongly rejects al-Hasiri's view, stating that it is an error and not permissible to act upon. The passage quotes from multiple authoritative sources that support the majority view: the kafalah is valid, the term is only for the guarantor, and the principal's debt remains immediate. The legal device works because the creditor claims from the guarantor at the term, who then claims from the principal immediately but only after the term ends.

Key Terms
الحيلة (al-hila): A legal device to achieve a permissible outcome that would otherwise be prohibited, here deferring a loan without riba.
أجلني (ajjilni): 'Grant me a term' - the phrase used by the guarantor to attribute the term to himself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first scenario, when the guarantor says 'ajjilni', the term applies to:
A) The guarantor only.
B) The principal only.
C) Both.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
In kafalah, the term applies only to the guarantor if he explicitly attributes it to himself; otherwise it applies to both.

8 min
Rejecting al-Hasiri's View on Kafalah Term
Rejects al-Hasiri's deviant opinion and affirms the majority view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Hasiri's view, why the majority rejects it, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Al-Hasiri's view that the kafalah term benefits the principal is an isolated error; the majority view is the only valid basis for fatwa.

In this passage, the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq confronts an intra-Hanafi dispute. Al-Hasiri claimed that when a loan is guaranteed to a term, the principal also gets the deferment. The author firmly rejects this, citing numerous authoritative sources. This lesson explores the grounds for rejection and the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Hasiri's claim
Al-Hasiri in al-Tahrir stated: if one guarantees a loan to a term, it is deferred upon the principal as well.
This would mean the principal's debt becomes deferred, not immediate.
2
Note the majority response
All major books (al-Quduri, al-Muhit, etc.) reject this. They hold the term applies only to the guarantor; the principal remains immediate.
The author calls this the 'legal device' (hila) for deferring a loan.
3
Understand the tarjih
If forced to choose between al-Hasiri alone and al-Quduri with all companions, the only permissible choice is al-Quduri's view. It is not even permissible to act on al-Hasiri's view.
This is a strong condemnation: the view is not just weak but invalid for practice.
4
Recognize the practical error
Some judges ruled according to al-Hasiri's view mistakenly, thinking it was the accepted hila. This is an error not permissible to act upon.
This shows the danger of relying on a shadhdh (isolated) opinion without proper verification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقل بعض الفضلاء عن الفتاوى الهندية تفصيلاً فقال: إذا كان لرجل على رجل ألف حالة درهم من ثمن مبيع فكفل بها رجل إلى سنة فهذا على وجهين: إن أضاف الكفيل الأجل إلى نفسه بأن قال أجلني ثبت الأجل في حق الكفيل وحده، وإذا لم يضف الأجل إلى نفسه بل ذكر ورضي به الطالب ثبت الأجل في حق الكفيل والأصيل جميعاً اهـ. فتأمل لعلك تحظى بالتوفيق. قوله: (وللطرسوسي كلام الخ) حيث نقل أولاً عن شرح مختصر الكرخي للقدوري وعن المحيط وخزانة الأكمال وشرح التكملة وغيرها مثل ما في التتارخانية ثم قال: فتحرر لنا من هذا كله أن الكفالة بالقرض إلى أجل تصح وتكون مؤجلة على الكفيل وحده وعلى الأصيل حال كما كان ولا يلتفت إلى ما قاله الحصيري من قوله في التحرير إذا كفل بالقرض إلى أجل يتأجل على الأصيل، وهذه الحيلة في تأجيل القرض فإن كل الكتب ترد ذلك ولم يقل هذه العبارة أحد غيره وإذا دار الأمر بين أن يفتي بما قاله الحصيري وحده أو بما قاله القدوري وكل الأصحاب فلا يفتي إلا بما قاله القدوري وبقية الأصحاب ولا يفتي بما قاله الحصيري ولا يجوز أن يعمل به، وكان بعض القضاة يحكم بما قاله الحصيري من غير أن يعرف أن الحصيري ذكره وإنما كان يقول سمعنا ذلك أنه هو الحيلة في تأجيل القرض وهو خطأ لا يجوز أن يعمل به
Some virtuous person transmitted from al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya a detailed explanation: If a man has an immediate debt of one thousand dirhams from a sale price, and another man guarantees it for one year, this is two ways: If the guarantor attributes the term to himself by saying 'grant me a term', then the term is established only for the guarantor; if he does not attribute the term to himself but mentions it and the creditor agrees, the term is established for both the guarantor and the principal. Reflect, perhaps you will attain success. His saying: (And al-Tarsusi has discussion etc.) when he first transmitted from the commentary of Mukhtasar al-Karkhi by al-Quduri, and from al-Muhit, Khizanat al-Akmal, Sharh al-Takmila, etc. like what is in al-Tatar Khaniyya, then he said: It is established for us from all of this that kafalah for a loan to a term is valid and is deferred upon the guarantor alone, while upon the principal it remains immediate as before; and no attention is paid to what al-Hasiri said in his saying in al-Tahrir: if one guarantees a loan to a term, it is deferred upon the principal. This is the legal device for deferring a loan, for all the books reject that, and no one else said that phrase. And if the matter is between giving fatwa according to al-Hasiri alone or according to al-Quduri and all the companions, one should only give fatwa according to al-Quduri and the rest of the companions, and not give fatwa according to al-Hasiri, and it is not permissible to act upon it. And some judges used to rule according to al-Hasiri without knowing that al-Hasiri mentioned it; rather they used to say 'we heard that it is the legal device for deferring a loan', which is an error not permissible to act upon.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific legal device (hila) for effectively deferring a loan through kafalah. The problem: a loan due immediately cannot be deferred by agreement (since that would be riba). The solution: have a third party guarantee the debt to a future term. The issue: does this term benefit the principal as well? The legal device works because the creditor claims from the guarantor at the term, who then claims from the principal immediately but only after the term ends.

Key Terms
الحيل (al-hiyal): Plural of hila; legal devices to achieve permissible outcomes while avoiding prohibitions.
الحصيري (al-Hasiri): A Hanafi scholar who held a minority view that the term in kafalah applies to the principal.
القدوري (al-Quduri): A leading Hanafi scholar (d. 428 AH), author of Mukhtasar al-Karkhi commentary, whose view is authoritative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which source is the main authority for the correct view?
A) al-Hasiri's al-Tahrir.
B) al-Quduri's commentary.
C) al-Tatar Khaniyya.
D) al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya.

Key Takeaway
The majority Hanafi view (al-Quduri and all companions) is that the term in kafalah only benefits the guarantor; al-Hasiri's opposing view is an isolated error not to be followed.

10 min
Effects of Settlement (Sulh) on the Guarantor and Principal
Expands on the effects of sulh on guarantor and principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the effect of sulh between the creditor and either the principal or the guarantor, and distinguish when the principal is released or not.

Core Idea
Settlement with the guarantor does not automatically release the principal unless the settlement specifically includes the debt.

When a debt is owed by a principal with a guarantor, a settlement (sulh) between the creditor and one of them can have complex effects on the other. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi rulings on who is released and who retains liability, as explained by Ibn Nujaym in al-Bahr al-Raiq. Understanding this will help you apply the rules of kafalah and sulh correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the nature of the debt
Note that the debt is on the principal, and the guarantor is jointly liable. The settlement is between the creditor and either the principal or the guarantor.
2
Determine the type of settlement
Check whether the settlement is for the same genus (e.g., cash for cash) or a different genus (e.g., commods). This changes the legal treatment: same genus is treated as a partial release (ibra), different genus as a sale.
3
Check for stipulations
If the guarantor settles, he may stipulate release of both himself and the principal, or only himself. If no stipulation, both are released from the amount paid. If only himself, the principal remains fully liable.
4
Apply the ruling on recourse
The guarantor can claim recourse from the principal for the amount he paid if the guarantee was by the principal's order. Otherwise, he cannot.
5
Distinguish settlement for the claim vs settlement for the debt
If the settlement is specifically for releasing the guarantor from the claim (i.e., rescinding the guarantee), the principal is not released. This is different from a settlement that pays part of the debt.
This distinction is crucial and often misunderstood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو صالح أحدهما رب المال عن ألف على نصفه برنا) أي صالح الأصيل أو الكفيل الطالب على نفس الدين برئ الكفيل والأصيل، أما إذا صالح الأصيل فظاهر لأنه بالصلح يبرأ وبراءته توجب براءة الكفيل، وأما إذا صالح الكفيل فلأنه أضافه إلى الألف الدين وهي على الأصيل فبرئ عن خمسمائة، فبراءته توجب براءة الكفيل، ثم برئا جميعاً عن خمسمائة بأداء الكفيل ويرجع على الأصيل بخمسمائة إن كانت الكفالة بأمره بخلاف ما إذا صالح على جنس آخر لكونه مبادلة فملكه فرجع بالألف. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا شرط الكفيل براءتهما أو براءة الأصيل أو لم يشرط شيئاً، وأما إذا شرط براءة الكفيل وحده برئ دون الأصيل؛ هكذا ذكر الشارح وليس المراد أن الطالب يأخذ البدل في مقابلة إبراء الكفيل عنها، وإنما المراد أن ما أخذه من الكفيل محسوب من أصل دينه ويرجع بالباقي على الأصيل قال في الهداية : ولو كان صالحه عما استوجب من الكفالة لا يبرأ الأصيل لأن هذا إبراء الكفيل عن المطالبة ا هـ . قال في النهاية أي ما وجب بالكفالة وهو المطالبة صورته ما في المبسوط : لو صالحة على مائة درهم على أن إبراء الكفيل خاصة من الباقي رجع الكفيل على الأصيل بمائة الطالب على الأصيل بتسعمائة لأن إبراء الكفيل يكون فسخاً للكفالة ولا ورجع يكون إسقاطاً لأصل الدين ا هـ . وهكذا في فتح القدير وقال قبله وإن شرط براءة الكفيل
His saying: (And if one of them reaches a settlement with the owner of the property for one thousand upon half of it [unclear]), i.e., the principal or the guarantor settles with the creditor for the same debt, the guarantor and the principal are released. As for if the principal settles, it is apparent because he is released by the settlement and his release necessitates the release of the guarantor. As for if the guarantor settles, because he adds to the thousand of the debt which is upon the principal, he is released from five hundred, so his release necessitates the release of the guarantor. Then both are released from five hundred by the guarantor's payment, and he claims recourse from the principal for five hundred if the guarantee was by his order, contrary to if he settled for another genus because it is an exchange, so he owns it and claims recourse for the thousand. He released it absolutely, so it includes whether the guarantor stipulated the release of both or the release of the principal or stipulated nothing. But if he stipulated the release of the guarantor alone, only the guarantor is released without the principal. Thus the commentator mentioned. And the intent is not that the creditor takes the substitute in exchange for releasing the guarantor from it; rather the intent is that what he took from the guarantor is counted from the original debt, and he claims the remainder from the principal. He said in al-Hidaya: If he settled with him for what is due from the guarantee, the principal is not released, because this is a release of the guarantor from the claim. He said in al-Nihaya: That is what is due by the guarantee, i.e., the claim. Its form is what is in al-Mabsut: If he settled with him for one hundred dirhams on condition of releasing the guarantor specifically from the remainder, the guarantor claims recourse from the principal for one hundred, and the creditor claims from the principal for nine hundred, because releasing the guarantor is a rescission of the guarantee, and the return is not a cancellation of the original debt. And similarly in Fath al-Qadir, and he said before: And if he stipulates the release of the guarantor...
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage deals with the effects of sulh (reconciliation) between the creditor and either the principal or the guarantor. The key scenario: two parties owe a debt of 1000, but one of them (the guarantor or principal) settles with the creditor for half (500) of the same debt type. If the principal settles, both are released because the principal's release automatically releases the guarantor. If the guarantor settles, the guarantor is released from half, but the principal remains liable for the other half; the guarantor can claim recourse from the principal for the amount paid if he was a guarantor at the principal's request. However, if the settlement is for a different type of property (e.g., a commodity instead of cash), it is treated as a sale, so the guarantor owns that commodity and claims the full debt from the principal. The author also discusses the distinction: if the settlement is specifically for releasing the guarantor from the claim (not the debt itself), the principal is not released. The text from al-Hidaya and al-Mabsut illustrates that a settlement that releases the guarantor from the claim is effectively a rescission of the guarantee, so the guarantor can still claim from the principal what he paid, but the creditor can claim the remainder from the principal.

Key Terms
الصّلح (al-sulh): A reconciliation contract where the creditor agrees to accept part of the debt or a substitute to settle the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the guarantor settles with the creditor for half the debt (same type) without stipulating anything, what happens?
A) Both the guarantor and principal are released from the entire debt.
B) The guarantor is released from half, the principal remains liable for the other half.
C) The guarantor is fully released, and the principal is released from half.
D) The guarantor remains liable for the full debt.

Key Takeaway
Sulh with the guarantor for part of the debt (same type) releases both from that portion, but the principal remains liable for the rest unless the settlement specifically releases him.

Does the Guarantor's Death Accelerate the Debt?
Ruling on whether the death of the guarantor accelerates the term of the guaranteed debt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the majority Hanafi position on the effect of the guarantor's death on the term of the guaranteed debt and explain the distinction between the creditor's claim and the guarantor's right of recourse.

Core Idea
The guarantor's death makes the debt due from his estate, but his heirs cannot seek recourse until the original term expires.

This lesson examines a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the consequences of a guarantor's death on a deferred debt. While it is agreed that the creditor can demand payment from the guarantor's estate immediately, the key disagreement concerns whether the guarantor's heirs can immediately seek reimbursement from the principal debtor. We will explore the majority opinion of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, and the dissenting view of Imam Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is the death of the guarantor. The ruling concerns the effect on the debt's term and the right of recourse.
Distinguish between the obligation to pay the creditor and the right to seek reimbursement.
2
Distinguish two separate rights
The creditor's right to claim from the estate versus the guarantor's right of recourse against the principal. These are distinct legal relationships.
The term 'ruju'' refers specifically to the guarantor's right to reclaim from the principal.
3
Follow the majority's reasoning
Why does the term drop for the creditor but not for recourse? Because the guarantor committed to a deferred debt, his right of recourse was also deferred.
The heirs step into the guarantor's shoes and cannot claim what he could not.
4
Examine Zufar's objection
Imam Zufar holds that the term drops entirely, including for recourse, because the guarantor's death nullifies the deferment.
This is a minority intra-Hanafi view.
5
Apply the analogy from the principal's death
The passage uses an analogy: if the principal drops the term during his life, it drops for him but not for the guarantor. Similarly, when the principal dies, the term drops for him but not for the guarantor.
Reinforces the principle that the term's removal by death is personal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أيضاً أن المال المكفول به يحل بموت الكفيل، ومقتضاه أن يكون ما عليه بالكفالة حالاً أيضاً وإن لم يحل على الأصيل. قال شيخ الإسلام عبد البر في شرح الوهبانية : فإن كان المال المكفول مؤجلاً في المبسوط أنه يحل بموت الكفيل ويؤخذ من تركته ولا ترجع الورثة على المكفول حتى يحل الأجل، وفي المجمع أن زفر يقول : إن ورثة الكفيل يرجعون في الحال ويسقط اعتبار الأجل اهـ. وفي الولوالجية : ولو مات الكفيل قبل الأجل حل عليه لأن الأجل يسقط بموت من له الأجل، فإن أدى ورثته لم يرجعوا على المطلوب إلا إلى أجله لأن الكفيل إنما يستحق الرجوع على الأصيل بالتزامه وقد التزم الدين مؤجلاً فلا يستحق الرجوع بالدين معجلاً ولا تقوم الورثة مقامه في الرجوع، فلو مات المطلوب قبل أجله حل عليه ولم يحل على الكفيل أما الأصيل فلأنه مات من له الأجل، وأما الكفيل فلأنه لو أسقط الأصيل في حياته الأجل يسقط في حقه ولا يسقط في حق الكفيل لأنه يريد أن يلزم الكفيل زيادة لم يلتزمها الكفيل فكذا إذا سقط الأجل بموته اهـ ؛ كذا في حاشية الرملي.
Also, the guaranteed wealth becomes due upon the death of the guarantor, and its implication is that what is upon him by the guarantee becomes due as well, even if it has not become due upon the principal. Shaykh al-Islam Abd al-Barr said in Sharh al-Wahbaniyyah: If the guaranteed wealth was deferred, it is stated in al-Mabsut that it becomes due upon the death of the guarantor and is taken from his estate, and the heirs do not have recourse against the guaranteed person until the term expires. In al-Majma', Zufar says: The heirs of the guarantor have recourse immediately, and consideration of the term is dropped. End quote. In al-Walwaljiyyah: If the guarantor dies before the term, it becomes due upon him because the term drops by the death of the one who has the term [i.e., the creditor?]. If his heirs pay, they do not have recourse against the debtor except at his term, because the guarantor is only entitled to recourse against the principal by his commitment, and he committed to the debt as deferred, so he is not entitled to recourse for the debt immediately, and the heirs do not stand in his place for recourse. If the debtor dies before his term, it becomes due upon him but does not become due upon the guarantor. As for the principal, because the one who had the term died. As for the guarantor, because if the principal had dropped the term during his life, it would drop in his right but not drop in the right of the guarantor, because he would want to impose upon the guarantor an increase he did not commit to; so also when the term drops by his death. End quote. Thus in the marginalia of al-Ramli.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of whether the death of the guarantor (kafil) causes the guaranteed debt to become due immediately, even if it was originally deferred (mu'ajjal). The author states that the guaranteed wealth does become due upon the guarantor's death, meaning it can be collected from his estate. However, a key point is that the guarantor's heirs, if they pay the debt from the estate, cannot immediately seek recourse (ruju') against the principal debtor (matlub/asil); they must wait until the original term expires. This is because the guarantor's right of recourse was based on his commitment to a deferred debt, and the heirs step into his shoes only as to what he was entitled to. The passage then presents a difference of opinion: Imam Zufar holds that the heirs can have recourse immediately, disregarding the term. The author cites al-Walwaljiyyah to support the majority position that the term remains for recourse. Additionally, if the principal debtor dies before the term, the debt becomes due on him (and his estate), but it does not become due on the guarantor—i.e., the creditor cannot demand immediate payment from the guarantor if the principal dies. This is because the term's removal due to death affects only the person who died, not the guarantor, who committed to a deferred obligation. The reasoning is analogous to the case where the principal drops the term during his life: it drops for him but not for the guarantor. This lesson clarifies the nuanced Hanafi position on the interaction between death, term, and recourse in guarantee contracts.

Key Terms
أجل (ajal): The specified term or due date of a deferred debt.
رجوع (ruju'): Recourse; the right of the guarantor (or his heirs) to claim reimbursement from the principal debtor after paying the debt.
تركة (tarika): Estate; the property left by a deceased person, from which debts are paid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view, if the guarantor dies before the deferred debt's term expires, what happens?
A) The debt becomes due immediately on the guarantor's estate, and his heirs can seek immediate recourse from the principal.
B) The debt remains deferred on the guarantor's estate until the term expires.
C) The debt becomes due on the guarantor's estate, but his heirs must wait until the original term to seek recourse from the principal.
D) The debt is cancelled entirely because the guarantor died.

Key Takeaway
The guarantor's death accelerates the debt for the creditor but not for the guarantor's right of recourse, which remains deferred.

Compromise by the Principal or Guarantor for Part of the Debt
Ruling on compromise for part of the debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when the principal or guarantor compromises for half the debt and explain the effect on both parties' liability and the guarantor's right of recourse.

Core Idea
A compromise for part of the debt frees both the principal and guarantor from that part, with recourse depending on the guarantor's authorization.

When a debt is owed, either the principal debtor or his guarantor may reach a compromise with the creditor, agreeing to pay a portion in full settlement. This lesson explains the Hanafi ruling on such a compromise and its consequences for the liability of both parties and the guarantor's ability to seek reimbursement from the principal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compromise
Recognize that the principal or guarantor is compromising with the creditor for a portion of the debt (e.g., half of 1000 dirhams). The compromise is a binding contract that discharges the debtor to that extent.
The compromise must be on the debt itself, not on the right of recourse against the guarantor.
2
Determine who makes the compromise
If the principal compromises, his freedom from that portion necessarily frees the guarantor, since the guarantor's liability is subsidiary. If the guarantor compromises, he pays the portion and both are freed from that amount.
In both cases, the compromise is valid (sahih).
3
Analyze the effect on liability
After the compromise and payment of the agreed amount, the principal and guarantor are both discharged from that portion of the debt. The remaining debt (if any) is still owed by the principal.
If the compromise is for the entire debt, both are fully discharged.
4
Examine the guarantor's right of recourse
If the guarantor compromised and paid, he can recover the amount he paid from the principal only if the guarantee was made at the principal's request (bi-amrihi). If unauthorized, he has no recourse.
If the compromise was for a different genus (jins akhar), the guarantor is treated as having purchased the debt and can recover the full original amount.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو صالح أحدهما رب المال عن ألف على نصفه برئا) أي صالح الأصيل أو الكفيل الطالب على نفس الدين برئ الكفيل والأصيل، أما إذا صالح الأصيل فظاهر لأنه بالصلح يبرأ وبراءته توجب براءة الكفيل، وأما إذا صالح الكفيل فلأنه أضافه إلى الألف الدين وهي على الأصيل فبرئ عن خمسمائة، فبراءته توجب براءة الكفيل، ثم برئا جميعاً عن خمسمائة بأداء الكفيل ويرجع على الأصيل بخمسمائة إن كانت الكفالة بأمره بخلاف ما إذا صالح على جنس آخر لكونه مبادلة فملكه فرجع بالألف. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا شرط الكفيل براءتهما أو براءة الأصيل أو لم يشرط شيئاً، وأما إذا شرط براءة الكفيل وحده برئ دون الأصيل؛ هكذا ذكر الشارح وليس المراد أن الطالب يأخذ البدل في مقابلة إبراء الكفيل عنها، وإنما المراد أن ما أخذه من الكفيل محسوب من أصل دينه ويرجع بالباقي على الأصيل قال في الهداية : ولو كان صالحه عما استوجب من الكفالة لا يبرأ الأصيل لأن هذا إبراء الكفيل عن المطالبة ا هـ . قال في النهاية أي ما وجب بالكفالة وهو المطالبة صورته ما في المبسوط : لو صالحة على مائة درهم على أن إبراء الكفيل خاصة من الباقي رجع الكفيل على الأصيل بمائة والطالب على الأصيل بتسعمائة لأن إبراء الكفيل يكون فسخاً للكفالة ولا يكون إسقاطاً لأصل الدين ا هـ . وهكذا في فتح القدير وقال قبله وإن شرط براءة الكفيل وحده برئ الكفيل عن خمسمائة والألف بتمامها على الأصيل فيرجع الكفيل بخمسمائة إن كان بأمره والطالب بخمسمائة . ا هـ وفي التتارخانية : الكفيل إن كان بالنفس إذا صالح الطالب على خمسمائة دينار على أن أبرأه من الكفالة بالنفس لا يجوز ولا يبرأ عنها، فلو كان كفيلاً بالنفس والمال عن إنسان واحد وصالح على خمسين بالشرط برئ. ثم قال: الكفيل بالنفس إذا قضى الدين الذي على الأصيل على أنه يبرئه عن الكفالة ففعل جاز القضاء والإبراء، وأما إذا أعطاه عشرة ليبرئه عن الكفالة بالنفس فأبرأه لم يسلم له العوض باتفاق الروايات وفي براءته عنها روايتان ا هـ . وفي الخانية : لو صالح الكفيل الطالب على شيء يصح الصلح ولا يجب المال على الكفيل ا.هـ. وهو بإطلاقه شامل للكفالة بالمال والكفالة بالنفس
His statement: (And if one of them compromises with the owner of the wealth for a thousand [dirhams] for half of it, they both are free) meaning: the principal or the guarantor compromises with the claimant regarding the debt itself; the guarantor and the principal become free. As for if the principal compromises, it is apparent because he becomes free by the compromise, and his freedom necessitates the freedom of the guarantor. As for if the guarantor compromises, it is because he attributes [the compromise] to the thousand debt which is upon the principal, so he becomes free from five hundred; then his freedom necessitates the freedom of the guarantor. Then they both become free from five hundred by the payment of the guarantor, and he has recourse against the principal for five hundred if the guarantee was by his order, contrary to if he compromises for another genus because it is an exchange, so he owns it and he has recourse for the thousand. He (al-Marghinani) gave it absolutely, so it includes whether the guarantor stipulated the freedom of both, or the freedom of the principal, or he did not stipulate anything. As for if he stipulated the freedom of the guarantor alone, he becomes free without the principal. Thus mentioned the commentator. And the meaning is not that the claimant takes the substitute in exchange for the discharge of the guarantor from it; rather, the meaning is that what he took from the guarantor is counted from the original debt, and he has recourse for the remainder against the principal. He said in al-Hidayah: And if he compromised with him regarding what he became entitled to from the guarantee, the principal does not become free because this is a discharge of the guarantor from liability. End quote. He said in al-Nihayah: i.e., what became obligatory by the guarantee, which is liability. Its example is what is in al-Mabsut: If he compromised with him for one hundred dirhams on condition that he discharges the guarantor specifically from the remainder, the guarantor has recourse against the principal for one hundred, and the claimant has recourse against the principal for nine hundred, because discharging the guarantor is a rescission of the guarantee and is not a dropping of the original debt. End quote. And thus in Fath al-Qadir, and he said before it: And if he stipulated the freedom of the guarantor alone, the guarantor becomes free from five hundred, and the thousand entirely remains upon the principal, so the guarantor has recourse for five hundred if it was by his order, and the claimant has recourse for five hundred. End quote. And in al-Tatarkhaniyyah: The guarantor for the person—if he compromises with the claimant for five hundred dinars on condition that he discharges him from the guarantee for the person, it is not permissible and he does not become free from it. If he is a guarantor for both person and wealth for one person, and he compromises for fifty with the condition, he becomes free. Then he said: The guarantor for the person, if he pays the debt that is upon the principal on condition that he discharges him from the guarantee, and he does so, the payment and discharge are valid. But if he gives him ten to discharge him from the guarantee for the person, and he discharges him, the compensation is not validated by agreement of the narrations, and regarding his freedom from it there are two narrations. End quote. And in al-Khaniyyah: If the guarantor compromises with the claimant for something, the compromise is valid and the wealth is not obligatory upon the guarantor. End quote. And its absolute [wording] includes the guarantee for wealth and the guarantee for the person.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the ruling of a compromise (sulh) between the creditor (talib) and either the principal debtor (asil) or the guarantor (kafil) for a portion of the debt. The base text states: 'If one of them (the principal or the guarantor) compromises with the creditor for a thousand [dirhams] for half of it, both become free.' The commentator clarifies that this applies whether the compromise is made by the principal or the guarantor. If the principal compromises, his freedom from that portion naturally frees the guarantor as well, since the guarantor's obligation is subsidiary. If the guarantor compromises, he pays half the debt, and both are freed from that half; the guarantor then has recourse against the principal for that half only if the guarantee was made at the principal's request (bi-amrihi). If the compromise is for a different type of asset (jins akhar), it is treated as an exchange (mubadala), and the guarantor may have recourse for the full original amount. The commentator further explains that if the guarantor stipulates freedom only for himself, the principal remains liable for the remaining half. He then cites al-Hidayah and al-Nihayah to distinguish between compromising the debt itself (which frees the principal) versus compromising the right of recourse against the guarantor (which does not). Finally, he quotes al-Tatarkhaniyyah and al-Khaniyyah regarding the guarantor for the person (kafil bi'l-nafs), noting that a compromise to be freed from personal guarantee is invalid unless the guarantor also pays the debt, and that in general a compromise by the guarantor is valid without obligating him to pay if it is a pure discharge.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafala): Guarantee; a contract where one person (kafil) becomes surety for another's debt or presence.
صلح (sulh): Compromise; an agreement to settle a debt or claim for a part payment or other consideration.
براءة (bara'a): Freedom or discharge from liability; the state of being free from a debt or obligation.
أصيل (asil): Principal debtor; the person primarily liable for the debt.
كفيل (kafil): Guarantor; the person who guarantees the debt or presence of the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a guarantor compromises with the creditor for half the debt without stipulating anything, what is the effect on the principal?
A) The principal is freed only if the compromise was made with his consent.
B) Both the guarantor and principal are freed from that half of the debt.
C) The principal remains liable for the full debt because the compromise only frees the guarantor.
D) The guarantor has no right of recourse against the principal even if he pays.

Key Takeaway
A compromise for part of the debt frees both the principal and guarantor from that part, with the guarantor's recourse conditional on the guarantee being by the principal's order.

Stipulations and Recourse in Compromise by the Guarantor
Detailed rules on stipulations and recourse in compromise by guarantor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between compromising the debt itself and compromising the right of recourse against the guarantor, and state the effect of stipulations on the parties' liability.

Core Idea
If the guarantor stipulates his own freedom alone, the principal remains liable; compromising the guarantee itself does not affect the debt.

The previous lesson established the basic ruling. Here we explore the nuances introduced by stipulations in the compromise agreement. What happens if the guarantor stipulates that only he is freed? What if the compromise is on the guarantee itself rather than the debt? These details are essential for accurate application and advise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of compromise
Determine whether the compromise is on the debt itself (sulh ala al-dayn) or on the right of recourse against the guarantor (sulh ala al-kafala). This distinction determines the effect on the principal's liability.
The text distinguishes between these two types using examples from Hidayah and Mabsut.
2
Analyze stipulations in the compromise
If the guarantor stipulates that both he and the principal are freed, or does not stipulate anything, both are freed from the compromised portion. If he stipulates only his own freedom, the principal remains liable for the full debt.
The stipulation is about the scope of discharge, not the amount.
3
Apply the ruling for compromise on the guarantee
If the parties compromise on the right of recourse against the guarantor (i.e., the guarantor pays to be released from his suretyship), the principal remains liable for the entire debt. The guarantor's payment is considered a discharge of his obligation, not a payment of the debt.
This is based on al-Hidayah: 'if he compromised with him regarding what he became entitled to from the guarantee, the principal does not become free.'
4
Distinguish between guarantee for wealth and guarantee for person
A compromise to be freed from a guarantee for the person (kafil bi'l-nafs) is invalid unless the guarantor also pays the debt. The text cites al-Tatarkhaniyyah: a compromise for money to be freed from personal guarantee is not permissible.
Guarantee for the person is about producing the debtor; it is not directly about money.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو صالح أحدهما رب المال عن ألف على نصفه برئا) أي صالح الأصيل أو الكفيل الطالب على نفس الدين برئ الكفيل والأصيل، أما إذا صالح الأصيل فظاهر لأنه بالصلح يبرأ وبراءته توجب براءة الكفيل، وأما إذا صالح الكفيل فلأنه أضافه إلى الألف الدين وهي على الأصيل فبرئ عن خمسمائة، فبراءته توجب براءة الكفيل، ثم برئا جميعاً عن خمسمائة بأداء الكفيل ويرجع على الأصيل بخمسمائة إن كانت الكفالة بأمره بخلاف ما إذا صالح على جنس آخر لكونه مبادلة فملكه فرجع بالألف. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا شرط الكفيل براءتهما أو براءة الأصيل أو لم يشرط شيئاً، وأما إذا شرط براءة الكفيل وحده برئ دون الأصيل؛ هكذا ذكر الشارح وليس المراد أن الطالب يأخذ البدل في مقابلة إبراء الكفيل عنها، وإنما المراد أن ما أخذه من الكفيل محسوب من أصل دينه ويرجع بالباقي على الأصيل قال في الهداية : ولو كان صالحه عما استوجب من الكفالة لا يبرأ الأصيل لأن هذا إبراء الكفيل عن المطالبة ا هـ . قال في النهاية أي ما وجب بالكفالة وهو المطالبة صورته ما في المبسوط : لو صالحة على مائة درهم على أن إبراء الكفيل خاصة من الباقي رجع الكفيل على الأصيل بمائة والطالب على الأصيل بتسعمائة لأن إبراء الكفيل يكون فسخاً للكفالة ولا يكون إسقاطاً لأصل الدين ا هـ . وهكذا في فتح القدير وقال قبله وإن شرط براءة الكفيل وحده برئ الكفيل عن خمسمائة والألف بتمامها على الأصيل فيرجع الكفيل بخمسمائة إن كان بأمره والطالب بخمسمائة . ا هـ وفي التتارخانية : الكفيل إن كان بالنفس إذا صالح الطالب على خمسمائة دينار على أن أبرأه من الكفالة بالنفس لا يجوز ولا يبرأ عنها، فلو كان كفيلاً بالنفس والمال عن إنسان واحد وصالح على خمسين بالشرط برئ. ثم قال: الكفيل بالنفس إذا قضى الدين الذي على الأصيل على أنه يبرئه عن الكفالة ففعل جاز القضاء والإبراء، وأما إذا أعطاه عشرة ليبرئه عن الكفالة بالنفس فأبرأه لم يسلم له العوض باتفاق الروايات وفي براءته عنها روايتان ا هـ . وفي الخانية : لو صالح الكفيل الطالب على شيء يصح الصلح ولا يجب المال على الكفيل ا.هـ. وهو بإطلاقه شامل للكفالة بالمال والكفالة بالنفس
His statement: (And if one of them compromises with the owner of the wealth for a thousand [dirhams] for half of it, they both are free) meaning: the principal or the guarantor compromises with the claimant regarding the debt itself; the guarantor and the principal become free. As for if the principal compromises, it is apparent because he becomes free by the compromise, and his freedom necessitates the freedom of the guarantor. As for if the guarantor compromises, it is because he attributes [the compromise] to the thousand debt which is upon the principal, so he becomes free from five hundred; then his freedom necessitates the freedom of the guarantor. Then they both become free from five hundred by the payment of the guarantor, and he has recourse against the principal for five hundred if the guarantee was by his order, contrary to if he compromises for another genus because it is an exchange, so he owns it and he has recourse for the thousand. He (al-Marghinani) gave it absolutely, so it includes whether the guarantor stipulated the freedom of both, or the freedom of the principal, or he did not stipulate anything. As for if he stipulated the freedom of the guarantor alone, he becomes free without the principal. Thus mentioned the commentator. And the meaning is not that the claimant takes the substitute in exchange for the discharge of the guarantor from it; rather, the meaning is that what he took from the guarantor is counted from the original debt, and he has recourse for the remainder against the principal. He said in al-Hidayah: And if he compromised with him regarding what he became entitled to from the guarantee, the principal does not become free because this is a discharge of the guarantor from liability. End quote. He said in al-Nihayah: i.e., what became obligatory by the guarantee, which is liability. Its example is what is in al-Mabsut: If he compromised with him for one hundred dirhams on condition that he discharges the guarantor specifically from the remainder, the guarantor has recourse against the principal for one hundred, and the claimant has recourse against the principal for nine hundred, because discharging the guarantor is a rescission of the guarantee and is not a dropping of the original debt. End quote. And thus in Fath al-Qadir, and he said before it: And if he stipulated the freedom of the guarantor alone, the guarantor becomes free from five hundred, and the thousand entirely remains upon the principal, so the guarantor has recourse for five hundred if it was by his order, and the claimant has recourse for five hundred. End quote. And in al-Tatarkhaniyyah: The guarantor for the person—if he compromises with the claimant for five hundred dinars on condition that he discharges him from the guarantee for the person, it is not permissible and he does not become free from it. If he is a guarantor for both person and wealth for one person, and he compromises for fifty with the condition, he becomes free. Then he said: The guarantor for the person, if he pays the debt that is upon the principal on condition that he discharges him from the guarantee, and he does so, the payment and discharge are valid. But if he gives him ten to discharge him from the guarantee for the person, and he discharges him, the compensation is not validated by agreement of the narrations, and regarding his freedom from it there are two narrations. End quote. And in al-Khaniyyah: If the guarantor compromises with the claimant for something, the compromise is valid and the wealth is not obligatory upon the guarantor. End quote. And its absolute [wording] includes the guarantee for wealth and the guarantee for the person.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the ruling of a compromise (sulh) between the creditor (talib) and either the principal debtor (asil) or the guarantor (kafil) for a portion of the debt. The base text states: 'If one of them (the principal or the guarantor) compromises with the creditor for a thousand [dirhams] for half of it, both become free.' The commentator clarifies that this applies whether the compromise is made by the principal or the guarantor. If the principal compromises, his freedom from that portion naturally frees the guarantor as well, since the guarantor's obligation is subsidiary. If the guarantor compromises, he pays half the debt, and both are freed from that half; the guarantor then has recourse against the principal for that half only if the guarantee was made at the principal's request (bi-amrihi). If the compromise is for a different type of asset (jins akhar), it is treated as an exchange (mubadala), and the guarantor may have recourse for the full original amount. The commentator further explains that if the guarantor stipulates freedom only for himself, the principal remains liable for the remaining half. He then cites al-Hidayah and al-Nihayah to distinguish between compromising the debt itself (which frees the principal) versus compromising the right of recourse against the guarantor (which does not). Finally, he quotes al-Tatarkhaniyyah and al-Khaniyyah regarding the guarantor for the person (kafil bi'l-nafs), noting that a compromise to be freed from personal guarantee is invalid unless the guarantor also pays the debt, and that in general a compromise by the guarantor is valid without obligating him to pay if it is a pure discharge.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibra'): Discharge; a unilateral release from a debt or obligation.
كفالة بالنفس (kafala bi al-nafs): Personal guarantee; a guarantee to produce the principal debtor if required.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission; the annulment of a contract, here the guarantee contract.
استوجب من الكفالة (istawjaba min al-kafala): What one becomes entitled to by virtue of the guarantee, i.e., the right to demand payment from the guarantor.
الصلح على جنس آخر (al-sulh ala jins akhar): Compromise for a different genus of asset (e.g., paying in goods instead of cash).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the guarantor compromises with the creditor for 100 dirhams on condition that the creditor discharges the guarantor from the guarantee, what is the effect on the principal's debt of 1000 dirhams?
A) The principal is freed from 1000 dirhams.
B) The principal is freed from 100 dirhams, and the guarantor has no recourse.
C) The principal owes 900 dirhams to the creditor and 100 dirhams to the guarantor (if the guarantee was by the principal's order).
D) The principal owes 1000 dirhams to the creditor, and the guarantor owes 100 dirhams to the principal.

Key Takeaway
When the guarantor compromises only his own discharge, the principal remains liable for the full debt, but the payment reduces the principal's debt to the creditor and gives the guarantor a right of recourse.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
The Effect of Creditor's Wording on Surety's Recourse: Bari'ta Ilayya vs Bari'ta vs Abra'tuka
Commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq explaining linguistic effects in kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal effect of the three creditor phrases 'bari’ta ilayya', 'bari’ta', and 'abra’tuka' on the surety's right of recourse, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding 'bari’ta'.

Core Idea
The surety's recourse hinges on whether the creditor's wording acknowledges payment or waives the debt; ambiguity blocks recourse.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a key nuance in suretyship (kafalah): the difference between two common release phrases. Understanding this distinction is crucial for both the creditor and the surety, as it determines whether the surety can recover from the principal debtor. The Hanafi school has a subtle internal disagreement on one phrase.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three phrases
The passage discusses three phrases: 'bari’ta ilayya min al-mal' (you are released from the debt to me), 'bari’ta' (you are released), and 'abra’tuka' (I release you). Each has a different effect on the surety's recourse.
2
Analyze the clear cases
'bari’ta ilayya' implies payment, so surety has recourse. 'abra’tuka' is a pure waiver, so no recourse. This is agreed upon.
3
Examine the dispute over 'bari’ta'
Imam Muhammad holds that 'bari’ta' is like 'abra’tuka' because it is ambiguous between payment and waiver, and doubt prevents recourse. Imam Abu Yusuf holds it is like 'bari’ta ilayya' because the word 'bari’ta' inherently implies a release that culminates in payment, not waiver.
4
Apply the practical rule
If the creditor is present, his clarification is accepted, resolving the ambiguity. This shows that the matter is contextual and depends on the creditor's intended meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن قال الطالب للكفيل برئت إلي من المال رجع على المطلوب) أي الكفيل على الأصيل معناه إذا ضمن بأمره لأن البراءة التي ابتداؤها من المطلوب ليبرئه عن الكفالة لا وانتهاؤها إلى الطالب لا تكون إلا بالإيفاء فيرجع فصار كإقراره بالقبض منه أو النقد منه الدفع إليه، واستفيد منه براءة المطلوب للطالب لإقراره كالكفيل قوله : (وفي برئت أو أبرأتك لا) أي في قول الطالب للكفيل برئت - بفتح التاء ـ أو أبرأتك لا يرجع الكفيل على أما في أبرأتك فلا خلاف فيه لأنه إبراء لا ينتهي إلى غيره وذلك بالإسقاط فلم يكن إقراراً بالإيفاء. وأنت في حل بمنزلة أبرأتك ، وأما في برئت فقال محمد: هو مثله لاحتماله البراءة بالأداء إليه والإبراء فيثبت الأدنى إذ لا رجوع بالشك. وقال أبو يوسف: هو المطلوب، ما مثل الأول لأنه أقر ببراءة ابتداؤها من المطلوب وإليه الإيفاء دون الإبراء. وقيل في جميع ذكرنا إذا كان الطالب حاضراً يرجع في البيان إليه لأنه هو المجمل حتى في «برئت إلي» لاحتمال لأني أبرأتك مجازاً وإن كان بعيداً في الاستعمال ؛ كذا في النهاية. وفي فتح القدير : والحوالة كالكفالة في هذا. قيد بقوله برئت لأنه لو كتب في الصك برئ الكفيل من الدراهم التي كفل بها كان إقراراً بالقبض عندهم جميعاً كقوله برئت إلي بقضية العرف فإن العرف بين الناس أن الصك يكتب على الطالب بالبراءة إذا حصلت بالإيفاء، وإن حصلت بالإبراء لا يكتب عليه الصك فجعلت الكتابة إقراراً بالقبض عرفاً ولا عرف عند الإبراء؛ كذا في فتح القدير. واختلف المتأخرون فيما إذا قال المدعى عليه أبرأني المدعي من الدعوى التي يدعي علي؛ منهم من قال هو إقرار بالمال كما لو قال أبرأني من المال الذي ادعاه، ومنهم من قال لا يكون إقراراً لأن الدعوى تكون بحق وباطل ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وفي البزازية من الدعوى: دعوى البراءة عن الدعوى لا يكون إقراراً بالدعوى عند المتقدمين وخالفهم المتأخرون، ودعوى البراءة عن المال إقرار وقول المتقدمين أصح اهـ.
His statement: (And if the creditor says to the surety, 'You are released from the debt to me,' [the surety] has recourse against the debtor) i.e., the surety against the principal debtor, meaning when he guaranteed with his command, because the release that begins from the debtor to release him from the suretyship? no, and ends with the creditor only occurs through payment, so he has recourse, so it becomes like his acknowledgment of receipt from him or payment from him to him. And from it is derived the release of the debtor from the creditor due to his acknowledgment like the surety. His statement: (And in 'You are released' or 'I release you' no) i.e., when the creditor says to the surety 'You are released' with the tā' open or 'I release you,' the surety does not have recourse against the debtor. As for 'I release you,' there is no disagreement because it is a release that does not end with another, and that is by waiver, so it is not an acknowledgment of payment. And 'You are free' is like 'I release you.' As for 'You are released,' Muhammad said: it is like it [i.e., like 'I release you'] because it could mean release by payment to him or waiver, so the lesser is affirmed since there is no recourse with doubt. Abu Yusuf said: it is like the first [i.e., 'You are released from the debt to me'] because he acknowledged a release whose beginning is from the debtor and its end is payment, not waiver. And it was said regarding all that we mentioned: if the creditor is present, the clarification refers to him because the expression is ambiguous, even in 'You are released from the debt to me' due to the possibility that 'I release you' metaphorically, even if it is remote in usage. Thus in al-Nihayah. And in Fath al-Qadir: The assignment of debt is like suretyship in this. He restricted it by saying 'You are released' because if it were written in the document 'the surety is released from the dirhams for which he stood surety,' it would be an acknowledgment of receipt according to all, like his saying 'You are released from the debt to me' due to custom. For the custom among people is that the document is written for the creditor upon release when it occurs by payment, but if it occurs by waiver, the document is not written upon him. So the writing is made an acknowledgment of receipt by custom, and there is no custom regarding waiver. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And the later scholars differed regarding when the defendant says 'the plaintiff released me from the claim that he claims against me.' Some said it is an acknowledgment of the debt, as if he said 'he released me from the money he claimed,' and some said it is not an acknowledgment because the claim could be right or wrong. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Bazzaziyya regarding claims: claiming release from a claim is not an acknowledgment of the claim according to the early scholars, and the later scholars oppose them, and claiming release from money is an acknowledgment, and the statement of the early scholars is more correct. End.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq, explaining the rulings in the chapter of suretyship (kafalah) regarding the effect of different wordings used by the creditor (talib) when releasing the surety (kafil). The author opens by explaining the matn's statement: 'If the creditor says to the surety, “You are released from the debt to me” (bari’ta ilayya min al-mal), the surety has recourse against the principal debtor (matlub).' The commentary clarifies that this applies only when the surety guaranteed with the debtor's command. The reasoning is that the phrase 'bari’ta ilayya' implies a release that starts from the debtor and ends with the creditor, which can only happen through actual payment by the surety to the creditor. Therefore, it is treated as an acknowledgment by the creditor that the surety paid, giving the surety the right to claim reimbursement from the debtor. The surety's acknowledgment also frees the debtor from the creditor's claim.

Next, the author addresses the matn's phrase: 'And in [the case where the creditor says] “You are released” (bari’ta) or “I release you” (abra’tuka), no [recourse].' The commentary explains that if the creditor uses these wordings without adding 'ilayya min al-mal', the surety does not have recourse against the debtor. For 'abra’tuka', there is no disagreement: it is a pure waiver (ibra’) that does not imply payment, so it is not an acknowledgment. For 'bari’ta' (with fatha on the ta), there is a dispute within the Hanafi school: Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani holds it is like 'abra’tuka' because it is ambiguous between payment and waiver, so the lesser (no recourse) is presumed since recourse cannot be based on doubt. Imam Abu Yusuf holds it is like 'bari’ta ilayya' because the word bari’ta alone still suggests a release that originates from the debtor and culminates in payment, not waiver. The text then mentions that some scholars say that if the creditor is present, his clarification is accepted because the phrase is ambiguous, even 'bari’ta ilayya' could metaphorically mean 'abra’tuka', though such usage is rare.

The discussion continues with a comparison to hawalah (assignment of debt): according to Fath al-Qadir, the same rules apply. It also notes that if the phrase is written in a document (sikk) as 'the surety is released from the dirhams', it is considered an acknowledgment of receipt by custom, because people only write such documents when release occurs by payment. Finally, the passage touches on a related issue: when a defendant says 'the plaintiff released me from the claim,' later scholars differed on whether it counts as an acknowledgment of the debt. The early scholars said it is not an acknowledgment, and their view is more correct according to al-Bazzaziyya. This entire section demonstrates how the Hanafi school uses linguistic analysis, custom ('urf), and scholarly debate to derive nuanced fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
البراءة (al-barā'a): Release from liability, whether by payment or by waiver.
الإبراء (al-ibrā'): The act of waiving a debt or claim; a pure release without payment.
الرجوع (al-rujū'): The surety's right to claim reimbursement from the principal debtor after paying the creditor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the effect of the phrase 'bari’ta ilayya min al-mal' when said by the creditor to the surety?
A) The surety has no recourse.
B) The surety has recourse because it acknowledges payment.
C) The surety is released from the guarantee.
D) The debtor is released from the debt.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'bari’ta ilayya' grants recourse; 'abra’tuka' denies it; 'bari’ta' is disputed between Abu Yusuf (recourse) and Muhammad (no recourse), with the creditor's clarification being decisive.
